### [1] [Exposing the Unsaid: Visualizing Hidden LLM Bias through Stochastic Path Aggregation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19344)
*Matteo Pelossi,Rita Sevastjanova,Thilo Spinner,Mennatallah El-Assady*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents TreeTracer, a visual analytics tool that reveals hidden biases in large language models by aggregating and comparing many stochastic generations as syntax-aligned trees, enabling more reliable bias detection than single-output or static metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing bias audits for LLMs usually inspect one or a few generations or use static metrics, which hide the underlying probability distribution and miss biases that appear only in low-probability branches. There is a need for tools that expose these hidden representational and syntactic biases in a way that is interpretable and comparable across semantic contexts.

Method: The authors develop TreeTracer, which takes prompts and systematically perturbs ontology-defined terms (e.g., demographic or semantic categories). For each perturbed prompt, it samples hundreds of generations from the LLM and aggregates them into a syntax-aligned hierarchical tree. It then uses an auxiliary language model for classification-aware node merging so that semantically similar branches are consolidated. The resulting trees for different contexts are visualized as custom Sankey-like diagrams that support side-by-side comparison. Additionally, the system performs contrastive inference to compute counterfactual token probabilities across contexts and directly overlays these differences on the visualization to avoid misinterpreting apparent biases.

Result: Case studies comparing GPT-2 XL (unaligned) with constitutionally aligned Apertus models show that TreeTracer surfaces subtle representational harms such as suppression of certain pronouns in counterfactual settings and patterns of conversational marginalization. A preliminary user study indicates that analysts using the aggregated, comparative interface experience reduced cognitive load and are more effective at identifying systemic biases than with conventional methods.

Conclusion: TreeTracer offers a more faithful and interpretable way to audit LLM biases by aggregating stochastic generations into ontology-driven, syntax-aligned visual structures and augmenting them with contrastive probability information. This approach can uncover hidden representational harms and supports human analysts in systematic, lower-effort bias detection, indicating that visual, distribution-aware tools are a promising direction for LLM auditing.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) exhibit representational and syntactic biases that are difficult to evaluate due to the stochastic nature of text generation. Standard auditing methods rely on a single output inspection or static automated metrics. These approaches obscure the underlying probability distributions and fail to capture biases hidden in lower-probability generation branches. This paper introduces TreeTracer, a visual analytics tool designed to evaluate LLM bias through aggregated comparison. Using a systematic perturbation analysis pipeline, the tool replaces ontology-defined terms in each input prompt, aggregates hundreds of stochastic generations into a syntax-aligned hierarchical structure, and then performs classification-aware node merging with an auxiliary language model. The resulting structure is visualized through a custom Sankey diagram. By juxtaposing two ontology-driven trees, the workspace enables direct comparison between semantic contexts and supports systematic bias detection. Because any visualization reflects only a subset of the model's learned behavior, the system further applies contrastive inference to compute and directly display counterfactual token probabilities across contexts, reducing the risk of misinterpreting the presence of bias. We validate the workspace through case studies comparing an unaligned baseline model GPT-2 XL against the constitutionally aligned Apertus models. The visual aggregation successfully exposes hidden representational harms, such as counterfactual pronoun suppression and conversational marginalization of individuals. A preliminary user study confirms that the aggregated comparative interface reduces cognitive load and effectively supports analysts in detecting systemic biases.

</details>


### [2] [Ensembles of Large Language Models for Identifying EQ-5D Studies in PubMed Based on Their Abstracts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19345)
*Zhyar Rzgar K. Rostam,Márta Péntek,János Tibor Czere,Zsombor Zrubka,László Gulácsi,Gábor Kertész*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes and evaluates an ensemble of large language models (Gemini and Gemma) to automatically detect abstracts that report EQ-5D health-related quality-of-life outcomes in PubMed, improving screening efficiency in systematic reviews.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Systematic literature reviews require screening large numbers of publications, which is increasingly time-consuming, inconsistent, and inefficient when done manually. Identifying studies that report specific patient-reported outcome measures, such as EQ-5D, is particularly challenging because it demands careful clinical interpretation of abstracts. There is a need for scalable, reliable automation methods that can accurately detect such studies using only published abstracts, to support biomedical researchers and health economists.

Method: The authors design a multi-phase framework using large language models (LLMs) for binary classification of PubMed abstracts according to whether they report EQ-5D data. They evaluate nine LLM variants (Gemini and Gemma families) on a dataset of abstracts labeled by two domain experts. The framework uses few-shot prompting to guide each model, then aggregates model outputs through a weighted ensemble, and finally applies a soft stacking meta-classifier that takes the models’ predicted probabilities as features to make the final decision. They perform feature analysis to understand which model outputs contribute most to final predictions and to assess interpretability.

Result: The best performance is achieved by a weighted ensemble of gemini-2.5-pro, gemma-3-12b, and gemma-3-27b, which yields a weighted F1-score of 0.74 and an accuracy of 0.74 on the labeled dataset. This ensemble outperforms each individual model used alone. The ensemble improves the trade-off between precision and recall, while the soft stacking meta-classifier enhances reliability and interpretability of predictions. Feature importance analysis indicates that the models’ probability scores are key drivers of the final classification decisions.

Conclusion: An ensemble-based approach using multiple LLMs is an effective and scalable solution for automating the detection of EQ-5D reporting in PubMed abstracts. By combining top-performing Gemini and Gemma models through weighted ensembling and soft stacking, the framework achieves better accuracy and balance between precision and recall than single models. The study suggests that similar ensemble LLM setups could be broadly beneficial for automating screening tasks in biomedical research and systematic literature reviews, reducing manual workload while maintaining reliable performance.

Abstract: The rapid increase in scientific publications leads to the fact that manual study screening in systematic literature reviews (SLRs) is increasingly resource consuming, inefficient, and inconsistent. Classifying studies that clearly report health-related quality-of-life results, such as EQ-5D data, requires a high level of clinical interpretation and poses challenges for human reviewers. This study investigates the use of Google's Gemini and Gemma large language models (LLMs) in automating EQ-5D detection in the PubMed biomedical database based only on published abstracts. A multi-phase framework is proposed that integrates few-shot prompting, weight ensembling aggregation, and a soft stacking meta-classifier. Nine LLMs are evaluated on a dataset of PubMed studies manually labeled by two experts regarding EQ-5D reporting. The weighted ensemble of gemini-2.5-pro, gemma-3-12b, and gemma-3-27b obtained a 0.74 weighted F1-score and 0.74 accuracy, exceeding individually attained results. The ensembling of top-performing models improved the balance between precision and recall compared to individual models, while the soft stacking approach provided greater reliability and interpretability. Feature analysis shows that the probability results from the models are important in guiding the final predictions. The findings suggest that an ensemble-based LLM setup is a reliable and scalable approach for automating screening in biomedical research.

</details>


### [3] [Disentangling Linguistic Relatedness from Task Alignment in Cross-Lingual Transfer](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19346)
*Ahmed Haj Ahmed,Ruochen Zhang,Alvin Grissom*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines whether fine-tuning large language models on Arabic yields better zero-shot reading comprehension transfer to other Semitic languages compared to non-Semitic languages, and finds no Semitic-specific advantage.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To test the common hypothesis that linguistic or typological similarity (e.g., within the Semitic family) leads to better cross-lingual transfer in large language models, and to understand whether improvements from fine-tuning stem from language-specific knowledge transfer or from better alignment with task format.

Method: Fine-tune seven large language models of varying sizes (4B–671B parameters), including both dense and Mixture-of-Experts architectures, on Arabic reading comprehension tasks. Then evaluate the models zero-shot on reading comprehension benchmarks in other Semitic languages and in non-Semitic control languages. Additionally, perform a chain-of-thought (CoT) ablation study, comparing performance with and without inference-time reasoning prompts to see how CoT interacts with or substitutes for fine-tuning.

Result: Fine-tuning on Arabic substantially improves zero-shot reading comprehension for models that initially have weak baselines, and these gains are similar across Semitic and non-Semitic languages. Models that start with strong baselines show only small improvements, again with no noticeable difference between Semitic and non-Semitic languages. The chain-of-thought ablation reveals that the models that gain most from fine-tuning also gain similarly from CoT prompting, indicating that both mechanisms mainly help with task-format alignment rather than transferring language-family-specific knowledge.

Conclusion: There is no empirical support in these experiments for Semitic-specific cross-lingual transfer in large language models. Improvements from Arabic fine-tuning and from chain-of-thought prompting appear to arise primarily from better alignment with the reading comprehension task format, rather than from enhanced transfer of linguistic or semantic knowledge to related Semitic languages.

Abstract: We study cross-lingual transfer by fine-tuning seven large language models (4B--671B parameters) on Arabic and evaluating zero-shot reading comprehension on Semitic languages and non-Semitic controls. Across dense and Mixture-of-Experts architectures, we find no evidence of Semitic-specific transfer: models with weak baselines improve dramatically across all languages, while strong-baseline models show only marginal gains regardless of language family. A chain-of-thought ablation reinforces this finding -- the same models that benefit most from fine-tuning benefit equally from inference-time reasoning, suggesting both mechanisms address task-format alignment rather than cross-lingual knowledge transfer.

</details>


### [4] [How LLMs Fail and Generalize in RTL Coding for Hardware Design?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19347)
*Guan-Ting Liu,Chao-Han Huck Yang,Chenhui Deng,Zhongzhi Yu,Brucek Khailany,Yu-Chiang Frank Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why large language models struggle to generate correct hardware (Verilog/RTL) code and introduces an error taxonomy that reveals a hard performance ceiling driven by deep functional reasoning failures rather than syntax or alignment issues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs can write code, translating sequential programming priors into the inherently parallel and temporally constrained world of hardware description languages (HDLs) like Verilog remains difficult. Existing benchmarks show high but plateauing success, yet we lack a principled understanding of what kinds of errors dominate and why improvements in alignment and decoding strategies are not breaking through these ceilings. The authors aim to systematically characterize LLM failure modes in hardware generation and to test whether further scaling of test-time computation or alignment can overcome current limitations.

Method: The authors construct a cognitive-theory-inspired error taxonomy for LLM-generated HDL code, with four classes: syntactic errors, semantic errors (e.g., type or interface mismatches), solvable functional errors (logic is flawed but fixable with additional sampling or search), and unsolvable functional errors (reflecting deeper knowledge or reasoning gaps). They apply this taxonomy to model outputs on the VerilogEval benchmark, analyzing performance across models and under different optimization and decoding conditions (e.g., alignment techniques, repeated sampling). They then examine which error types dominate at different accuracy levels and how optimization shifts the error distribution.

Result: On the VerilogEval benchmark, leading LLMs plateau at an initial pass rate of about 90.8%, indicating a hard empirical performance ceiling. Most remaining errors at this frontier are unsolvable functional errors, which persist even when increasing test-time computation or employing advanced sampling/optimization methods. While optimization and alignment substantially reduce syntax errors, they also correlate with an increase in deeper functional failures. Repeated sampling can often repair solvable functional errors but does not meaningfully reduce the rate of unsolvable functional ones, revealing that RTL coding capacity is constrained by what the model learned during pretraining.

Conclusion: The work concludes that current improvements in alignment and decoding mainly teach LLMs to produce syntactically valid, compiling hardware code rather than genuinely correct RTL designs. The dominant barrier to further progress on benchmarks like VerilogEval is unsolvable functional errors stemming from limited reasoning and domain knowledge, not superficial syntax issues. As a result, test-time compute and alignment interventions cannot surpass the observed performance ceiling; instead, future research must focus on enhancing model reasoning, representation of hardware concepts, and pretraining strategies if we wish to meaningfully advance automatic hardware generation with LLMs.

Abstract: Translating sequential programming priors into the parallel temporal logic of hardware design remains a crucial bottleneck for large language models(LLM). To investigate this, we introduce a new error taxonomy grounded in problem solvability, inspired by cognitive theory. Our taxonomy categorizes failures into syntactic, semantic, solvable functional, and unsolvable functional types. Evaluations reveal a strict empirical ceiling on the VerilogEval benchmark, as frontier models plateau at a 90.8% initial pass rate. These plateaus are defined by unsolvable functional errors, exposing persistent knowledge gaps immune to test time compute scaling. Furthermore, we expose a striking surface convergence gap: optimization readily eliminates syntax errors but concurrently exacerbates deeper functional failures. Our findings demonstrate that alignment techniques merely teach models to compile. While repeated sampling strategies can patch solvable errors, register-transfer level(RTL) coding capacity remains strictly bounded by pretraining knowledge. Addressing challenges in the current LLM based hardware generation pipeline requires more studies in model reasoning rather than alignment interventions.

</details>


### [5] [DeepSeek-V4: Towards Highly Efficient Million-Token Context Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19348)
*DeepSeek-AI,Anyi Xu,Bangcai Lin,Bing Xue,Bingxuan Wang,Bingzheng Xu,Bochao Wu,Bowei Zhang,Chaofan Lin,Chen Dong,Chenchen Ling,Chengda Lu,Chenggang Zhao,Chengqi Deng,Chengyu Hou,Chenhao Xu,Chenze Shao,Zhixian Huang,Zhixuan Chen,Zhiyu Wu,Zhizhou Ren,Zhongyu Wu,Zhuoshu Li,Zhuping Zhang,Zian Xu,Zihao Wang,Zihua Qu,Zihui Gu,Zijia Zhu,Zilin Li,Zipeng Zhang,Ziwei Xie,Ziyi Gao,Ziyi Wan,Zizheng Pan,Zongqing Yao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Preview of DeepSeek-V4 MoE LLMs with 1M-token context, new attention and optimization techniques, and strong long-context efficiency and reasoning performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing large language models struggle with efficient long-context processing, stable and fast training at very large scales, and maximizing reasoning performance under compute constraints. The paper aims to push the state-of-the-art in open models by designing architectures and optimization methods that support million-token contexts and higher reasoning quality while reducing inference cost.

Method: Introduce two Mixture-of-Experts models (DeepSeek-V4-Pro and DeepSeek-V4-Flash) with trillions of parameters but limited activated parameters per token. Architecturally, they use a hybrid attention scheme that mixes Compressed Sparse Attention and Heavily Compressed Attention for long contexts, and introduce Manifold-Constrained Hyper-Connections to improve over standard residual connections. Optimization-wise, they employ the Muon optimizer for faster convergence and training stability. Models are pre-trained on >32T diverse, high-quality tokens and then undergo a comprehensive post-training pipeline for capability enhancement and alignment. A high-effort reasoning mode (DeepSeek-V4-Pro-Max) is defined for test-time scaling of reasoning.

Result: DeepSeek-V4-Pro and DeepSeek-V4-Flash achieve strong performance, with DeepSeek-V4-Pro-Max redefining the state-of-the-art among open models on core reasoning tasks (per the authors). For long-context usage up to one million tokens, DeepSeek-V4-Pro uses only 27% of the single-token inference FLOPs and 10% of the KV cache compared with DeepSeek-V3.2, demonstrating large efficiency gains while maintaining or improving performance.

Conclusion: The DeepSeek-V4 series shows that combining advanced MoE architectures, hybrid long-context attention, improved residual-style connections, and a new optimizer enables million-token-context LLMs that are both powerful and efficient. These techniques make long-horizon applications and test-time scaling more practical, and the released checkpoints provide a new open-state-of-the-art baseline for research and deployment.

Abstract: We present a preview version of DeepSeek-V4 series, including two strong Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) language models -- DeepSeek-V4-Pro with 1.6T parameters (49B activated) and DeepSeek-V4-Flash with 284B parameters (13B activated) -- both supporting a context length of one million tokens. DeepSeek-V4 series incorporate several key upgrades in architecture and optimization: (1) a hybrid attention architecture that combines Compressed Sparse Attention (CSA) and Heavily Compressed Attention (HCA) to improve long-context efficiency; (2) Manifold-Constrained Hyper-Connections (mHC) that enhance conventional residual connections; (3) and the Muon optimizer for faster convergence and greater training stability. We pre-train both models on more than 32T diverse and high-quality tokens, followed by a comprehensive post-training pipeline that unlocks and further enhances their capabilities. DeepSeek-V4-Pro-Max, the maximum reasoning effort mode of DeepSeek-V4-Pro, redefines the state-of-the-art for open models, outperforming its predecessors in core tasks. Meanwhile, DeepSeek-V4 series are highly efficient in long-context scenarios. In the one-million-token context setting, DeepSeek-V4-Pro requires only 27% of single-token inference FLOPs and 10% of KV cache compared with DeepSeek-V3.2. This enables us to routinely support one-million-token contexts, thereby making long-horizon tasks and further test-time scaling more feasible. The model checkpoints are available at https://huggingface.co/collections/deepseek-ai/deepseek-v4.

</details>


### [6] [Where to Place the Query? Unveiling and Mitigating Positional Bias in In-Context Learning for Diffusion LLMs via Decoding Dynamics](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19349)
*Zhengheng Li,Panrui Li,Xuyang Liu,Puzhi Xia*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how in-context learning works in diffusion large language models (dLLMs), showing that where you place the query in the context is crucial, and proposes an automatic, training-free method (Auto-ICL) to adaptively choose the best query position using a new confidence metric.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing studies of in-context learning focus on autoregressive LLMs with unidirectional attention, while diffusion LLMs use bidirectional attention and allow more flexible query placement. However, current practice naively copies AR-style templates and ignores whether position matters, and standard confidence measures are not suitable for dLLMs. The authors aim to understand and exploit spatial aspects of ICL in dLLMs.

Method: They empirically analyze how query position affects performance in dLLMs by decoupling positional effects from example semantic quality. They inspect internal attention patterns to identify a spatial "Recency Effect" and task-dependent decoding trajectories. Finding that traditional single-step confidence is unreliable, they define Average Confidence (\overline{C}) over the iterative diffusion decoding steps and use it to guide an adaptive, training-free query placement strategy called Auto-ICL.

Result: They show that query position is a first-order factor in dLLMs, with positional variance influencing performance as strongly as example quality. They find a spatial recency bias and shifting decoding paths across tasks. Average Confidence correlates better with output quality than single-step confidence, enabling the Auto-ICL routing strategy to select near-optimal query locations. Auto-ICL approaches oracle performance and is robust across diverse reasoning and perception benchmarks without additional training.

Conclusion: Spatial layout in the context is fundamentally important for in-context learning in diffusion LLMs, contrary to the implicit assumption imported from autoregressive models. By characterizing positional sensitivity and introducing Average Confidence as a suitable signal for dLLMs, the paper establishes spatial ICL baselines and offers Auto-ICL as a practical, training-free way to adapt query placement, substantially stabilizing and improving dLLM performance across tasks.

Abstract: While In-Context Learning (ICL) is extensively studied in Autoregressive (AR) LLMs, its mechanism within Diffusion Large Language Models (dLLMs) remains largely unexplored. Unlike AR models restricted by unidirectional causal masking, dLLMs intrinsically utilize bidirectional attention, offering extensive spatial flexibility for query placement. Unfortunately, current practices conventionally inherit AR-style trailing-query templates, often overlooking the structural paradigm shift. This paper presents a comprehensive analysis unveiling that query position is actually a first-order variable in dLLMs. Through empirical decoupling, we demonstrate that positional variance impacts generation quality on par with example semantic quality. Internally, this positional sensitivity stems from a spatial ``Recency Effect'' in attention flow and task-dependent shifts in decoding trajectories. To mitigate this instability without ground-truth labels, we reveal that traditional single-step confidence ($C_{decoded}$) fails in dLLMs. Instead, we propose Average Confidence ($\overline{C}$), a novel metric tracking the iterative decoding process. By establishing the foundational spatial ICL baselines, we introduce Auto-ICL, a training-free adaptive routing strategy that dynamically optimizes query placement, robustly approaching oracle performance across heterogeneous reasoning and perception tasks.

</details>


### [7] [Pruning via Causal Attribution Preserves Reasoning Performance in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19350)
*Amogh Sheth,Biruk Assefa,Yi Wen Huang,Andrew Lin,Yuhao Ge*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose Causal Attribution Pruning (CAP), a training-free pruning method that uses causal impact of attention heads on reasoning performance to guide fine-grained weight pruning, outperforming magnitude/activation-based methods like Wanda at moderate sparsity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are good at multi-step reasoning but are expensive to run, especially for inference. Existing pruning methods often rely on correlational or magnitude-based metrics that do not directly measure functional importance of components, which can hurt reasoning performance, especially at the attention-head level. The authors want a pruning scheme that reduces inference cost while preserving reasoning ability as much as possible.

Method: They introduce Causal Attribution Pruning (CAP). For each attention head, they perform an intervention by masking that head during forward passes on a small calibration set of reasoning tasks (e.g., GSM8K, StrategyQA, ARC-Challenge). They measure the expected performance degradation caused by masking, which yields a causal score for each head. These head-level scores are then mapped to weight-level importance for the corresponding projection matrices, enabling fine-grained weight pruning guided by these causal attributions, without any retraining. They compare this causally-informed pruning against magnitude-based/activation-based baselines like Wanda at different sparsity levels (10%, 20%, 50%) on Llama-3-8B-Instruct and Mistral-7B-Instruct.

Result: On benchmarks GSM8K, StrategyQA, and ARC-Challenge, and models Llama-3-8B-Instruct and Mistral-7B-Instruct, CAP achieves higher accuracy than Wanda at moderate sparsity levels (10–20%). On ARC-Challenge with Llama-3-8B-Instruct at 20% sparsity, CAP yields up to 61% relative accuracy improvement over Wanda. However, at high sparsity (50%), performance is limited, particularly due to coarse attribution for MLP components.

Conclusion: Causal attribution at the attention-head level is a more effective criterion for pruning than traditional correlational or magnitude-based methods when the goal is to preserve reasoning ability under moderate sparsity. CAP, being training-free and based on interventional measurements, can substantially reduce inference cost while maintaining or improving downstream reasoning performance, although its benefits diminish at very high sparsity where MLP pruning becomes a bottleneck.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) excel at multi-step reasoning but incur substantial inference cost. We introduce Causal Attribution Pruning (CAP), a training-free method that identifies critical attention heads by measuring their causal impact on reasoning tasks and uses these head-level scores to guide fine-grained weight pruning. For each attention head, CAP estimates the expected performance degradation when the head is masked during forward passes on a small calibration set of reasoning problems. These causal scores are then converted into weight-level importance values for the corresponding projection matrices. Unlike magnitude-only or activation-based criteria, CAP's interventional measurement directly captures each head's functional contribution, yielding relative accuracy gains of up to 61% over Wanda on ARC-Challenge at 20% sparsity. We evaluate CAP on GSM8K, StrategyQA, and ARC-Challenge using Llama-3-8B-Instruct and Mistral-7B-Instruct at 10%, 20%, and 50% sparsity. At moderate sparsity (10-20%), CAP improves over Wanda in most model-benchmark configurations. with especially large gains on ARC-Challenge for Llama-3. Our results suggest that attention-head-level causal attribution can better preserve reasoning performance on downstream benchmarks than correlational pruning criteria at equivalent sparsity, while remaining limited by coarse MLP attribution at 50% sparsity.

</details>


### [8] [Detecting Hallucinations for Large Language Model-based Knowledge Graph Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19351)
*Xinyan Zhu,Yaoqi Liu,Yue Gao,Huadong Ma,Cheng Yang,Chuan Shi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes LUCID, a hallucination detection method for LLM-based knowledge graph reasoning that combines LLM attention, semantic features, and KG structure via a GNN, achieving state-of-the-art results on multiple datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models used for knowledge graph reasoning often hallucinate, producing incorrect outputs even when provided with relevant KG facts. Existing hallucination detection approaches either look only at internal LLM signals or only at consistency with retrieved text, and they ignore the rich structural information in knowledge graphs, leading to weaker detection performance. There is a need for a method that explicitly exploits KG structure, semantics, and LLM signals together to better detect hallucinations in KG reasoning scenarios.

Method: The authors introduce LUCID, a hallucination detection framework tailored to LLM-based KG reasoning. LUCID first obtains attention scores from the LLM and semantic similarity features between entities/relations. It then defines node and edge features based on these attention- and semantics-derived signals. These features are combined with the inherent structural information of the knowledge graph and fed into a graph neural network, which learns to predict whether a given LLM-produced reasoning output is hallucinated. The system is trained and evaluated on newly constructed, manually annotated benchmarks for hallucination in KG reasoning.

Result: On nine benchmark datasets, LUCID outperforms 15 existing hallucination detection baselines, achieving state-of-the-art performance. This indicates that incorporating KG structural information together with LLM attention and semantic features substantially improves the ability to spot hallucinated outputs in KG reasoning tasks.

Conclusion: Leveraging KG structure is crucial for effective hallucination detection in LLM-based knowledge graph reasoning. By jointly modeling LLM attention, semantic similarities, and graph topology through a GNN, LUCID significantly improves hallucination detection over prior methods that ignore graph structure. The work also contributes new annotated benchmarks, providing a basis for future research on reliable LLM-KG integration.

Abstract: Knowledge graph (KG) reasoning infers new knowledge from existing facts and is widely applied in question answering, recommendation, and decision support. With the rapid development of large language models (LLMs), LLM-based KG reasoning frameworks have become increasingly popular by leveraging retrieved KG information. However, hallucinations in LLMs remain a critical issue. Even when relevant KG knowledge is incorporated, models may still generate incorrect outputs, leading to misinformation and unreliable decisions. Existing hallucination detection methods either focus on LLM internal states or verify consistency with retrieved contexts, but both overlook the structural information in KGs, resulting in suboptimal performance. To address this gap, we propose LUCID, the first halLUcination deteCtIon method for LLM-based knowleDge graph reasoning frameworks. LUCID jointly leverages LLM attention scores, KG semantics, and structural information. Specifically, it extracts node and edge features from attention scores and semantic similarities, and integrates them with KG structure using a graph neural network. We also construct manually annotated benchmark datasets for evaluation. Experiments on nine datasets show that LUCID achieves state of the art performance compared to 15 baselines.

</details>

### [10] [Quantifying Aleatoric Uncertainty of In-Context Learning for Robust Measure of LLM Prediction Confidence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19353)
*Jinseok Chung,Minkyoung Song,Hyunji Jung,Namhoon Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new way to decompose and measure uncertainty for large language models doing in-context learning, using internal representations and a new evaluation protocol.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In-context learning by LLMs is powerful but unreliable: performance is very sensitive to prompts, and it's hard to tell whether errors come from noisy/ambiguous data (aleatoric uncertainty) or from model limitations or lack of knowledge (epistemic uncertainty). Existing uncertainty estimation methods are built for standard generation and don't capture the special behavior of ICL, and there is no proper benchmark to evaluate uncertainty decomposition in this setting.

Method: The authors introduce self-function vectors, a representation derived from internal activations of the model under a Bayesian and mechanistic-interpretability perspective, intended to represent the latent function or concept learned from in-context examples. Using these vectors, they build a Bayesian framework that directly estimates aleatoric uncertainty without relying on perturbing inputs or decoding strategies. They also design a controlled evaluation protocol where datasets are synthetically and systematically manipulated so that aleatoric and epistemic uncertainties can be varied and measured separately, and then extend this framework to real-world datasets.

Result: Under their new evaluation protocol, both on synthetic tasks and real-world data, their self-function–based Bayesian method produces uncertainty estimates for ICL predictions that align better with ground-truth aleatoric and epistemic components than existing baselines. It more reliably detects when predictions are uncertain due to data noise or ambiguity. They also demonstrate that the method is useful in practice for tasks like detecting hallucinations in LLM outputs.

Conclusion: The paper shows that by leveraging internal model representations as self-function vectors and evaluating them under a carefully controlled protocol, one can more accurately decompose and measure aleatoric versus epistemic uncertainty in in-context learning. This improves reliability and interpretability of LLM predictions and opens a path to tighter integration between uncertainty quantification and mechanistic understanding of model behavior, with direct applications to trustworthy AI such as hallucination detection.

Abstract: In-Context Learning (ICL) allows LLMs to adapt to new tasks from a few demonstrations, but its reliability remains a concern: predictions are highly sensitive to both prompt design and the model's ability to understand the context, obscuring whether failures arise from data properties or model limitations. Uncertainty decomposition-separating aleatoric from epistemic sources-is particularly crucial in this setting, yet existing methods, designed for standard generation tasks, fail to capture the unique dynamics of ICL. To address this, we introduce a concept of self-function vectors, built upon Bayesian views and the mechanistic interpretability of ICL. These vectors leverage internal model representations to model the latent concept learned during in-context prompting, thereby enabling a direct estimation of aleatoric uncertainty within a Bayesian framework and circumventing the reliance on brittle input or decoding manipulations. Given the lack of established benchmarks and suitable evaluation protocols, we also propose the first and rigorous evaluation protocol, in which data is manipulated in controlled ways so as to quantify aleatoric uncertainty precisely and separately from epistemic uncertainty. With this new evaluation framework, initially grounded in synthetic tasks for conceptual development and subsequently extended to real-world datasets, we show that our proposed methodology can measure uncertainty of LLM predictions made under ICL more reliably than existing alternative methods. Moreover, we show it can be used as a practical tool for trustworthy-related applications, such as hallucination detection. Our findings pave a new direction for connecting the quantitative view of uncertainty with the mechanistic understanding of model behavior.

### [11] [Granularity-Regulated Adaptive Computational Efficiency for Optimal Verification in Test-Time Scaling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19354)
*Ardit Krasniqi,Luan Vejsiu,Elira Dervishi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces GRACE, a theoretical framework for determining the compute-optimal verification granularity in test-time scaling for LLM reasoning, showing when fine-grained vs coarse-grained verification is preferable and proposing an adaptive strategy that is empirically validated.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Test-time scaling for LLMs relies heavily on verifiers to select or score candidate reasoning paths, but existing approaches use either coarse-grained or fine-grained verification in a fixed way. It is unclear, given a limited compute budget, how fine the verification should be to maximize performance, and no unified theory connects different search methods or prescribes an adaptive strategy.

Method: The authors develop a unified theoretical framework, GRACE, that models verification granularity as a function of problem difficulty, verifier accuracy, and compute budget. Within this framework, they analyze and compare coarse-grained outcome reward models and fine-grained process reward models, establishing conditions under which each is optimal. They show that Best-of-N, beam search, and step-level MCTS are special cases within a single Pareto-optimality analysis, and from this derive an adaptive granularity verification algorithm. They then empirically test this algorithm and baselines on math reasoning benchmarks.

Result: The theory predicts a phase transition in the optimal verification strategy: fine-grained verification is superior when compute is high or problems are difficult, while coarse-grained verification is better with low compute or easy problems. The adaptive GRACE-based strategy traces the compute-performance Pareto frontier in this framework. Empirical experiments on MATH-500, GSM8K, and AIME show that the adaptive granularity method consistently outperforms fixed-granularity baselines, achieving up to 3.1% absolute accuracy improvement at equal compute cost.

Conclusion: Verification granularity in test-time scaling should not be fixed; it should adapt to problem difficulty, verifier quality, and available compute. The GRACE framework provides both a theoretical characterization of when fine-vs coarse-grained verification is optimal and a practical adaptive strategy that attains near-Pareto-optimal compute-performance tradeoffs, validated on standard math reasoning benchmarks.

Abstract: Test-time scaling (TTS) has emerged as a powerful paradigm for improving the reasoning performance of large language models (LLMs) by investing additional compute at inference time. A central component of TTS is the \emph{verifier}, which selects or scores candidate solutions to guide the search process. While prior work has explored the benefit of verification, a fundamental question remains underexplored: \emph{what is the optimal granularity of verification under a given compute budget?} Coarse-grained outcome reward models (ORMs) and fine-grained process reward models (PRMs) represent two extremes, yet neither alone achieves compute-optimality across all regimes. In this paper, we establish a unified theoretical framework, called \textbf{GRACE} (\underline{G}ranularity-\underline{R}egulated \underline{A}daptive \underline{C}omputational \underline{E}fficiency), that characterizes the optimal verification granularity as an explicit function of problem difficulty, verifier accuracy, and compute budget. We prove that there exists a phase transition: fine-grained verification dominates when either the compute budget is large or the problem is hard, whereas coarse-grained verification is preferred in the low-budget, easy-problem regime. Our theory unifies Best-of-$N$, beam search, and step-level MCTS within a single Pareto-optimality framework, and motivates an adaptive granularity strategy that provably achieves the compute-performance Pareto frontier. Empirical results on MATH-500, GSM8K, and AIME benchmarks corroborate all four theoretical claims, with our adaptive strategy outperforming fixed-granularity baselines by up to 3.1\% accuracy at matched compute.

### [12] [Trustworthy Multi-Agent Systems: Mitigating Semantic Drift with the Argent Signaling Protocol](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19356)
*Anantha Sharma*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces the Argent Signaling Protocol (ASP), a machine-readable header that attaches structured quality signals to LLM outputs so controllers can distinguish fixable from ungrounded failures, improving both standalone QA performance and safety in multi-agent pipelines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In multi-agent LLM systems, not all wrong answers are the same: some are partially correct and grounded in evidence and can be repaired, while others are ungrounded and should be contained. Existing retry strategies treat all failures uniformly, so systems keep retrying even when answers are hallucinated, and human supervisors cannot tell when a retry was justified versus when the system should have halted. The authors want a principled, machine-readable way to expose internal signals about answer quality so controllers can make smarter decisions about retries, repair, and containment.

Method: The authors design the Argent Signaling Protocol (ASP), a compact header that accompanies every LLM response. ASP encodes: (1) certainty (@C), (2) grounding (@G), (3) stochasticity (@S), and (4) an assumption index describing the evidentiary basis for each claim. These structured signals allow a controller to distinguish between repairable failures and containment cases and to route them differently (e.g., retry with different parameters vs. halt or escalate). They test ASP in two setups: (a) standalone mode, where controller policies, driven by ASP signals, manage a document-grounded QA task over a legal license agreement using three local GGUF models (Qwen 0.8B, Dobby 8B, SmolLM3 3B), and (b) multi-agent mode, where an ASP-enabled sidecar monitors outputs from a retrieval agent and decides what can safely be passed to a downstream decision agent, blocking ungrounded content.

Result: In standalone QA over 27 questions on the Array BioPharma/Ono license agreement, ASP-based controllers significantly improve performance versus baseline prompting. For Qwen (0.8B), pass rate rises from 11.1% to 33.3%, and mean term coverage from 36.7% to 65.4%. For Dobby (8B), ASP enables 4 fail-to-pass recoveries, moving pass rate from 33.3% to 44.4%. For SmolLM3 (3B), ASP adaptively chooses between repairing an answer and containing it, depending on question and signals. Overall, across three models and 81 question-model combinations, passes increase from 12/81 to 21/81. In multi-agent mode, the ASP sidecar completely prevents ungrounded retrieval outputs from propagating: it blocks all 24 ungrounded cases out of 27 total, with zero ungrounded answers reaching the downstream decision agent.

Conclusion: Encoding structured quality and grounding information into a protocol header (ASP) allows systems to treat different failure types differently, rather than blindly retrying. ASP improves answer quality and coverage in document-grounded QA for small local models and acts as an effective safety layer in multi-agent pipelines by blocking ungrounded outputs. This demonstrates that simple, explicit signaling about certainty, grounding, and assumptions can meaningfully enhance controllability, reliability, and containment in LLM-based systems.

Abstract: When multi-agent LLM systems produce bad answers, not all failures are equal: some answers are grounded in the right material but incomplete, while others are simply ungrounded and should be stopped. Current retry strategies treat both cases identically (try again and hope for the best), leaving human supervisors unable to tell whether a retry was warranted or whether the system should have halted instead.
  We introduce the Argent Signaling Protocol (ASP), a compact machine-readable header that accompanies every AI-generated response with structured quality signals: certainty (@C), grounding (@G), stochasticity (@S), and an assumption index that classifies the evidentiary basis of each claim. These signals enable a controller to distinguish repairable failures from containment failures and route each case differently.
  We evaluate ASP in two modes. In standalone mode, a 27-question document-grounded QA benchmark over the Array BioPharma/Ono license agreement compares baseline prompts against ASP-instrumented controller actions across three local GGUF models. On Qwen~(0.8B), ASP improves pass rate from 11.1% to 33.3% and mean term coverage from 36.7% to 65.4%; on Dobby~(8B), ASP produces 4 fail-to-pass recoveries, raising pass rate from 33.3% to 44.4%; on SmolLM3~(3B), ASP alternates between repair and containment per question. Aggregate improvement is meaningful (12/81 to 21/81 passes). In multi-agent mode, an ASP sidecar sits between a retrieval agent and a downstream decision agent; the sidecar blocks 100% of ungrounded upstream outputs from reaching the downstream agent (24/27 blocked, 0 ungrounded propagations).

### [13] [Characterizing Narrative Content in Web-scale LLM Pretraining Data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19468)
*Teagan Johnson,Elliott Ash,Andrew Piper,Maria Antoniak*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce NarraBERT and NarraDolma to measure fine-grained narrative structure (agency, setting, events) in a massive LLM pretraining corpus (Dolma), showing such structure is measurable, multidimensional, and unevenly distributed across sources and topics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Despite narrative being central to human communication, we know little about how narrative structures appear in the web-scale corpora used to pretrain large language models. Existing curation practices treat data largely in coarse ways (source, quality filters) and do not capture narrative dimensions, which may matter for models’ narrative reasoning abilities and downstream behavior. The authors aim to systematically characterize and quantify narrative features in a large pretraining corpus to provide insight into data composition and its potential effects.

Method: 1) Define a narrative-analytic framework based on narrative theory, covering three core elements—agency, setting, and events—further broken down into 11 interpretable, operationalized dimensions. 2) Sample 400 diverse passages from Dolma and manually annotate them along these dimensions. 3) Finetune and validate NarraBERT, a RoBERTa-based classifier/regressor for predicting the fine-grained narrative features. 4) Apply NarraBERT to 3 million passages from Dolma, creating an automatically annotated dataset named NarraDolma. 5) Analyze the large-scale annotations to characterize the distribution and structure of narrative qualities across sources and topics in the corpus.

Result: They demonstrate that (a) narrative structure can be reliably detected and quantified at scale in highly heterogeneous web text; (b) there exists a continuous, multidimensional latent narrative structure spanning the 11 dimensions across the corpus; and (c) narrative properties are distributed unevenly across data sources and topics, and these patterns are not captured by current corpus curation practices. They release NarraBERT and the NarraDolma annotations as public resources.

Conclusion: Narrative features are a measurable, structured component of web-scale LLM pretraining data and are distributed in systematic but uneven ways across sources and topics. Existing data curation overlooks these dimensions, potentially affecting models’ narrative reasoning capabilities. The proposed framework, NarraBERT model, and NarraDolma dataset establish a foundation for future work on how narrative composition of training data influences LLM behavior and performance on narrative tasks.

Abstract: The narrative composition of web-scale LLM pretraining corpora remains largely unexplored even though narrative is a fundamental mode of human communication. We present the first fine-grained study of narrative features in Dolma, a 3-trillion-token open pretraining corpus. Drawing on narrative theory, we design a framework spanning three core narrative elements (agency, setting, and events) operationalized as 11 interpretable dimensions. After sampling and annotating a diverse set of 400 passages, we finetune and validate NarraBERT, a RoBERTa-based model for fine-grained narrative prediction. We apply NarraBERT to 3M passages, resulting in a new dataset, NarraDolma. We find (i) narrative structure is measurable at scale across extremely heterogeneous data, (ii) we uncover a continuous, multidimensional narrative structure underlying web text, and (iii) narrative qualities are unequally distributed across pretraining sources and topics in ways that current curation practices neither measure nor account for. Our framework, dataset, and analyses provide a foundation for understanding how narrative qualities are distributed in LLM pretraining data and for studying how data composition affects narrative reasoning tasks. We publicly release NarraDolma and NarraBERT.

### [14] [Reliability without Validity: A Systematic, Large-Scale Evaluation of LLM-as-a-Judge Models Across Agreement, Consistency, and Bias](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19544)
*Justin D. Norman,Michael U. Rivera,D. Alex Hughes*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper critically examines current 'LLM-as-a-Judge' evaluation practices, showing that widely used exact-match agreement metrics are flawed, and proposes a more robust validation protocol based on extensive empirical analysis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-as-a-Judge is now the standard way to evaluate language models, but current validation of these judges typically uses exact-match agreement, which ignores chance agreement and inflates perceived reliability and discriminative power. This can mislead both researchers and practitioners about how trustworthy automated LLM-based evaluations really are. The authors aim to rigorously stress-test LLM judges at scale, identify systemic issues (like overestimated agreement and hidden biases), and provide a sounder methodology for validating such judges.

Method: The authors conduct a large-scale empirical evaluation of 21 LLM judges from nine providers on three major benchmarks (MT-Bench, JudgeBench, RewardBench). They run 118 evaluation experiments totaling about 541,000 individual judgments, under three main protocols: (1) agreement analysis, comparing judge outputs using both exact-match and Cohen's kappa to quantify chance-corrected agreement and measure 'kappa deflation'; (2) consistency analysis, studying test–retest reliability of judges across runs; and (3) bias audits, measuring systematic biases such as position bias and verbosity bias under a controlled pairwise scoring rubric. They then compare judge rankings across benchmarks and metrics and distill their empirical findings into a recommended validation protocol.

Result: Across all judges and benchmarks, they find: (1) a universal and substantial drop (33–41 percentage points on MT-Bench) when moving from exact-match agreement to Cohen's kappa, indicating that exact-match metrics systematically overstate agreement/discriminative ability; (2) judge rankings can shift by as many as 14 positions depending on the benchmark and metric used, highlighting instability in current leaderboards; (3) some production-deployed judges exhibit very high test–retest reliability (>0.95) while also showing strong position bias (>0.10), revealing a 'consistency–bias paradox' where judges can be stably wrong in a biased way; and (4) verbosity bias is relatively small (<0.011) under the examined pairwise rubric across the cohort. These quantitative findings are consistent across the full set of evaluated judges, including frontier models as of April 2026.

Conclusion: The study shows that current LLM-as-a-Judge validation practices are overoptimistic when based on exact-match agreement alone and can conceal serious, systematic biases even in highly consistent judges. Correcting for chance via Cohen's kappa, checking cross-benchmark ranking stability, and explicitly auditing for biases such as position bias are essential. The authors summarize these insights into a 'Minimum Viable Validation Protocol' that practitioners should adopt when deploying LLMs as evaluators, thereby making LLM-based evaluation more statistically sound, transparent, and robust.

Abstract: LLM-as-a-Judge has become the dominant evaluation paradigm for language models, but judge validation in practice relies on exact-match agreement, a metric that does not correct for chance and systematically overstates discriminative ability. We present the largest systematic evaluation of LLM-as-a-Judge to date: 21 judges from nine providers across MT-Bench, JudgeBench, and RewardBench, evaluated under three protocols (agreement, consistency, bias audit) over 118 runs and approximately 541,000 individual judgments. Four findings emerge, consistent across the full cohort, including the April 2026 frontier: kappa deflation between exact match and Cohen's kappa is universal (33--41 pp on MT-Bench), judge rankings shift by up to 14 positions across benchmarks, high test--retest reliability (>0.95) coexists with severe position bias (>0.10) in two production-deployed judges (instantiating a consistency--bias paradox), and verbosity bias is small (<0.011) across our cohort under a single pairwise rubric. We distill these into a Minimum Viable Validation Protocol.

</details>


### [15] [LaViSA: A Language and Vision Structural Ambiguity Benchmark](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19552)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Structural ambiguity—where one sentence has multiple valid meanings due to syntax—is a core problem in language understanding. Humans often rely on visual context to disambiguate such sentences, but it is unclear how well modern Vision-Language Models can do this. There is a need for a systematic, fine-grained benchmark to measure VLMs’ ability to use visual information to resolve different types of structural ambiguities.

Method: The authors construct the LaViSA benchmark, which contains (a) structurally ambiguous sentences, (b) their corresponding unambiguous (disambiguated) variants, and (c) images matched to those disambiguated meanings. The benchmark spans seven types of structural ambiguity. They then evaluate a range of VLMs—both proprietary and open-source, with different sizes and reasoning capabilities—on tasks that require choosing or generating the correct interpretation given the image and ambiguous sentence.

Result: Across the benchmark, modern VLMs show only partial success: they can sometimes use the visual scene to select the correct disambiguated meaning, but performance varies widely across ambiguity types. Models especially struggle with subtler forms of ambiguity and fine-grained semantic distinctions that hinge on small visual differences.

Conclusion: While VLMs can leverage images to help resolve some structural ambiguities, they remain far from human-level robustness in this area. LaViSA exposes systematic weaknesses, particularly for certain categories of ambiguity and visually subtle semantic contrasts, highlighting the need for better multimodal reasoning and more sophisticated training or architectures to handle structural ambiguity.

Abstract: Structural ambiguity arises when a single sentence admits multiple valid interpretations due to its syntactic structure, posing a fundamental challenge for language understanding. Visual scenes serve as useful cues for resolving such ambiguity, and Vision and Language Models (VLMs) need to be capable of deriving possible semantic interpretations from visual scenes. We introduce Language and Vision Structural Ambiguity (LaViSA), a benchmark designed to evaluate the ability of VLMs to resolve structural ambiguity leveraging visual scenes. LaViSA consists of ambiguous sentences, their disambiguated sentences, and corresponding images of these disambiguated sentences across seven ambiguity categories. Using LaViSA, we conduct a comprehensive evaluation of diverse VLMs, including both proprietary and open-source models with varying parameter scales and reasoning capabilities. Experimental results show that although recent VLMs can leverage visual scenes to resolve structural ambiguity to a some extent, they still struggle with certain ambiguity types and visually subtle semantic distinctions, indicating remaining limitations in resolving structural ambiguity using visual scenes.

The work demonstrates that careful design of intermediate stages in a hierarchical framework, especially aligning them with final summaries, is beneficial in low-resource languages like Vietnamese.

Abstract: In this technical report, we focus on solving the challenge of Vietnamese multi-document abstractive summarization, introduced in the International Workshop on Vietnamese Language and Speech Processing (VLSP) 2022. We choose to follow the popular hierarchical approach, i.e. condensing each document followed by aggregation and summarization. ### [17] [Where Does Social Reasoning Come From? Capability Provenance in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19625)
*Glenn Matlin,Chandreyi Chakraborty,Saehee Eom,Mika Okamoto,Rayan Castilla,Louis Jaburi,Alvin Deng,Taywon Min,Lucia Quirke,Stella Biderman,Mark Riedl*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper uses training-data attribution on OLMo3-7B to discover which parts of its pretraining corpus support social vs. STEM reasoning, validates these links with targeted unlearning, and releases resources.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To better understand and control which parts of a large language model’s training data give rise to different reasoning capabilities, especially social versus STEM reasoning, going beyond prior attribution work that focused mostly on factual knowledge at the document level.

Method: They apply gradient-based training-data attribution (TrackStar via Bergson) to OLMo3-7B using a working set from the deduplicated Dolma3 corpus. They aggregate influence scores over WebOrganizer’s 24-format × 24-topic taxonomy, yielding 576 corpus bins. They then contrast influence patterns across four benchmarks in a 2×2 design: social vs. STEM domains and reasoning vs. knowledge capabilities (SocialIQA and MMLU Social Sciences vs. ARC-Challenge and MMLU STEM). Finally, they perform targeted machine unlearning on high-attribution topic bins to test causal effects on benchmark performance.

Result: They find that social and STEM reasoning rely on qualitatively different regions of the training corpus, with a clearer separation at the reasoning level than at the knowledge level. When they selectively unlearn high-attribution topic bins (e.g., Literature for SocialIQA), performance on the corresponding benchmark drops more than when unlearning random data from the same bins, supporting a causal link between those corpus regions and specific capabilities.

Conclusion: Training-data attribution, when aggregated over a structured corpus taxonomy, can identify which corpus regions support different reasoning capabilities in LLMs. Social and STEM reasoning are underpinned by distinct training data, and targeted unlearning provides partial causal validation of these attributions. The authors release code, manifests, influence matrices, and unlearning checkpoints to support further research on data-centric capability discovery and control.

Abstract: We use training-data attribution as an interpretable tool for capability discovery, mapping which regions of the pretraining corpus support social-reasoning versus STEM-reasoning in OLMo3-7B. Training-data attribution measures how strongly each training document influences a model's predictions on a benchmark, but document-level scores are too noisy to identify which corpus regions support which capabilities, and prior work has emphasized factual knowledge rather than reasoning. We compute gradient-based attribution (TrackStar via Bergson) over a working set drawn from the de-duplicated Dolma3 mix, aggregate influence across WebOrganizer's 24-format x 24-topic taxonomy (576 bins), and contrast benchmark pairs in a 2x2 design that varies domain (social vs. STEM) and capability type (reasoning vs. knowledge): SocialIQA and MMLU Social Sciences against ARC-Challenge and MMLU STEM. Social and STEM reasoning draw on qualitatively distinct corpus regions, and the contrast is sharper at the reasoning level than at the knowledge level. Targeted machine unlearning provides partial causal validation: forgetting high-attribution topic bins (e.g., Literature for SocialIQA) degrades the aligned benchmark more than within-bin random baselines, and we open-source all code, sampling manifests, the bin-level influence matrix, and unlearning checkpoints.

</details>


### [18] [Before the Labels: How Dataset Construction Shapes Suicidality Detection in Clinical Text](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19637)
*Priyanshi Garg,Ishita Rao,Jieqiong Ding,Amandalynne Paullada*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical NLP often treats suicidality labels derived from EHRs as more trustworthy than those from social media, but this may ignore how institutional processes and clinician perspectives shape what is recorded as suicidality. The authors want to surface and analyze these hidden assumptions to prevent misinterpretation and misuse of such datasets.

Method: They conduct a case study on the ScAN dataset derived from MIMIC-III clinical notes. They analyze how dataset construction choices—governance constraints, ICD-code-based cohort selection, single-annotator labeling, and aggregation at the hospital-stay level—shape the nature of suicidality labels. They further perform a linguistic analysis of the clinical notes to show that instances sharing the same suicidality label actually encompass diverse clinical framings (e.g., differing temporality, negation, and uncertainty).

Result: They find that the labels primarily reflect clinician-documented judgments rather than an objective measure of suicidality, frame suicidality as discrete episodes tied to hospital stays, and rely on the assumption that suicidal intent is reliably inferable from documentation. Their linguistic analysis shows that texts with identical labels often differ substantially in terms of whether suicidality is past, present, or hypothetical; affirmed or negated; and expressed with varying degrees of uncertainty.

Conclusion: EHR-based suicidality datasets encode a specific, institutionally shaped operationalization of suicidality. Therefore, clinical NLP researchers should critically examine the construction choices and embedded assumptions of such datasets instead of uncritically treating their labels as ground truth, especially when building or evaluating models intended to detect suicidal behavior.

Abstract: Clinical NLP increasingly relies on electronic health record (EHR) data to detect suicidal behaviors, treating clinical documentation as more reliable ground truth than social media. We argue that this framing obscures how EHR-based suicidality datasets encode a particular operationalization of suicidality, shaped by who authors the data, how episodes are bounded, and how ambiguity is resolved. We ground this argument in a case study of the ScAN dataset, built over MIMIC-III clinical notes. We show how governance constraints, ICD-based cohort selection, single-annotator labeling, and hospital-stay-level aggregation produce labels that reflect clinician-documented judgments, treat suicidality as a bounded episode, and assume that intent can be reliably inferred from documentation. A linguistic analysis demonstrates that identical labels subsume heterogeneous clinical framings differing in temporality, negation, and uncertainty. We argue that clinical NLP should examine the assumptions embedded in suicidality datasets before interpreting their labels as ground truth.

### [19] [MiqraBERT: Regression-Based Sentence-BERT Finetuning for Biblical Hebrew Parallel Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19638)
*David M. Smiley*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents MiqraBERT, a Sentence-BERT model fine-tuned for detecting semantic similarity between Biblical Hebrew verses, outperforming lexical-overlap-based methods especially for narrative parallels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing computational methods for detecting textual reuse in the Hebrew Bible rely heavily on lexical overlap and perform poorly when parallels involve paraphrase, lexical substitution, or syntactic reworking. There is a need for a semantic similarity model tailored to Biblical Hebrew to more effectively identify such nuanced parallels.

Method: The authors fine-tune a Sentence-BERT architecture starting from AlephBERT, a Modern Hebrew encoder, targeting verse-level semantic similarity in Biblical Hebrew. They construct a training dataset of 1,650 verse and half-verse pairs: 825 true parallels (from Chronicles' synoptic material and seminal studies of poetic parallelism) and 825 randomly sampled negative pairs. Using cosine-similarity regression, they train an embedding space where parallel verses cluster and unrelated verses are separated. They evaluate the separation quality via distribution-based metrics, specifically Wasserstein distance and the overlap coefficient, across ten random seeds.

Result: Compared to the pre-trained AlephBERT baseline, MiqraBERT improves distributional separation between parallel and non-parallel verses by a factor of 2.7 and shrinks the ambiguous overlap region from about 24% to around 6%. For narrative synoptic parallels, the model achieves a recall@10 of 87.1%. Performance on poetic parallels is much lower, under 9%, revealing a strong genre dependency.

Conclusion: MiqraBERT substantially enhances the detection of narrative textual reuse in the Hebrew Bible by modeling semantic similarity rather than simple lexical overlap, but it struggles with poetic parallels, limiting its reliable applicability to narrative genres. The model is released publicly for further research and applications in computational Biblical studies.

Abstract: Textual reuse pervades the Hebrew Bible, yet the computational methods used to detect it still rest largely on lexical overlap, and they falter once a parallel involves paraphrase, lexical substitution, or syntactic reworking. This paper introduces MiqraBERT, a Sentence-BERT model finetuned from AlephBERT (a Modern Hebrew encoder) for verse-level semantic similarity in Biblical Hebrew. The training set comprises 1,650 labeled verse and half-verse pairs: 825 true parallels drawn from the Chronicles synoptic material and from foundational studies of poetic parallelism, balanced against 825 randomly sampled negatives. Through cosine-similarity regression, the model learns an embedding space in which parallel verses cluster together and unrelated verses move apart. We evaluate separation with distribution-based metrics, Wasserstein distance and the overlap coefficient, across ten random seeds. MiqraBERT improves distributional separation 2.7-fold over the pre-trained baseline and reduces the ambiguous overlap region from roughly 24% to about 6%. Narrative synoptic parallels reach a recall@10 of 87.1%; poetic parallels remain difficult, below 9%. This genre-dependent asymmetry confines the model's reliable scope to narrative textual reuse. MiqraBERT is publicly available at https://huggingface.co/davidmsmiley/MiqraBERT

Abstract: AI and large language models (LLMs) have emerged as promising tools to address global mental health challenges. To mitigate this gap, researchers increasingly generate synthetic clinical personas to simulate user data and test digital mental health support systems. However, most validated personas rely on English-centric contexts. This paper investigates whether similar persona-based methods can be used to generate multilingual mental health datasets. We modified nationality and language parameters in personas to generate clinical dialogues in Mandarin, Bengali, and Hindi. We then examined how different LLMs perform when evaluating the depression severity of these generated multilingual datasets against the baseline in English. Our findings indicate that just adding nationality and language parameters in personas might not be adequate, as it can introduce clinical inconsistency across languages. LLM judge models often exhibit inaccuracies in assessing depression severity in non-English texts, with performance varying across different models. This exposes the systemic limitations of applying English-centric personas to multilingual contexts. Ultimately, our work highlights the urgent need for culturally responsive data generation to ensure equitable mental health systems globally.

Findings suggest that distinct languages carried distinct frames: Portuguese mobilization, Spanish crisis, English nation-making, and a shared platform-metric spectacle through which peripheral athletic performance became globally visible. As a v0.1 pilot, the paper releases the corpus schema, frame taxonomy, annotation guidelines, hashed visual-evidence log, and typed timeline, while flagging full double annotation and inter-annotator agreement as planned work.
