### [22] [SAGE-OPD: Selective Agent-Guided Intervention for Multi-Turn On-Policy Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19659)
*Yuhang Zhou,Lizhu Zhang,Yifan Wu,Mingyi Wang,Bo Peng,Jiayi Liu,Xiangjun Fan,Zhuokai Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper introduces SAGE-OPD, a selective, verifier-free on-policy distillation framework tailored for multi-turn LLM agents, which improves robustness by only intervening on turns where the teacher is confident and feedback indicates a need, achieving significant gains over standard OPD.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard on-policy distillation methods are mostly designed for single-turn settings and apply dense token-level supervision uniformly, which becomes brittle for multi-turn LLM agents. In multi-turn environments, early errors compound and change future observations, and uniform OPD can over-penalize valid alternatives, entrench local pathologies like repeated actions, and propagate unreliable teacher guidance on off-distribution trajectories. There is a need for an OPD framework that respects on-policy training while allocating teacher supervision more judiciously across turns.

Method: The authors propose SAGE-OPD, a verifier-free selective intervention framework for multi-turn OPD. The method first rolls out the student policy on-policy, then, after observing environment feedback, uses a teacher model to decide per turn whether to skip supervision or to intervene on the student's response. When intervening, token-level distillation losses are reweighted by the teacher's confidence to reduce the effect of uncertain supervision, especially on corrupted or ambiguous histories. Additionally, a loss normalization scheme is introduced so that the aggregate loss scale matches standard OPD while still allowing selective turn-level weighting. The framework thus combines on-policy trajectories, selective turn-level intervention, confidence-based weighting, and normalized losses.

Result: On multi-turn agent benchmarks, including ALFWorld, SAGE-OPD consistently outperforms standard OPD and other baselines. In particular, it achieves up to a 13.3% relative improvement in unseen success rate on ALFWorld compared to standard OPD. Ablation studies show that each component—turn-level selective intervention, teacher confidence weighting, and loss normalization—contributes distinctly, and their combination yields the best performance.

Conclusion: The study concludes that for multi-turn LLM agents, effective on-policy distillation should remain on-policy but must avoid uniform, dense supervision across all turns. Instead, teacher guidance should be applied selectively at turns where environment feedback and teacher confidence indicate that intervention is both necessary and reliable. SAGE-OPD operationalizes this principle and delivers significant performance gains, suggesting that selective, confidence-aware supervision is a key ingredient for robust multi-turn OPD.

Abstract: On-policy distillation (OPD) improves student models by training them on trajectories induced by their own policy, making it a promising approach for mitigating exposure bias in agent training. However, most OPD studies focus on single-turn settings, while realistic LLM agents interact with environments over multiple turns. In this regime, early errors can alter future observations and compound across the trajectory, and standard dense token-level OPD becomes brittle, as it may over-penalize semantically valid alternatives, reinforce local degeneracies such as repeated actions, and propagate unreliable teacher supervision on off-distribution histories. We propose SAGE-OPD, a verifier-free selective intervention framework specifically designed for multi-turn OPD. Instead of applying teacher supervision uniformly across all turns, SAGE-OPD first observes environment feedback and uses teacher judgment to decide whether each student response should be skipped or intervened on. To further address compounding errors, SAGE-OPD weights token-level distillation by teacher confidence, reducing the influence of uncertain teacher distributions on corrupted or ambiguous histories. Finally, SAGE-OPD applies loss normalization to preserve the overall loss scale of standard OPD while retaining selective turn-level weighting. Experiments on agent tasks show that SAGE-OPD consistently improves over baselines, achieving up to a 13.3% relative improvement in ALFWorld unseen success rate over standard OPD. Ablation studies further demonstrate that turn-level intervention, teacher confidence weighting, and loss normalization provide complementary benefits. Our results suggest that effective multi-turn OPD should remain on-policy, but teacher supervision should be selectively allocated to turns where intervention is necessary and reliable.

</details>


### [23] [CacheWeaver: Cache-Aware Evidence Ordering for Efficient Grounded RAG Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19667)
*Kaizhen Tan,Rong Gu,Mingyuan Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CacheWeaver is a prompt-layer method that reorders retrieved evidence to maximize shared prefixes between queries, enabling better reuse of prefix caching in RAG systems and significantly reducing time-to-first-token without changing retrieval results or answer quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In Retrieval-Augmented Generation, adding external evidence improves factual accuracy but makes prompts longer and increases prefill (prefix) computation cost. Existing prefix caching in serving engines like vLLM only helps when different requests share an identical prefix. In practice, RAG queries often retrieve overlapping evidence pieces but in different orders, so overlapping content does not translate into overlapping token prefixes and the cache benefit is largely lost. A method is needed to exploit this latent overlap without modifying the model or retrieval system, and without degrading answer quality.

Method: CacheWeaver introduces a cache-aware, prompt-layer reordering strategy for the sequence of retrieved evidence chunks in RAG. It maintains a prefix tree (trie) over the evidence-order sequences recently served to the model. For each new query, it performs a greedy walk over this prefix tree to choose an ordering of the retrieved evidence that maximizes reuse of an existing prefix, subject to preserving the same evidence set. This reordering is done between retrieval and inference and does not change which evidence is used or how the serving engine operates. The approach is lightweight and compatible with existing prefix caching in engines like vLLM.

Result: In experiments across three different vLLM deployment configurations, CacheWeaver reduces median time-to-first-token (TTFT) by approximately 20–33% compared with a baseline where evidence is ordered according to the retrieval system’s native order (retrieval-order prefix caching). The method preserves answer quality in QA evaluations, indicating that reordering evidence does not harm model accuracy. A comparison with an oracle that optimally orders evidence shows that the greedy policy recovers 97.5% of the achievable median TTFT improvement, suggesting that simple scheduling captures nearly all practical prefix-reuse benefits.

Conclusion: CacheWeaver demonstrates that a simple prompt-layer reordering of retrieved evidence, informed by a prefix tree over recent requests, can substantially improve efficiency in RAG systems by enhancing prefix-cache reuse, without modifying the underlying model, retriever, or serving engine. The approach delivers sizable reductions in time-to-first-token while maintaining answer quality, and its near-oracle performance indicates that most potential caching locality can be unlocked via a lightweight scheduling layer between retrieval and inference.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) improves factual grounding, but it also lengthens prompts and raises prefill cost. Prefix caching in serving engines such as vLLM reduces this cost only when requests share the same token prefix. In grounded generation, however, adjacent queries may retrieve overlapping evidence in different orders, so set overlap does not become reusable prefix overlap. We present CacheWeaver, a lightweight prompt-layer method for cache-aware evidence ordering. The method keeps a prefix tree over recently served evidence sequences and uses a greedy walk to place the most reusable prefix first, while leaving the serving engine and retrieved evidence set unchanged. Across three vLLM configurations, the method lowers median time-to-first-token (TTFT) by about 20-33 percent relative to retrieval-order prefix caching, without hurting answer quality in our QA tests. The greedy policy reaches 97.5 percent of the median TTFT gain from oracle ordering, indicating that most reusable prefix locality can be recovered by a simple scheduling layer between retrieval and inference.

</details>


### [24] [Code-Switching Reveals Language Anchoring in Multilingual LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19668)
*Jeonghyun Park,Seunghyun Yoon,Yonghyun Jun,Hwanhee Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates why multilingual large language models perform worse on code-switched inputs and proposes an inference-time method, CANVAS, that realigns internal representations to mitigate this degradation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multilingual LLMs are widely used in settings where speakers naturally mix languages (code-switching), but these models often show worse performance on such inputs compared to purely monolingual ones. Existing work has limited insight into *where* and *how* this degradation arises in the internal representations of MLLMs. The authors aim to develop a controlled diagnostic framework to localize the problem in representation space and then leverage this understanding to design a lightweight, inference-time intervention that improves performance on code-switched tasks without retraining the models.

Method: 1) Construct a grammar-forced code-switching setup where they systematically generate CS inputs that share clear source and target monolingual counterparts, allowing controlled comparison of hidden states. 2) Define a geometric metric, Anchor Bias, which for a given CS hidden state measures whether it is closer (in representation space) to the source-language or target-language monolingual counterpart. 3) Apply this diagnostic to multiple multilingual LLMs across different CS patterns and question answering tasks, analyzing how CS framing (source-framed vs target-framed) affects representation anchoring and downstream performance. 4) Based on these findings, introduce CANVAS (Contextual Anchor-based Neural Vector Alignment Steering), an inference-time method: it extracts a ‘source-side canvas’ from the input, identifies source-language anchor representations, and then softly steers target-language hidden states toward these source anchors during the prefill phase, modifying internal vectors but not model weights. 5) Evaluate CANVAS across models and CS conditions by measuring improvements in QA F1 scores.

Result: The Anchor Bias metric shows a systematic grammar-frame effect across diverse MLLMs: when the sentence structure is framed in the source language, the code-switched representations remain closer to the source-language anchors; when framed in the target language, the representations shift toward the target-language side and are associated with larger drops in QA performance. This indicates that representational anchoring is tightly coupled with grammatical framing and correlates with CS-induced degradation. Applying CANVAS realigns internal representations toward more stable source-language anchors. Empirically, CANVAS consistently improves QA F1 scores across different multilingual LLMs and various code-switching conditions, partially or fully recovering the performance lost due to code-switching, without retraining.

Conclusion: The study concludes that code-switching performance degradation in multilingual LLMs is closely linked to how internal representations are anchored between source and target languages, particularly under different grammatical frames. Anchor Bias provides a clear, quantitative lens on these anchoring dynamics, revealing that target-framed CS tends to induce harmful target-ward shifts in representation space. Crucially, these anchoring patterns are not merely diagnostic but offer a practical control signal: the proposed CANVAS method can steer hidden states toward beneficial anchors at inference time and thereby mitigate code-switching failures. Overall, the work shows that fine-grained analysis of representation geometry can guide lightweight, training-free interventions that improve multilingual, code-switched QA performance.

Abstract: Multilingual Large Language Models (MLLMs) are increasingly expected to handle Code-Switched (CS) inputs, yet mixing languages frequently degrades performance relative to source- or target-language monolingual counterparts. To understand this degradation, we use grammar-forced CS as a controlled diagnostic setting for locating CS representations relative to their source and target counterparts. We introduce Anchor Bias, a geometric measure that quantifies language anchoring, whether a CS hidden state aligns closer to its source or target language counterpart. Across diverse MLLMs, Anchor Bias reveals a consistent grammar-frame effect: source-framed CS stays source-anchored, whereas target-framed CS shifts target-ward and shows larger Question Answering (QA) degradation. Motivated by this representational pattern, we propose CANVAS (Contextual Anchor-based Neural Vector Alignment Steering), an inference-time intervention that extracts a source-side canvas from the input and softly steers target-language hidden states toward the source anchor during prefill. CANVAS consistently recovers QA F1 across MLLMs and CS conditions, showing that internal anchoring signals provide an actionable target for mitigating CS inference failures.

</details>


### [25] [What sentiment analysis can't see: Measuring whether customers were helped, and what went wrong, across 70,000 support conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19698)

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This leads to blind spots: neutral tone can hide both success and failure, and negative tone may trigger false alarms even when outcomes are fine. The authors want a better way to read customer conversations that reflects true satisfaction and problem types, to guide product and support improvements more accurately.

Method: The authors used GPT-5.4 to annotate 70,450 customer support conversations from a major online fundraising platform. For each conversation, the model produced three labels: (1) tone/sentiment, (2) an estimate of the customer’s satisfaction, and (3) a flag for whether the customer reported a specific, concrete problem. They then validated these three annotations against the 1–5 star ratings that customers voluntarily left on rated conversations, comparing how well satisfaction vs. sentiment correlated with those ratings and how effectively each identified unhappy customers. They also analyzed disagreements between tone and satisfaction and categorized patterns such as neutral tone with differing satisfaction levels and cases of satisfied customers who still reported fixable issues.

Analysis showed that sentiment alone misses important distinctions: in 44% of conversations, tone and satisfaction disagree; a single neutral tone label covers a wide range of underlying outcomes from satisfied to quietly dissatisfied; and the largest segment is “tolerated friction,” customers who end up satisfied but still report a solvable problem. These nuanced states are invisible to sentiment-only dashboards.

Conclusion: LLM-based annotation of support conversations can capture detailed, actionable information about customer satisfaction and the presence and nature of concrete problems, going well beyond raw emotional tone. This enables new business metrics centered on the customer’s actual state and the root causes of their issues, derived directly from unstructured text. As a result, organizations can move past sentiment-only analytics toward more accurate detection of dissatisfaction, better prioritization of product fixes, and a clearer understanding of where customers experience but tolerate friction.

Abstract: Most companies read their customer support data at scale using sentiment analysis, which measures how customers sound rather than whether they were satisfied with the result. We tested a richer alternative on 70,450 support conversations from a leading online fundraising platform: alongside tone, we used GPT-5.4 to estimate each customer's satisfaction and to flag whether they reported a concrete problem, then validated all three readings against the 1-to-5 ratings customers left on the conversations they rated. The satisfaction estimate tracked those ratings far better than sentiment did, correlating at 0.47 against 0.36 and flagging unhappy customers with far fewer false alarms. The structured read also sees what sentiment cannot: tone and satisfaction disagree in 44% of conversations, a single "Neutral" label hides everything from quietly satisfied customers to ones who quietly gave up, and the largest group of all is "tolerated friction," customers who are satisfied but still reporting a fixable problem, a standing issue that no sentiment-based dashboard can surface. The broader finding is that LLM-based annotation can capture far more than the tonality of a customer's language, offering strong potential for new business metrics grounded instead in the customer's state (whether they were satisfied) and the cause of their problem extracted directly from the raw textual data of interactions and feedback.

TL;DR: The paper proposes FineREX, a fine-tuned LLM-based pipeline for constructing knowledge graphs from court documents about human smuggling, achieving significantly better NER/RE accuracy, cleaner graphs, and faster processing than larger general-purpose models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Court proceedings contain rich but buried information about human smuggling networks. Existing knowledge graph construction methods rely on general-purpose LLMs that are not optimized for the specific entities and relations in this domain, leading to noisy, redundant graphs and inefficient multi-stage pipelines. There is a need for a domain-tuned approach that improves extraction quality and efficiency for illicit network analysis.

Method: The authors develop FineREX, a streamlined knowledge graph construction pipeline centered on a fine-tuned LLM specialized for named entity recognition and relation extraction (NER-RE) in court documents about human smuggling. They manually annotate 512 text chunks with domain-specific entities and relations, use this dataset to fine-tune the LLM, and integrate the model into a simplified pipeline that removes document rewriting and redundant extraction stages. They then compare performance against a larger general-purpose LLM baseline in terms of NER/RE F1-scores, graph quality metrics, and processing time.

Result: FineREX achieves absolute improvements of 15.50% in entity F1-score and 31.46% in relationship F1-score over the larger general-purpose baseline. These improvements yield higher-quality knowledge graphs, with legal noise nearly halved and node duplication on long documents reduced from 17.78% to 11.17%. The streamlined pipeline also cuts end-to-end processing time by 50.0%.

Conclusion: Domain-specific fine-tuning of LLMs for NER and relation extraction can substantially outperform larger general-purpose models for building knowledge graphs from legal texts. FineREX delivers more accurate, cleaner, and more efficient knowledge graph construction for analyzing illicit networks, highlighting the value of targeted fine-tuning and simplified pipelines over relying solely on large, generic LLMs.

Abstract: Court proceedings contain valuable evidence about human smuggling networks, but this information is often buried within unstructured, jargon-heavy legal documents. While large language models (LLMs) can support knowledge graph construction through automated information extraction, existing approaches rely on general-purpose models that are not tailored to the entity and relationship definitions required in this domain. We introduce FineREX, a streamlined knowledge graph construction pipeline built around a fine-tuned LLM for named entity recognition and relationship extraction (NER-RE). Using a manually annotated dataset of $512$ text chunks, FineREX achieves absolute improvements of 15.50% and 31.46% in entity and relationship F1-score, respectively, compared to a larger general-purpose baseline. These gains translate into higher-quality knowledge graphs, reducing legal noise by nearly half and lowering node duplication on long documents from 17.78% to 11.17%. By eliminating document rewriting and redundant extraction stages, FineREX also reduces end-to-end processing time by 50.0%. Our results demonstrate that domain-specific fine-tuning can substantially outperform larger general-purpose models while improving both the quality and efficiency of knowledge graph construction for illicit network analysis.

### [29] [Beyond Uniform Forgetting: A Study of Sequential Direct Preference Optimization Across Preference Settings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19744)
*Pranav Bhandari,Nicolas Fay,Amitava Datta,Usman Naseem,Mehwish Nasim*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how sequentially applying multiple preference optimization objectives using DPO affects previously learned preferences, finding that the impact varies with objective relationships, signal strength, and training order rather than following a uniform forgetting pattern.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In aligning language models to human preferences, practitioners often optimize several behavioural objectives (e.g., safety, helpfulness, style) one after another using methods like Direct Preference Optimization. However, it is unclear whether later optimization steps consistently degrade earlier-aligned behaviours or whether the outcome depends on how these objectives relate to each other. Understanding this is important for designing reliable multi-stage alignment pipelines.

Method: The authors perform systematic experiments with sequential Direct Preference Optimization on Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct using LoRA adapters. They construct four preference settings that capture different relationships between objectives: distributional conflict, multi-attribute interaction, strong safety signals, and compatible response-quality objectives. At each stage of sequential training, they evaluate all objectives against a fixed base-model reference rather than a shifting one. They further conduct pair-level analysis using length-normalized policy margins and quartile decomposition to understand heterogeneous changes in preferences. Mechanistic diagnostics, including analysis of Stage 2 gradients and adapter parameter updates, are used to study alignment or orthogonality between objectives at the representation/parameter level.

Result: The experiments show that sequential DPO does not induce a single, consistent forgetting pattern. Instead, its effect on earlier preferences ranges from partial degradation to relative stability, redistribution across preference pairs, and even positive transfer. The specific outcome depends on the relationships among objectives, the strength of the optimization signals, and the order of training. Pair-level margin analysis reveals heterogeneous changes often hidden by aggregate metrics, and quartile analysis shows that even high-confidence preference pairs can either deteriorate or improve under subsequent training. Mechanistically, gradients and adapter updates from the second training stage are found to be nearly orthogonal to those from the first stage, suggesting that direct gradient opposition is not the main cause of preference changes.

Conclusion: Sequential preference optimization with DPO should not be assumed to produce uniform forgetting of earlier objectives. Instead, the interaction between objectives, their signal strengths, and training order critically shapes how preferences evolve over stages. Aggregate metrics can be misleading, necessitating finer-grained pair-level analyses. The near-orthogonality of gradients across stages implies that more subtle mechanisms drive preference interactions. Future alignment pipelines should explicitly consider objective compatibility and signal strength when designing sequential training strategies, rather than treating later objectives as uniformly overriding earlier ones.

Abstract: Aligning language models with human preferences often requires optimising multiple behavioural objectives. A practical approach is to apply these objectives sequentially using preference optimisation methods such as Direct Preference Optimisation (DPO), but it remains unclear whether later training uniformly degrades preferences learned earlier or whether the effect depends on the relationship between objectives. We study sequential DPO across four preference settings covering distributional conflict, multi-attribute interaction, strong safety signal, and compatible response-quality objectives. Using Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct with LoRA adapters, we evaluate all objectives after every stage with a fixed base-model reference. We find that sequential DPO does not produce a single forgetting pattern; preference change ranges from partial degradation to stability, pair-level redistribution, or positive transfer depending on objective relationship, signal strength, and training order. Pair-level analysis using length-normalised policy margins shows that aggregate metrics can mask heterogeneous changes across preference pairs, whereas quartile decomposition reveals that high-confidence pairs can either degrade or improve depending on the setting. Mechanistic diagnostics show that Stage~2 gradients and adapter updates are near-orthogonal to the previous objective across all settings, providing little evidence that direct gradient opposition is the primary driver. These findings suggest that future sequential alignment pipelines should account for objective compatibility and signal strength, rather than assuming that later objectives affect earlier preferences uniformly.

### [30] [Clusters are All You Need: Pre-Training the Tsetlin Machine with Semantic Clusters from Language Models for Interpretability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19815)
*Jiechao Gao,Rohan Kumar Yadav,Yuangang Li,Yuandong Pan,Jie Wang,Ying Liu,Michael Lepech*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a semantic pre-training framework that transfers knowledge from a pre-trained language model into a fully interpretable Tsetlin Machine, achieving near-BERT performance in text classification while preserving interpretability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Pre-trained language models like BERT perform very well on text classification but are opaque, which is problematic in high-stakes domains that require transparency. Tsetlin Machines, on the other hand, are highly interpretable but weak in capturing semantic and contextual information. Previous attempts to combine them used static word embeddings, which fail to capture contextual meaning, leaving a gap for a method that can bring contextual semantics into TMs without sacrificing interpretability.

Method: The authors design a semantic pre-training pipeline where a powerful pre-trained language model is used indirectly: they cluster text samples into semantically coherent groups using K-means or Top2Vec, producing cluster labels that reflect semantic structure. These cluster-sample pairs are then used to pre-train a non-negated Tsetlin Machine, with modified (enhanced) Type I feedback to encourage learning of positive, interpretable clauses. The TM learns human-understandable semantic keywords and clauses during this pre-training phase, and is subsequently fine-tuned on specific downstream text classification tasks.

Result: On five benchmark datasets, the semantically pre-trained TM significantly outperforms both standard (vanilla) Tsetlin Machines and previous variants that rely on static word embeddings. Its classification performance is reported to be competitive with BERT, narrowing the accuracy gap while maintaining the TM’s inherently interpretable, clause-based decision process.

Conclusion: By transferring semantic structure from a pre-trained language model to a Tsetlin Machine via clustering-based pre-training, the proposed framework enables interpretable models to approach the accuracy of state-of-the-art opaque models like BERT. This demonstrates that it is possible to obtain strong text classification performance without sacrificing transparency, and positions the approach as a promising option for high-stakes applications requiring both accountability and accuracy.

Abstract: Pre-trained language models such as BERT achieve strong text classification performance but lack transparency, limiting their use in high-stakes settings. The Tsetlin Machine (TM) offers fully interpretable, clause-based reasoning but captures little semantic information, and prior attempts to bridge the two rely on static word embeddings that miss contextual meaning. We propose a semantic pre-training framework that transfers knowledge from a pre-trained language model into a TM without using embeddings. Text samples are grouped into semantically coherent clusters with K-means or Top2Vec, and the resulting cluster-sample pairs pre-train a non-negated TM with enhanced Type I feedback. The TM thereby learns interpretable semantic keywords that are fine-tuned on downstream tasks. Across five datasets, our method substantially outperforms vanilla and embedding-based TMs and reaches performance competitive with BERT while remaining interpretable.

### [31] [CREDENCE: Claim Reduction for Decomposition & Enhanced Credibility -- Semantic Metrics and Convergence Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19819)
*Phuong Huu Vu Tran,Thuan Duc Mai,Bach Xuan Le*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Credence, a framework for decomposing complex sentences into atomic claims for fact-checking, replacing token-overlap metrics with a semantic similarity metric and providing formal guarantees for a repair pipeline, along with new benchmarks and multi-model evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods for decomposing sentences into atomic claims use Jaccard (token-overlap) metrics, which penalize paraphrases and thus underestimate decomposition quality, and they lack a formal analysis of the termination and behavior of the repair loop used to refine decompositions. This hinders reliable automated fact-checking and cross-domain generalization assessment.

Method: The authors propose Credence, which consists of: (1) a new evaluation metric, Semantic-F1, based on cosine similarity from the BGE-large embedding model, to measure fidelity of decomposed claims without penalizing paraphrasing; (2) formal convergence theorems analyzing the repair pipeline, proving monotone and finitely terminating behavior for a rule-based repair approach under an oracle parser assumption, and showing that LLM-based self-repair is non-monotone and thus needs an early-exit guard; (3) three evaluation benchmarks across social media, encyclopedic, and news domains (SocialClaimSplit, WikiSplitBench, ClaimDecompBench); and (4) a multi-model benchmarking setup involving four open decomposer models (3.8B–12B) plus a closed API model.

Result: Across the three benchmarks, Semantic-F1 improves over the traditional Jaccard-F1 metric by 15–32 percentage points in measuring decomposition quality, and Effective Precision-Recall (EPR) scores range from 0.94 to 1.00 on SocialClaimSplit and WikiSplitBench. ClaimDecompBench, being more difficult due to news-style constructions, shows lower base EPR (down to 0.824). Applying rule-based repair significantly decreases the Atomicity Violation Rate (AVR) by 47–100% over the base decomposer without harming fidelity as measured by Semantic-F1.

Conclusion: Credence provides a more accurate and semantically aware evaluation of claim decomposition quality, accompanied by theoretical guarantees about the behavior and termination of repair procedures. It demonstrates strong performance across diverse domains and shows that rule-based repair can meaningfully improve atomicity without sacrificing fidelity, making it a better foundation for automated fact-checking systems than prior Jaccard-based approaches.

Abstract: Decomposing compound sentences into atomic, verifiable claims is a prerequisite for reliable automated fact-checking. Prior work has relied on token-overlap (Jaccard) metrics that systematically underestimate decomposition quality for paraphrastic claims, and has lacked formal termination analysis for the repair loop. We present Credence, a revised claim decomposition and evaluation framework addressing both shortcomings. Our contributions are: (1) Semantic-F1: we use BGE-large cosine similarity fidelity metric that resolves Jaccard's penalisation and improves downstream fact-checking accuracy; (2) Convergence theorems: we formally characterise four properties of the repair pipeline, establishing that rule-based repair is monotone and finitely terminating under an oracle parser assumption; LLM-based self-repair is provably non-monotone and requires an early-exit guard; (3) Three evaluation benchmarks spanning social-media, encyclopaedic, and news domains for cross-domain generalisation measurement; (4) Multi-model benchmarking across four decomposer models (3.8B-12B) and a closed API model. Experiments on SocialClaimSplit, WikiSplitBench, and ClaimDecompBench show that Semantic-F1 outperforms Jaccard-F1 by +15-32pp. EPR ranges from 0.94 to 1.00 on SocialClaimSplit and WikiSplitBench, while ClaimDecompBench includes lower base EPR cases (down to 0.824) due to harder news-domain constructions, and rule-repair reduces the Atomicity Violation Rate (AVR) by 47-100% relative to the base model without degrading fidelity.

### [32] [Leverage Is Not Reach: A Control-Window Law for Single-Neuron Steering in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19831)
*Hongliang Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a theoretical and empirical framework explaining when and how single neurons in aligned language models can coherently control behaviors (like refusal or mode switches) without causing model collapse, treating steering as a budgeted intervention problem.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although sparse neurons in large language models are known to gate high-level behaviors (such as refusal or language routing), there is no principled theory predicting when directly steering a single neuron will produce a clean, coherent change in behavior versus destabilizing or collapsing the model’s output. Practitioners have relied on ad hoc, anecdotal neuron interventions without guarantees, and existing gradient-based attribution methods often fail to identify true control neurons. This paper aims to provide a predictive, mechanistic theory for single-neuron control in aligned LMs.

Method: The authors introduce a "budget normalized control window" framework. They model single-neuron interventions as moving the residual stream along a specific write direction, and show that a dose along this direction reduces to a single control coordinate: the inner product between the residual stream and the write vector. This coordinate evolves along a universal saturation curve parameterized by a coherence budget proportional to (residual-norm / write-norm). Under this model, coherent behavioral control is possible when the activation needed to trigger a behavior lies below a predicted collapse ceiling. They analytically derive the ceiling from model weights and a single generic forward pass, and empirically measure behavior triggers at rollout. They test predictions on 15 previously unseen neurons, analyze failure modes (e.g., collapse before trigger, insufficient depth, normalization limits), and compare controller discovery via forward-only contrastive screening against gradient attribution methods.

Result: On a set of 15 held-out neurons, the framework’s predicted collapse ceiling matches empirical behavior with a mean absolute error of about 0.14 overall, and ~0.07 in bulk layers, and its binary prediction of whether a neuron can be coherently opened or closed is correct for 11 neurons, beating a 10/15 majority-class baseline. Detected “failures” are explained as three mechanistic failure modes rather than violations of the theory: early collapse before the behavioral trigger, insufficient depth for effects to propagate, or normalization layers that cap effective influence. The work shows that influential controller neurons tend to write off the readout axis and thus have near-zero first-order gradients, meaning gradient attribution underestimates them. A forward-only, contrastive screen built on the control window framework successfully recovers such neurons. In analyzing refusal behaviors, the authors find that successful interventions are typed (qualitatively distinct) rather than purely scalar: some neurons enable coherent bypass (fluent, on-task text with no actionable content), while true actionable bypass appears only for a minority of audited Llama refusal pivots and only at later rollout steps.

Conclusion: Single-neuron steering in aligned language models is best understood as a budgeted and typed form of control, not as an arbitrary scalar knob. The proposed budget-normalized control window theory predicts when a neuron can coherently control a behavior without collapsing the model, explains why standard gradient-based attribution can miss key controller neurons, and provides a principled, forward-pass-based screening method to identify them. This reframes neuron-level interventions as a systematic audit tool with clear limits (coherence budgets, collapse ceilings, and typed intervention outcomes), rather than as isolated anecdotes of “magic neurons.”

Abstract: Aligned language models gate behaviors such as refusal and language routing through sparse feed forward neurons, yet no theory predicts when a single neuron intervention controls a behavior coherently rather than collapsing the output. We develop a budget normalized control window framework for single neuron steering. A dose along one write direction reduces to one control coordinate: the alignment between the residual stream and the write, driven along a universal saturation curve in units of a coherence budget set by the residual norm divided by the write norm. Coherent control exists when a behavior trigger lies below the collapse ceiling. The same coordinate governs benign mode switches and refusal; the ceiling follows from weights and one generic forward pass, while triggers are measured at rollout. On fifteen held out neurons, the predicted ceiling has mean absolute error 0.14, about 0.07 in bulk layers, and the committed open or closed verdict holds on eleven against a ten of fifteen majority baseline. Closed cases expose three failure modes rather than violations: collapse before trigger, too little depth to propagate, or a normalization that caps how far one neuron can push. The law explains why local gradient attribution anti predicts control: true controllers write off the readout axis and carry a near zero first order gradient. A forward only contrastive screen made precise by the window recovers controllers that attribution misses. On refusal, the hardest case, intervention success is typed, not scalar: coherent bypass and strict actionable reach separate, so a neuron can flip refusal in fluent, on task text with no actionable content, and genuine actionable reach appears only for three of six audited Llama pivots and only at later rollout horizons. Single neuron steering is therefore a budgeted, typed audit of controllability rather than a fixed dose anecdote.

</details>


### [33] [AtomMem: Building Simple and Effective Memory System for LLM Agents via Atomic Facts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19847)
*Yanyu Yao,Shangze Li,Zhi Zheng,Hui Zheng,Qi Liu,Tong Xu,Enhong Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: AtomMem is a long-term memory system for LLMs that stores compact, high-value atomic facts and organizes them into hierarchical events and temporal user profiles to support multi-session reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are limited by fixed context windows and cannot naturally accumulate or reuse information across many sessions. Existing memory-augmented approaches either use coarse, redundant memory units or unstable, unconstrained updates that hurt reliability and efficiency. Method: The authors design AtomMem, which first uses a Fact Executor component to extract high-value atomic facts from long-form interactions. These facts are used as efficient memory units and are then structured into hierarchical event representations and temporal profiles that model episodic context and evolving user attributes. For retrieval, AtomMem activates an associative memory graph that links related pieces of information, enabling coherent recall of fragmented memories during reasoning.

Result: On the LoCoMo benchmark for long-term conversation and memory, AtomMem achieves state-of-the-art performance across multiple reasoning tasks, demonstrating better accuracy and efficiency compared to prior long-term memory systems for LLMs.

Conclusion: AtomMem provides a scalable, economically efficient long-term memory architecture for LLM agents. By focusing on atomic fact extraction, hierarchical event and temporal modeling, and graph-based associative retrieval, it enables more reliable, context-rich reasoning in multi-session, personalized settings.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) demonstrate strong reasoning and generation abilities, but their fixed context windows limit long-term information accumulation and reuse across multi-session interactions. Existing memory-augmented systems often construct memory in a coarse and unstable manner, relying on inefficient memory representations or unstable unconstrained updates. To address these challenges, we propose AtomMem, a long-term memory system designed for value-dense storage and stable memory evolution. AtomMem introduces a Fact Executor, which selectively extracts high value atomic facts from long form interactions to serve as highly efficient memory representations. Subsequently, AtomMem organizes these facts into hierarchical event structures and temporal profiles, capturing coherent episodic contexts and tracking dynamically evolving user attributes over time. During retrieval, the system activates an associative memory graph to connect fragmented memories. Experiments on the LoCoMo benchmark confirm that AtomMem achieves state-of-the-art performance across various reasoning tasks, offering a scalable and economically viable solution for deploying intelligent personalized agents.

### [35] [Large Language Models Do Not Always Need Readable Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19857)
*Jiayi Zhu,Haoxuan Peng,Junxi Wang,Liang Ke,Chen Zhang,Linfeng Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces BabelTele, a compact, model-centric text representation that is hard for humans to read but still understandable by LLMs, enabling shorter prompts with nearly preserved meaning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most LLM interactions use human-readable natural language even when the consumer is another model, which is inefficient in context length and may not be necessary. The authors want to know if we can create text formats optimized for models, not humans, that are much denser yet still semantically recoverable by LLMs.

Method: They define BabelTele as a family of non-standard, compressed textual encodings optimized for LLMs, then empirically probe its properties. They evaluate it using: (1) readability diagnostics and human questionnaires to confirm it is hard for humans; (2) likelihood and internal metrics from LLMs to see if models find it plausible; and (3) downstream task evaluations (including cross-model transfer, agent memory, and multi-agent communication) to measure how well semantics are preserved and tasks are performed using these encodings. They vary the compression rate and compressor/reader model pairs.

Result: BabelTele can strongly diverge from ordinary language yet still preserve core semantics for instruction-tuned LLMs. It achieves very high information density: up to 99.5% semantic fidelity even when the token length is reduced to 27.9% of the original. In many settings it reduces context overhead while retaining reliable downstream task performance, though performance depends on which model does the compression and which model reads it, as well as on the task.

Conclusion: Human readability, similarity to natural language, and an LLM’s ability to recover meaning are partially independent. It is possible to design model-native text representations like BabelTele that are much more compact than natural language yet semantically robust for LLMs. This opens a direction toward communication protocols and memory formats optimized for models rather than humans, with potential benefits for context efficiency and multi-agent systems, though their effectiveness is contingent on specific model and task configurations.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are commonly prompted and interfaced with human-readable natural language, even when the intended reader is another model. This paper investigates whether semantic information can be encoded in compact, non-standard textual forms that sacrifice human readability while remaining recoverable by LLMs. We refer to this class of model-centric textual representations as BabelTele, approached here not as a fixed protocol but as an empirical probe into LLMs' capacity to generate and interpret such representations. Through readability diagnostics, model likelihood measures, human questionnaires, and downstream task evaluations, we find that BabelTele can substantially depart from ordinary natural language while preserving core semantics for instruction-tuned LLMs. As a task-agnostic representational paradigm, BabelTele demonstrates high information density, maintaining 99.5% semantic fidelity even when the text volume is condensed to 27.9% of its original length. We further evaluate its semantic robustness in cross-model transfer, agent memory, and multi-agent communication. Results suggest that BabelTele can reduce context overhead while generally maintaining reliable downstream performance, although its effectiveness depends on the compressor-reader pair and task setting. These findings indicate that human readability, natural-language typicality, and model-side semantic recoverability can be partially decoupled, opening a path toward model-native representations in future exploration of LLM systems.

### [36] [The Almost Intelligent Revolution: Options for Scaling Up Deliberation and Empowering People with AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19864)

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The chapter examines how Large Language Models (LLMs) can both support and threaten democratic deliberation, focusing on language variation, bias, and inclusivity, and proposes directions to use LLMs to scale and democratise participation while avoiding the reinforcement of linguistic inequalities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs become more prominent in public discourse, they influence who can participate and how in democratic deliberation. Existing safety work like red teaming covers narrow risks but does not adequately address structural linguistic inequalities, biases, and sycophancy that can marginalise certain groups. The authors are motivated to understand and design LLM-supported deliberation that expands, rather than restricts or distorts, inclusive democratic engagement.

Method: Conceptual and empirical analysis is grounded in Systemic-Functional Linguistics to study variation in language users (e.g., across socio-demographic groups) and language use (e.g., communicative functions). The chapter reviews and presents AI-driven deliberation studies, using them as case material to assess how LLM-based tools can scaffold argumentation, improve access, and reshape the linguistic norms that govern deliberative spaces.

Result: The chapter finds that LLMs can help scaffold argumentation, broaden access, and potentially reduce the dominance of prestigious, exclusionary registers in deliberation. At the same time, the chapter shows that LLMs inherit and can reproduce linguistic biases and sycophantic behaviors, which can subtly reinforce inequalities and distort deliberative quality if left unchecked. It identifies both the promise and current limitations of deploying LLMs for large-scale, democratic deliberation.

Conclusion: LLMs have substantial potential to scale and democratise deliberation by supporting more inclusive, linguistically diverse participation, but only if designed and governed carefully. The chapter warns against both hype and undue pessimism, and calls for future research focused on embedding ethical safeguards, addressing linguistic bias and inequality, and systematically evaluating AI-assisted deliberation so its democratic benefits can be realised without deepening existing exclusions.

Abstract: The increasing prominence of Large Language Models (LLMs) in public discourse presents both opportunities and challenges for democratic deliberation. While red teaming strategies help mitigate specific risks, broader concerns persist regarding linguistic constraints, biases, and the sycophantic tendencies of LLMs. This chapter explores how LLMs can be used to significantly scale up and democratise deliberation, particularly in fostering inclusivity and empowering traditionally marginalised groups. Drawing on concepts from Systemic-Functional Linguistics, the chapter examines how variations across language users (for example, with respect to socio-demographic groups) and across language use (for example, with respect to communicative functions) shape participation in AI-supported deliberation. The chapter presents AI-driven deliberation studies and assesses their potential to scaffold argumentation, enhance access, and reduce the influence of exclusionary linguistic norms and biases which are embedded in prestigious registers. At the same time, the chapter cautions against both overclaiming, which leads to unrealistic expectations, and underclaiming, which risks missed opportunities for AI-assisted engagement. The chapter concludes by identifying future research directions to maximise the democratic potential of AI-assisted participation while embedding ethical safeguards to counteract the reproduction of linguistic inequalities.

</details>


### [37] [REDACT: A Systematically Controlled Multilingual Benchmark for Personal Information Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19881)
*Guneesh Vats,Anubha Agrawal,Shikha Singhal,Ajita Dash,Praison Selvaraj,Vidhan Jhawar,Ranga Prasad Chenna,Bharadwaj Y M G*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces REDACT, a large, systematically controlled multilingual benchmark for evaluating PII detection systems, revealing nuanced, architecture-dependent weaknesses that aggregate F1 scores hide, especially on the most sensitive data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing PII detection benchmarks are limited: they cover few entity types, are generated under ad hoc and poorly documented conditions, and do not clarify which textual surface conditions (like format, domain, code-switching, or context) cause failures. Moreover, current evaluations mostly rely on aggregate or per-entity-type F1 scores, which obscure how detectors behave on the most sensitive information or under particular disclosure forms and conditions. This creates a gap for practitioners and researchers who need reliable, fine-grained evaluation of PII detection (e.g., for privacy compliance and safety in multilingual settings).

Method: The authors construct REDACT, a benchmark of 13,427 records containing 324,078 annotated entities across 51 PII types in 25 languages and 9 scripts. They control data generation using a strength-2 covering-array sampler over nine axes: domain, format, difficulty, length, entity density, code-switching presence, language, adjacency of entities, and co-occurrence of entity types. Each entity is annotated with three metadata fields: disclosure status, disclosure form (e.g., verbatim vs. obfuscated), and a GDPR-aligned sensitivity tier. They then evaluate five PII detectors—Presidio (rule-based), GLiNER, OpenAI Privacy Filter, GPT-4.1, and Claude Sonnet 4.6—on a locked, language-stratified subset of 1,000 records. Beyond standard F1, they analyze performance stratified by sensitivity tier, disclosure form, and other axes, and they further use a three-model, reference-free LLM-as-judge setup to assess difficulty, particularly of sensitivity-tier assignment.

Result: REDACT provides a large, diverse, and systematically controlled dataset with 4,127 distinct surface-form patterns for PII, spanning many languages and scripts. Evaluation results show that simple aggregate F1 scores conceal important patterns: the rule-based detector (Presidio) performs especially poorly on the highest-stakes categories, including HIGH-sensitivity PII and non-verbatim disclosures, attaining recall as low as 0.07 on the HIGH tier. In contrast, LLM-based detectors (OpenAI Privacy Filter, GPT-4.1, Claude Sonnet 4.6) and GLiNER are more robust, with the HIGH-sensitivity tier often being their best-performing slice. The LLM-as-judge analysis agrees that assigning correct sensitivity tiers is the most challenging aspect of the task.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that REDACT fills a critical gap in PII detection evaluation by offering a multilingual, systematically varied, and richly annotated benchmark that exposes architecture-dependent failure modes not visible in aggregate metrics. Rule-based systems are shown to be unreliable on the most sensitive and non-verbatim PII, whereas LLM-based detectors handle these difficult cases more effectively. Sensitivity-tier classification remains a hard, open challenge. By releasing the benchmark, schema, prompts, and stratified evaluation harness, the authors aim to standardize and improve the rigor of PII detection research and practice, especially for high-stakes and multilingual privacy applications.

Abstract: Benchmark infrastructure for personally identifiable information (PII) detection remains limited: existing corpora cover few entity types, use ad hoc generation conditions, and do not show which surface conditions cause detector failures. We present REDACT, a systematically controlled multilingual PII benchmark with 13,427 records, 324,078 entity annotations, 51 entity types, 4,127 surface-form patterns, and 25 languages across 9 scripts. A strength-2 covering-array sampler controls nine generation axes: domain, format, difficulty, length, density, code-switching, language, adjacency, and co-occurrence. Three entity-level metadata fields (disclosure status, disclosure form, and a GDPR-aligned sensitivity tier) enable stratified evaluation beyond aggregate or per-type F1. From the full benchmark, we evaluate five detectors (Presidio, GLiNER, the OpenAI Privacy Filter, GPT-4.1, and Claude Sonnet 4.6) on a locked, language-stratified sample of 1,000 records. Aggregate F1 masks an architecture-dependent failure structure: the rule-based detector performs poorly on the highest-stakes data, including HIGH-sensitivity categories (recall 0.07) and non-verbatim disclosure forms, while the LLM detectors remain more robust, with the HIGH tier as their strongest sensitivity slice. A three-model reference-free LLM-as-judge assessment corroborates that sensitivity-tier assignment is the task's hardest axis. We release the benchmark, schema, prompts, and stratified evaluation harness.

### [38] [Light-weight Pronunciation Assessment via Discrete Speech Token Surprisal](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19910)
*Syeda Faiza Ahmed Sara,Shammur Absar Chowdhury*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose an automated pronunciation scoring framework trained only on native speech, avoiding expensive non-native data, and achieve performance close to supervised systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing pronunciation assessment models depend on labeled learner errors or non-native speech corpora, which are expensive and difficult to collect. There is a need for a method that can be trained only on abundant native speech while still effectively detecting learner pronunciation errors.

Method: They discretize learner speech using a self-supervised learning (SSL) encoder and a K-means codebook to obtain acoustic tokens. A token-level language model trained on sequences from native speakers is used to compute surprisal scores, where higher surprisal corresponds to more phonotactically unlikely patterns. They further introduce a transcript-guided Text2DUnit--DTW module that predicts expected native token sequences from the reference text and aligns them to the actual acoustic tokens via dynamic time warping (DTW), deriving error-sensitive alignment features. Finally, they fuse surprisal and alignment-based features using a simple regression model to predict pronunciation scores.

Result: On the SpeechOcean762 dataset, their framework improves Pearson correlation coefficient (PCC) from 0.60 (without transcript guidance) to 0.66 when using the transcript-guided module, reaching performance close to fully supervised baselines. Cross-dataset tests on L2-ARCTIC also show consistent performance gains, indicating good generalization.

Conclusion: Training pronunciation assessment purely on native speech with discrete token LMs and transcript-guided alignment is feasible and competitive with supervised approaches requiring non-native data. The combination of surprisal-based phonotactic deviation measures and alignment-derived error features yields a lightweight yet effective scoring system that generalizes across datasets.

Abstract: Training automated pronunciation assessment often relies on labeled learner errors or non-native corpora that are costly to collect. We propose a lightweight framework trained only on native speech resources, operating unsupervised or lightly calibrated with a small set of scored utterances. At inference, learner speech is discretized with an SSL encoder and a K-means codebook. A token language model trained on native sequences computes surprisal where higher surprisal indicates phonotactic deviation. We add a transcript-guided Text2DUnit--DTW module that predicts native token sequences from reference text and aligns them to acoustic tokens to derive error-sensitive features. Surprisal and alignment features are fused via simple regression. On SpeechOcean762, PCC improves from 0.60 to 0.66 with transcript guidance, near supervised baselines. Cross-dataset evaluation on L2-ARCTIC shows consistent gains.

### [39] [GEMS: Geometric Constraints Enable Multi-Semantic Superposition in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19946)
*Yu Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why naive multi-directional activation steering causes model collapse and proposes GEMS, a training-free, geometry-aware method that enables stable, simultaneous steering along multiple semantic directions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Activation steering has emerged as a powerful way to control language model behavior at inference time by adding semantic directions to hidden states. However, existing approaches are largely limited to single-direction interventions. When practitioners try to combine several semantic directions (e.g., style, safety, task bias) by simple addition, model performance catastrophically degrades (“collapse”). There is a need to understand the mechanisms behind this collapse and to design a principled, training-free method that supports stable multi-directional steering without retraining the model.

Method: The authors first decompose collapse into two independent factors: (1) distributional deviation caused by the uncontrolled growth of perturbation norms across layers, which pushes activations out of the training distribution, and (2) directional interference arising when non-orthogonal steering vectors are superposed and damp each other. Based on this analysis, they introduce GEMS, a method that enforces geometric constraints on the steering signals. For distributional deviation, GEMS uses norm-preserving weighted superposition and injects signals through carefully chosen attention pathways rather than indiscriminately across the network. For directional interference, GEMS performs on-the-fly orthogonalization of multiple steering directions so they do not destructively interfere. The approach is entirely training-free and operates at inference time only. The authors verify the effect of each design choice via component ablations and layer-wise probes.

Result: On GSM8K, adding three non-mathematical steering directions with naive, unconstrained addition collapses model accuracy from 92% to 4%. Using GEMS, the model maintains 98% accuracy, essentially preserving or even slightly improving performance relative to the baseline. On Wikitext-2 language modeling, the same multi-directional injection with GEMS leads to only a 2.2% increase in perplexity, demonstrating minimal degradation of core modeling ability. Qualitative analyses show that the intended steering effects hold across model sizes from 3B to 31B parameters. Probing experiments reveal that orthogonalized steering signals survive passage through the feedforward network pathway and manifest at the output distribution with distinct semantic effects. Ablation studies confirm each geometric constraint’s causal contribution to performance stability.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that multi-directional activation steering fails primarily due to two geometric issues—distributional deviation and directional interference—that can be treated separately. By enforcing norm control, targeted pathways, and real-time orthogonalization, GEMS enables stable, training-free, simultaneous steering along multiple semantic directions while preserving task performance and language modeling quality. This establishes practical design principles for safe, multi-attribute activation interventions and shows that such geometric control generalizes across model scales.

Abstract: Activation steering controls model behavior by modifying intermediate hidden states at inference time without retraining. Existing methods handle only single-direction injection; when multiple semantic directions are superposed without constraints, the model collapses. We show that this collapse decomposes into two independently acting sources: distributional deviation, where additive perturbations accumulate in norm across layers and drive activations outside the training distribution, and directional interference, where non-orthogonal semantic vectors mutually dampen when superposed. These two sources define the design constraints that any training-free multi-directional intervention must address. As one instantiation of these principles, we propose GEMS, a training-free method that maps each source to a corresponding geometric constraint: norm-preserving weighted superposition and targeted attention-pathway injection for distributional deviation, and real-time orthogonalization for directional interference. On GSM8K, injecting three concurrent non-mathematical directions preserves accuracy at 98% (baseline 92%), while unconstrained addition collapses to 4%; on Wikitext-2, the same injection incurs only 2.2% PPL increase. Component ablation isolates the causal role of each constraint, and layer-level probes confirm that orthogonalized signals survive the FFN pathway and reach the output distribution with semantic specificity. Qualitative steering effects transfer across architectures from 3B to 31B.

</details>

*Sunghee Ahn,Guijin Son,Youngjae Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces STAGE, a pipeline that generates high-quality text-to-JSON training data from spreadsheets using LLMs, markedly improving information extraction performance.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Creating reliable, scalable training data for mapping text to JSON is difficult, yet essential for automating access to this information.

They also construct STAGE-Eval, a benchmark of 851 test examples built using the same source-grounded principles.

For example, when training Qwen3-4B for text-to-JSON extraction, the exact match metric improves from 31.37% to 74.27%, and value-level accuracy improves from 45.46% to 90.69%.

Conclusion: Source-grounded data generation from spreadsheets via STAGE yields substantially higher-quality text-to-JSON training data than existing methods, enabling smaller models like Qwen3-4B to achieve much stronger performance on structured information extraction from long, unstructured documents.

Abstract: From financial filings to clinical records, legacy industries rely heavily on long, unstructured documents to store high-value information. Reliably extracting this information into structured, machine-readable representations is a key prerequisite to making the contents accessible to automated systems. JSON is a natural target for such structured extraction, yet constructing reliable and scalable text-to-JSON training data remains challenging. To address this gap, we propose STAGE (Spreadsheet-grounded Text-to-JSON Artifact GEneration), a source-grounded data generation pipeline that constructs reports and JSON schema by using LLMs for scalable synthesis while validating ground-truth values against the underlying spreadsheet. Evaluations on STAGE-Eval, our source-grounded benchmark with an 851-example test set, show that STAGE produces stronger training data than existing approaches. This improves Qwen3-4B exact match from 31.37% to 74.27% and value accuracy from 45.46% to 90.69%.

Method: The authors pretrain a monolingual RoBERTa-base encoder (125M parameters) from scratch on a 45 GB curated subset of the Sepahr-Danesh corpus (~7–8B tokens). The tokenizer ablation shows BPE slightly reduces subword fragmentation compared to WordPiece at the same vocabulary size.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that semantically curated large-scale pretraining and a tailored BPE tokenizer substantially improve Persian language modeling, especially on comprehension-oriented tasks like extractive QA and NLI. IHUBERT establishes strong or state-of-the-art baselines on multiple Persian NLU benchmarks, while revealing relation extraction as a remaining challenge. Abstract: Persian pretrained language models (PLMs) are still limited by the scarcity of large-scale, high-quality pretraining corpora and by insufficient evaluation beyond standard classification and NER tasks. To improve corpus quality and reduce redundancy, we employ a multi-stage preprocessing pipeline that includes normalization, exact and near-duplicate removal, anonymization, and vector-database-based semantic deduplication for distribution balancing control across domains and registers. We additionally train a 139k-vocabulary BPE tokenizer on the full pretraining corpus to better capture Persian morphology and orthographic variation. IHUBERT is evaluated on seven Persian NLU benchmarks covering NER, sentiment analysis, topic classification, NLI, extractive question answering, and relation extraction, using task-standard metrics (entity-level F1, Macro-F1, EM/F1). IHUBERT achieves its strongest gains on extractive QA, ranking first on both PQuAD (F1 88.3542) and ParsiNLU-RC (F1 49.0987), and attains the best result on FarsTail (Macro-F1 0.8350). ### [42] [Self-Preference Is Weak or Absent in Verifiable Instruction-Following Revision: A Four-Model Test Under Genuine Authorship](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20093)
*William Guey,Pierrick Bougault*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether large language models show a self-preference bias when asked to judge corrections to their own outputs, using a deterministic verifier to define valid fixes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used to review and revise text, often their own, and past work found that they prefer their own generations when acting as judges. This raises a concern: do LLMs unfairly resist valid corrections to their own writing, which would undermine their usefulness in self-editing and collaborative workflows?

Method: The authors construct a controlled revision task using the IFEval benchmark. A model first writes a draft that violates an instruction. The official IFEval checker is then used to deterministically verify that the draft indeed violates a constraint and that a proposed edit would fix it. The model is then asked to accept or reject this edit under two roles: (1) as the original in-context author of the draft and (2) as a fresh model that sees the draft without author context. They run this procedure across four mid-tier model families, yielding 85 author-versus-fresh comparisons. They also explore a self-skepticism prompting hint in a smaller pilot.

Result: Across all models and comparisons, the authors find no statistically detectable self-preference: models acting as authors reject verified-good corrections at essentially the same rate as fresh models judging the same drafts, with an estimated gap of -5.1 percentage points and a 95% confidence interval of [-12.9, +2.7]. The self-skepticism hint effect observed in a small pilot fails to replicate at scale. Qualitatively, when authors do reject a verified-good fix, 97% of their stated reasons emphasize flaw-detection rather than preference for their own wording.

Conclusion: Within the limits of their experimental design and sample size, the authors find no evidence that LLMs resist valid corrections to their own text more than they resist corrections to others’ text. The dominant pattern in stated justifications is error-focused reasoning, not self-preferential bias. However, the study cannot rule out smaller effects below roughly 13 percentage points, leaving open the possibility of more subtle self-preference in other settings or at smaller magnitudes.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly review and revise text, including their own. A documented self-preference bias (models favoring their own generations when acting as judges) raises the question of whether models also resist valid corrections to their own writing. We test this in a setting where "valid" is decided not by another model but by a deterministic verifier: instruction-following revision on IFEval. A model writes a draft; the official IFEval checker confirms the draft violates a constraint and that a candidate edit fixes it; the model then accepts or rejects that edit either as the genuine in-context author or as a fresh model that sees the draft neutrally. Across four mid-tier model families and 85 author-versus-fresh comparisons, we find no detectable self-preference: authors reject verified-good fixes to their own drafts at essentially the same rate as fresh models judging the same drafts (gap -5.1 pp, 95% CI [-12.9, +2.7]). A self-skepticism hint from a smaller pilot did not replicate at scale. The one robust observation is qualitative: when authors do reject a verified-good fix, 97% of their stated reasons are flaw-catching rather than preference, that is, about the character of rejections, not an elevated rate. Effects smaller than ~13 pp cannot be excluded at this sample size.

</details>


### [43] [HydraHead: From Head-Level Functional Heterogeneity to Specialized Attention Hybridization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20097)
*Zhentao Tan,Wei Chen,Jingyi Shen,Yao Liu,Xu Shen,Yue Wu,Jieping Ye*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: HydraHead proposes a head-level hybrid attention mechanism that selectively applies full attention to interpretability-identified critical heads while using linear attention elsewhere, achieving strong long-context performance with reduced quadratic cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Attention’s quadratic complexity limits efficient long-context processing. Existing hybrid attention models largely combine linear and full attention at the layer level, but prior work shows FA and LA are hard to integrate and that this design space is underexplored. The authors are motivated to find a more principled granularity for hybridization that preserves retrieval-critical behaviors while gaining scalability.

Method: Through interpretability analysis, the authors find layers form functionally similar blocks while heads within a layer specialize in distinct functions, suggesting head-level heterogeneity. They design HydraHead, which hybridizes full and linear attention along the head axis. HydraHead uses: (1) an interpretability-driven strategy to identify retrieval-critical heads and keep full attention only for these; (2) a scale-normalized fusion module to reconcile distributional differences between FA and LA head outputs; and (3) a three-stage transfer pipeline with parameter reuse and distillation to efficiently train high-performance hybrids with low overhead.

Result: Under a unified training setup, HydraHead surpasses other hybrid attention designs on long-context benchmarks while preserving strong general reasoning ability. With interpretability-driven head selection, it can match the long-context performance of a 3:1 layer-wise hybrid using a more aggressive 7:1 LA-to-FA ratio. Trained on only 15B tokens, HydraHead yields more than 69% improvement over a baseline model at 512K context length and approaches the performance of Qwen3.5, a state-of-the-art model of similar size with a native 256K context window.

Conclusion: Head-level hybridization is a powerful and scalable way to combine full and linear attention. By targeting only retrieval-critical heads for full attention and carefully fusing heterogeneous head outputs, HydraHead reduces attention cost while maintaining or improving long-context and general reasoning performance. The results indicate substantial scaling potential in this head-axis hybridization strategy compared with conventional layer-wise hybrids.

Abstract: The quadratic complexity of attention poses a critical bottleneck for long-context processing, spurring interest in hybrid attention designs. Most open-source hybrid models adopt a layer-wise strategy. Yet, prior work has noted the inherent difficulty of integrating Linear Attention (LA) with Full Attention (FA), suggesting that the design space of attention hybridization remains underexplored. To probe this space, we conduct interpretability analysis and observe that layers exhibit block-wise functional similarity, while individual heads within the same layer display distinct functional specialization despite sharing input features. This head-level heterogeneity suggests that the head dimension provides a natural and principled granularity for fusing heterogeneous attention signals. Building on this insight, we introduce HydraHead, a novel architecture that hybridizes FA and LA along the head axis. HydraHead features two key innovations: (1) an interpretability-driven selection strategy that identifies retrieval-critical heads and preserves FA only for them, and (2) a scale-normalized fusion module that reconciles the distributional gap between FA and LA head outputs. By leveraging a three-stage transfer pipeline with parameter reuse and distillation, we achieve high-performance hybrid models with minimal training overhead. Under a unified training setup, HydraHead outperforms other hybrid designs in long-context tasks while maintaining strong general reasoning. With interpretability-driven head selection, it matches a 3:1 layer-wise hybrid's long-context performance at a 7:1 LA-to-FA ratio. Crucially, trained on only 15B tokens, HydraHead achieves over 69% improvement over the baseline at 512K context length, approaching Qwen3.5, a leading model of comparable size with a native context length of 256K. This highlights the significant scaling potential of head-level hybridization.
