### [44] [When Does Streaming Tool Use Help? Characterizing Tool-Intent Stabilization in Streaming Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20113)
*Elroy Galbraith*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies when and how much Streaming Retrieval-Augmented Generation (Streaming RAG) can actually hide tool latency by issuing retrieval early, before the user finishes their query.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work on Streaming RAG shows aggregate latency reductions, but these are reported as averages across queries. In reality, speculative retrieval can only help when the model can infer the correct tool query before the user finishes speaking or typing. The authors aim to isolate this intrinsic, query-level property and quantify how often and how early queries become determinate enough to support correct retrieval, giving principled limits on achievable latency hiding and guidance for when to trigger speculation.

Method: They introduce and measure "tool-intent stabilization": the point in the incremental input stream at which a speculative retrieval converges to the same answer-bearing result as using the full query. Using the CRAG benchmark (1371 validation questions), they: (i) empirically estimate the distribution of stabilization points over queries, (ii) derive a model-agnostic analytical upper bound H on the fraction of tool latency that can be overlapped with remaining user input, as a function of tool latency L and input cadence δ (words per second), (iii) build and test an actual streaming RAG pipeline to compare realized latency savings with the theoretical bound, and (iv) analyze which query properties (e.g., question type, evidence characteristics) correlate with early or late stabilization. They distinguish a favorable subset of questions where gold evidence is verbatim in the corpus and recoverable by BM25, and treat the rest with a grounding-free top-1 settling criterion.

Result: On CRAG with realistic parameters (tool latency L=600 ms, user input rate δ=3 words/s, and stabilization threshold θ=0.8), they find that 73.9% of all queries can hide a substantial portion of tool latency via streaming. For the favorable 21.3% of questions where the gold evidence is verbatim-present and BM25 can retrieve it, 95.2% are streamable under their criteria. On this favorable slice, the sufficiency-based stabilization fraction φ_suf lies tightly in [0.26, 0.281] under exact vs. relaxed grounding, indicating that evidence-stable retrieval occurs after only about a quarter of the query has been seen. Statistical analysis shows that question type significantly predicts early vs. late stabilization (Kruskal-Wallis p=0.017, epsilon^2=0.04), enabling a coarse categorization of which queries are most amenable to speculative retrieval. Their implemented streaming pipeline achieves latency savings matching or exceeding the theoretical bound H, validating the analytical model.

Conclusion: Streaming RAG’s benefits are largely governed by how quickly tool intent stabilizes within a query, a property that varies substantially across questions but can be characterized without training or GPUs. Under realistic latency and typing/speaking rates, most queries in CRAG allow meaningful hiding of tool latency, especially when the answer evidence is verbatim-retrievable. The paper provides: (1) a formal notion and measurement protocol for tool-intent stabilization, (2) a simple bound on attainable latency hiding as a function of system parameters, (3) empirical confirmation from a working streaming pipeline, and (4) evidence that coarse query-type features can predict early vs. late stabilization, informing when it is worthwhile to trigger speculative retrieval in practice.

Abstract: Streaming Retrieval-Augmented Generation (Streaming RAG) reduces user-perceived latency by issuing tool queries in parallel with ongoing user input, before the utterance is complete. Reported gains are aggregate, yet the mechanism's benefit is fundamentally query-intrinsic: speculation can only help when the correct tool query becomes determinable before the user stops speaking or typing. We isolate and measure this property -- tool-intent stabilization, the point in the input stream at which a speculative query's retrieval converges to the answer-bearing result. On the CRAG benchmark (1371 validation questions) we (i) measure the distribution of stabilization, (ii) derive a model-agnostic bound H on the portion of tool latency that can be hidden behind the user's remaining input, as a function of tool latency L and input cadence δ, (iii) validate against a working streaming pipeline that realized savings meet or exceed this bound, and (iv) identify which query properties predict early versus late stabilization. The study requires no model training and runs on commodity CPU hardware. We find that at a realistic operating point (L=600ms, δ=3w/s, θ=0.8), 73.9% of queries across the full benchmark admit substantial latency hiding -- a blended figure that mixes sufficiency stabilization on the 21.3% of questions where gold evidence is verbatim-present and BM25-retrievable (95.2% streamable on this favorable slice) with a grounding-free top-1-settling fallback on the remainder. On the favorable slice, φ_suf is bracketed to [0.26, 0.281] by exact and relaxed grounding -- both early. Question type produces a significant but coarse early/late split (Kruskal-Wallis p=0.017, epsilon^2=0.04), directly informing when a learned speculative trigger is worth its cost.

### [45] [From Texts to Scores: Tracing the Emergence of Essay Quality Representations in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20152)
*Jiaxu Zuo,Mu You,Kaixin Lan,Tao Fang,Yujia Huo,Henghua Shen,Lidia S. Chao,Derek F. Wong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how large language models internally represent essay quality, showing that this information is linearly decodable, robust across prompts and languages, and associated with specific "essay scoring" neurons.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs have significantly improved automated essay scoring performance, it is unclear how they internally encode essay quality, raising concerns about interpretability, reliability, and transferability across prompts and datasets.

Method: The authors analyze hidden representations of eight LLMs on three essay datasets (two English, one Portuguese) using linear and nonlinear probing classifiers, cross-prompt generalization experiments, dimensionality reduction, and neuron-level analyses, including targeted interventions on neurons correlated with essay scores.

Result: They find that essay quality information is linearly accessible in LLM representations, emerges gradually across layers, is robust to different prompting strategies, and partially transfers across prompts and scoring rubrics. Nonlinear probes yield only small, inconsistent gains over linear probes. They also identify individual neurons whose activations correlate strongly with essay scores, whose manipulation affects scoring behavior, and whose layer-wise distribution shifts toward deeper layers for longer essays.

Conclusion: LLMs encode structured, largely linearly decodable representations of essay quality, with identifiable neurons contributing to scoring decisions and a length-dependent layer usage pattern. These findings enhance the interpretability of LLM-based automated essay scoring and suggest directions for more transparent and controllable AES systems.

Abstract: Recent advances in Large Language Models (LLMs) have substantially transformed Automated Essay Scoring (AES), yet the internal mechanisms underlying LLM-based scoring remain poorly understood. In this work, we systematically analyze the hidden representations of eight LLMs across two English essay datasets (ASAP++, CSEE) and one Portuguese dataset (ENEM). Using linear probing, cross-prompt generalization, dimensionality reduction, and neuron-level analyses, we find consistent evidence that essay quality information is encoded in a linearly accessible form within LLM representations. These representations emerge progressively across layers, remain robust across prompting strategies, and partially transfer across essay prompts despite differences in scoring rubrics. In addition, nonlinear probes provide only marginal and inconsistent improvements over linear probes, suggesting that most essay quality information is already linearly decodable. We further identify individual ``essay scoring neurons'' whose activations strongly correlate with essay scores and whose behavior is sensitive to targeted intervention. Moreover, the layer-wise distribution of these neurons systematically shifts with essay length, with longer essays relying more heavily on deeper layers. Overall, our findings provide evidence that LLMs encode structured representations related to essay quality and offer new insights into the interpretability of LLM-based AES systems.

</details>

*Aueaphum Aueawatthanaphisut*

TL;DR: MedRLM is a recursive, multimodal clinical decision support framework that reasons over long, heterogeneous patient data using coordinated agents and a clinical evidence graph to provide auditable, workflow-aware support from screening to referral.

  <summary>Details</summary>

A Clinical Evidence Graph Memory links patient observations with retrieved evidence, standardized concepts, sensor biomarkers, and referral criteria. A sensor-guided recursive triggering mechanism prompts deeper reasoning when abnormal patterns are detected, and uncertainty-gated refinement flags high-risk or low-confidence cases for further review. It claims that MedRLM can support long-context multimodal clinical reasoning, sensor-informed screening, and community-to-tertiary referral support, with auditable evidence tracing via the Clinical Evidence Graph Memory. Specific performance metrics are not provided in the abstract.

By treating the patient case as an inspectable environment and coordinating specialized agents with an evidence graph and uncertainty-aware mechanisms, it aims to provide more robust, auditable clinical reasoning across heterogeneous, longitudinal data sources and real clinical workflows.

Abstract: Real-world clinical decision support requires reasoning over heterogeneous and longitudinal patient information rather than answering isolated medical questions. However, current medical large language models and retrieval-augmented generation systems often rely on single-step prompting or retrieval, which can be fragile when clinical evidence is distributed across long electronic health records, medical images, sensor streams, guidelines, and referral constraints. This paper proposes MedRLM, a Recursive Multimodal Health Intelligence framework for long-context clinical reasoning, sensor-guided screening, and community-to-tertiary referral support. Instead of compressing all patient information into one prompt, MedRLM treats the patient case as an external clinical environment that can be recursively inspected, decomposed, retrieved, verified, and synthesized. The framework coordinates specialized agents for clinical text, longitudinal EHR, medical imaging, physiological sensor signals, guideline retrieval, uncertainty auditing, and referral planning. It further introduces a Clinical Evidence Graph Memory to connect patient-specific observations with retrieved evidence, standardized definitions, sensor-derived biomarkers, and referral criteria. A sensor-guided recursive triggering mechanism activates deeper reasoning when abnormal physiological or behavioral patterns are detected, while uncertainty-gated refinement supports clinician review for high-risk or low-confidence cases. We also outline a real-data evaluation design using public and credentialed clinical datasets spanning EHR, radiology, ECG, ICU time series, and referral-proxy outcomes. MedRLM aims to move medical AI from static question answering toward auditable, multimodal, and workflow-aware clinical decision support.

### [47] [ReNikud: Audio-Supervised Hebrew Grapheme-to-Phoneme Conversion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20179)
*Maxim Melichov,Yakov Kolani,Morris Alper*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ReNikud, a new Hebrew grapheme-to-phoneme (G2P) system that leverages weakly supervised audio-derived phoneme labels and a character-aligned pseudo-vocalization architecture, achieving state-of-the-art performance on Hebrew G2P benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern Hebrew G2P is difficult because the abjad script omits most vowels, causing ambiguity. Existing pipelines either rely on scarce, formal diacritization data that does not capture spoken norms or use direct seq2seq IPA prediction that struggles in low-resource settings and ignores the natural character-phoneme alignment of abjad scripts. There is a need for a method that (a) captures real spoken pronunciation, (b) works with limited labeled data, and (c) exploits character-level alignment.

Method: ReNikud has two main components. First, it uses weak audio supervision: a phoneme-based ASR system is applied to thousands of hours of unlabeled Hebrew speech to produce phonemic pseudo-labels that approximate real spoken pronunciation without manual annotation. Second, it introduces a pseudo-vocalization G2P architecture that predicts an IPA phoneme sequence aligned to each input grapheme position, thereby embedding character-level alignment as an inductive bias, instead of generating an unconstrained global IPA sequence. The model is trained on these ASR-derived phoneme labels and evaluated on multiple benchmarks.

Result: On standard Hebrew G2P benchmarks and a newly proposed MILIM benchmark specifically targeting spoken Hebrew, ReNikud outperforms prior state-of-the-art systems, demonstrating better accuracy in predicting phonemic forms that reflect actual spoken usage. The gains show the effectiveness of both the audio-based weak supervision and the pseudo-vocalization architecture.

Conclusion: By combining large-scale weak supervision from unlabeled audio with a character-aligned pseudo-vocalization architecture, ReNikud delivers superior Hebrew G2P performance and better matches spoken language than previous approaches. The authors plan to release code and pretrained models, positioning ReNikud as a foundation for improved Hebrew TTS and related speech technologies, and illustrating a promising general strategy for G2P in abjad scripts.

Abstract: Grapheme-to-phoneme (G2P) conversion for Modern Hebrew is needed for applications like text-to-speech (TTS), but is challenging due to the language's abjad writing system, which leaves vowels largely unwritten, creating substantial ambiguity. Standard approaches first predict vowel diacritics (nikud) to produce International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA) transcriptions, but this is limited: vocalization data is scarce and laborious to produce, it does not specify features such as lexical stress, and it reflects formal grammatical rules rather than everyday spoken pronunciation. Direct sequence-to-sequence IPA prediction, meanwhile, struggles on limited data and fails to exploit the character-level alignment characteristic of abjads. Our method, ReNikud, overcomes these limitations with two key insights: (1) Weak audio supervision via a phoneme-based automatic speech recognition (ASR) pseudo-labeling pipeline on thousands of hours of unlabeled Hebrew audio, yielding phonemic transcriptions that reflect natural spoken norms without manual annotation. (2) A pseudo-vocalization architecture that predicts IPA phonemes at each character position, enforcing character-level alignment as an inductive bias. Results on existing Hebrew G2P benchmarks and the new targeted MILIM benchmark for spoken Hebrew show that ReNikud surpasses previous state-of-the-art methods. We will release our code and trained models to support further work on Hebrew TTS and speech technologies.

### [48] [Pitch Spelling Jazz Lead Sheets, Solo Transcriptions, Classical Piano and Monophonic Scores](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20198)
*Augustin Bouquillard,Florent Jacquemard*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an algorithm that, from MIDI-like note and bar data, automatically assigns note spellings, a key signature, and per-bar scales, using a two-stage optimization procedure (modal then tonal), and evaluates it on diverse jazz, traditional, and classical datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: When converting performance-like MIDI data into readable scores, pitches are given only as semitone numbers, not as musically meaningful note names (e.g., G♯ vs. A♭), key signatures, or local scales. Good musical notation matters for analysis, pedagogy, and cultural preservation, especially in large-scale automatic transcription projects (e.g., jazz solos). Existing approaches either are not optimized for minimizing accidentals or do not jointly consider local scales and global key signatures in a principled way. The authors aim to design an algorithm that produces high-quality, human-readable notation from raw pitch data that is suitable for building digital collections and for broader music-processing tasks.

Method: The method takes as input MIDI-like sequences with pitch classes (as semitone integers) and bar boundaries. It performs two linked optimization stages. In the first, "modal" stage, it assigns a most likely scale to each bar by minimizing the number of accidentals that would appear, formulated as a shortest-path problem over possible scale sequences across bars. In the second, "tonal" stage, it uses the inferred bar-level scales to infer a global key signature and concrete note spellings (e.g., choosing between enharmonic equivalents) that yield the most musically coherent notation across the whole piece. The method also introduces distance measures between common jazz scales, which are used in the optimization and can support musicological analysis.

Result: The algorithm was evaluated on several datasets: jazz lead sheets from the Real Book, transcriptions of jazz solos and bass lines from recordings, traditional tunes, and classical scores for piano and monophonic instruments. The evaluations suggest that the method reliably recovers appropriate note spellings, key signatures, and local scales across these varied styles, producing musically sound notations suitable for analysis and teaching. It also yields scale-distance measures applicable to jazz-theoretical and musicological research.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that a two-stage modal–tonal optimization framework is effective for automatic pitch spelling, key estimation, and local scale identification from MIDI-like data. The approach is well suited for automatic transcription workflows, including the creation of high-quality digital jazz solo collections for analysis, education, and heritage preservation, and it can support other notation-processing tasks. The newly defined distances between jazz scales provide an additional contribution with potential value for musicological studies.

Abstract: We present an algorithm for pitch spelling and key estimation. Given an input in MIDI-like format, containing information on note pitches (expressed in semitones relative to the lowest reference note) and bar boundaries, it estimates the appropriate note names, a global Key Signature, and a local scale for each bar. This related information elements are evaluated jointly during two stages of optimisation. During an initial 'modal' stage, a probable scale is proposed for each bar, minimising the number of accidentals to be printed in the printed score with a shortest-path search. Then, during a second stage called 'tonal', these local scales are used to estimate the Key Signature and note names that would result in the best musical notation for the entire piece. We present evaluations conducted on datasets comprising a variety of digital musical scores: jazz lead sheets taken from the Real Book, transcriptions of recordings of jazz soli and bass lines, traditional tunes, as well as classical scores for piano and monophonic instruments. Our procedure was originally designed for use in music transcription, specifically for building digital collections of jazz solos transcribed from audio recordings, for the purposes of music analysis, teaching and the preservation of cultural heritage. This method should also prove useful for other tasks related to the processing of musical notation. Furthermore, to this end, we have defined new distances between various common jazz scales, which may be of some interest to musicological studies.

### [50] [Actionable Activation Directions for Detecting and Mitigating Emergent Misalignment Across Language Model Families](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20225)
*Abdul Rafay Syed*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how fine-tuning language models on insecure code causes misaligned behavior, and whether this behavior aligns with a common, causally meaningful direction in activation space across different model architectures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuning LMs on insecure code can produce misaligned behaviors (e.g., insecure code generation) whose internal mechanisms are not well understood. Understanding whether these misaligned features correspond to consistent, controllable directions in activation space—especially ones that generalize across architectures—could enable more principled auditing and correction methods.

Method: The authors fine-tune four small instruction-tuned model families (Qwen2.5-1.5B, Gemma-2-2B, Llama-3.2-1B, Ministral-3-3B) on insecure code. They compute a difference-in-means activation direction between aligned and misaligned behaviors at the final layer, analyze how well it separates the two, and then causally intervene by subtracting this direction to see if misaligned behavior (code spillover) is reduced. They also perform a secure-code control to test content specificity. For cross-architecture transfer, they use ridge regression to map directions between models, test behavioral suppression, and compare against random and orthogonal directions. They analyze transfer topology and specificity properties of these directions.

Result: Within each model, a single linear direction (difference-in-means at the final layer) separates aligned vs. misaligned activations with 99.6% accuracy and causally reduces insecure code spillover by 21–51 percentage points, with secure-code controls confirming that these directions are content-specific. Cross-architecture transfer of these directions using ridge-regression mappings still yields substantial mitigation of misaligned behavior (up to 46 percentage points), but fails specificity tests because random and orthogonal directions achieve similar suppression. They find an asymmetric geometry in transfer: Gemma and Qwen act as donors whose directions transfer well, while Llama is mainly a receiver.

Conclusion: Misalignment from insecure-code fine-tuning manifests as a strong, causally actionable linear feature within each model, allowing targeted behavioral correction. However, when transferred across models, the same kind of linear feature remains causally impactful but loses behavioral specificity, limiting the usefulness of purely linear cross-architecture correction schemes. Effective auditing and correction therefore should rely primarily on within-model probing rather than on generic cross-model geometric mappings.

Abstract: Fine-tuning language models on insecure code induces emergent misalignment with poorly understood internal structure. We investigate whether this misalignment corresponds to a causally actionable activation-space direction shared across architectures. Across four instruction-tuned model families (Qwen2.5-1.5B, Gemma-2-2B, Llama-3.2-1B, Ministral-3-3B) finetuned identically, a difference-in-means direction achieves 99.6% separation of aligned and misaligned activations at each model's final layer. Causal steering by subtracting this direction reduces code spillover by 21-51 points, while a secure-code control confirms content specificity. Cross-architecture transfer via ridge regression maps yields large behavioral suppression (up to 46 points) but fails specificity controls as random and orthogonal directions perform comparably. We identify a two-tier specificity structure: within-model directions are causally specific and actionable; cross-model directions are causally real but non-specific. An asymmetric transfer topology emerges, with Gemma and Qwen acting as geometric donors and Llama as a receiver. These findings define the limits of linear cross-architecture correction and recommend within-model probing for auditing.

### [51] [The Register Gap: A Meaning Intelligence Framework for Nigerian Public Discourse](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20255)
*Celestine Achi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes the Meaning Intelligence Framework (MIF), a nine-dimension schema to better capture communicative intent in Nigerian public discourse, showing that giving models this schema significantly improves performance over basic sentiment labels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Nigerian-language sentiment benchmarks reduce language understanding to simple polarity (positive/negative/neutral), which misses how context, register, and subtext flip meanings in real discourse. The authors observe that AI failures are mainly due to contextual and pragmatic misunderstandings, not just translation issues, and want a framework that explicitly encodes these richer dimensions of meaning.

Method: They design the Meaning Intelligence Framework with nine scored dimensions: register, surface sentiment, true intent, irony, coded subtext, risk tier, annotator confidence, speaker emotion, and recommended action. They then build a 30-item calibration dataset across Standard English, Nigerian English, Nigerian Pidgin, and code-mixed language. Using this dataset, they evaluate a frontier LLM (Gemini 2.5 Flash) in zero-shot versus schema-informed prompting, where the latter includes the MIF schema in-context. They compute accuracy for register classification and a composite Meaning Intelligence Score over the dimensions.

Result: Zero-shot register classification is poor (33.3% accuracy), but rises to 73.3% (+40 percentage points) when the model is given the MIF schema in the prompt. The composite Meaning Intelligence Score improves from 73.2 to 78.6 (+5.4 points) with schema-informed prompting, with the largest gains in register identification, coded subtext detection (~+10 points), and strategic action recommendation (~+10.3 points).

Conclusion: Encoding a richer, multi-dimensional representation of meaning—beyond simple sentiment polarity—substantially improves LLM understanding of Nigerian public discourse, especially for register, subtext, and recommended responses. The MIF framework and released calibration set provide a more realistic, context-aware benchmark and tool for evaluating and steering AI systems on Nigerian data, while a private holdout protects against contamination.

Abstract: We introduce the Meaning Intelligence Framework (MIF), a nine-dimension annotation and evaluation schema for Nigerian public discourse that separates surface sentiment from true communicative intent. Existing benchmarks for Nigerian languages, including NaijaSenti and AfriSenti, treat sentiment classification as a three-way polarity task (positive, negative, neutral). We argue that the dominant failure mode of AI systems on Nigerian discourse is not translation failure but context failure: the same utterance carries opposite pragmatic force depending on speaker, audience, and situation. The MIF operationalises this insight across nine scored dimensions: register, surface sentiment, true intent, irony, coded subtext, risk tier, annotator confidence, speaker emotion, and recommended communications action. We construct a 30-item calibration dataset spanning Standard English, Nigerian English, Nigerian Pidgin, and code-mixed registers, and evaluate a frontier language model (Gemini 2.5 Flash) under zero-shot and schema-informed prompting conditions. The headline finding is the Register Gap: zero-shot register classification accuracy is 33.3%, rising to 73.3% (+40 points) when the model receives the MIF schema in-context. The composite Meaning Intelligence Score increases by 5.4 points (73.2 to 78.6) under schema-informed prompting, with the largest practical gains in register identification, coded-subtext detection (+10 points), and strategic action recommendation (+10.3 points). We release the framework specification, annotation guidelines, and the 30-item public calibration set to support reproducibility, while retaining a private holdout corpus for contamination-protected evaluation.

To address this fragmentation, this work proposes PsyScore, a psychometrically-aware framework that integrates diagnostic assessment with instructional scaffolding through a shared latent ability representation. PsyScore comprises three key modules: a Trait-Adaptive Neural IRT Scorer that incorporates the Graded Partial Credit Model (GPCM) into a neural architecture, enabling the precise estimation of student ability while maintaining psychometric interpretability, a ZPD-Scaffolded Feedback Generator, which conditions multi-agent feedback strategies on the diagnosed ability parameter to adapt instructional focus across different proficiency levels, and a Multi-Perspective Feedback Evaluation Strategy that assesses feedback quality via pairwise preference judgements and student revision simulations. ### [54] [Your Mouse and Eyes Secretly Leak Your Preference: LLM Alignment using Implicit Feedback from Users](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20482)
*Haw-Shiuan Chang,Jeffrey Gomez,Mehul Patwari,Aryan Sajith,Hamed Zamani*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper introduces IFLLM, a dataset of implicit human feedback (mouse trajectories and eye gaze) on LLM responses, and shows that incorporating this implicit feedback into reward models substantially improves alignment performance compared to text-only feedback.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM alignment methods rely mainly on explicit human feedback, such as ratings or preference labels over responses, which is expensive and rare in real user interactions. They also ignore implicit behavioral signals—like where users look and how they move their mouse—that Internet platforms successfully exploit to model user preference and engagement. The paper is motivated by the idea that such implicit feedback could be a rich, underused resource for aligning LLMs more effectively and at scale.

Method: The authors construct a new dataset, IFLLM, by recruiting 59 Mechanical Turk workers to interact with LLMs over 1336 multi-turn questions while recording their mouse trajectories and eye-gaze data via webcams. They analyze the diversity of user gaze and mouse behaviors and train a reward model that takes these implicit-feedback signals, along with text, to predict user preferences over LLM responses. They then apply Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) to eight different LLMs using this enhanced reward model and compare it against a standard text-only reward baseline.

Result: IFLLM reveals substantial diversity in users’ gaze patterns and mouse movements, indicating rich, non-textual information about how users process LLM outputs. Incorporating implicit user feedback into the reward model improves its predictive accuracy from 55% to 64% versus a text-only model. When used to optimize eight LLMs via DPO, the implicit-feedback-based reward nearly triples the relative improvement in response quality compared to using a purely text-based reward model.

Conclusion: Implicit behavioral signals such as eye gaze and mouse trajectories can significantly enhance reward modeling for LLM alignment beyond what is achievable with explicit, text-based feedback alone. The IFLLM dataset demonstrates that these signals are informative, diverse across users, and practically useful for training better-aligned LLMs. This suggests that large-scale, low-friction collection of implicit feedback in real-world deployments could be a powerful and cost-effective complement to traditional explicit preference labeling for future LLM alignment systems.

Abstract: To align a Large Language Model (LLM), most existing methods collect explicit human feedback and train a reward model to predict the human preference based on the response text. These existing methods have two key limitations. First, the users rarely provide explicit feedback for LLM responses, which makes the high-quality preference annotation expensive to collect. Second, the methods do not leverage implicit human feedback, which has proven vital to the economic moats of Internet giants. To quantify the value of implicit feedback, we build a new dataset called IFLLM, which collects 1336 multi-turn questions from the 59 Mechanical Turk workers, their mouse trajectories, and eye gazing points to the LLMs' responses from their webcams. IFLLM shows that the users have very diverse types of gazing behavior and mouse trajectories. Our reward model based on the implicit user feedback boosts the accuracy of the text-based reward model from 55% to 64% and nearly triples the relative response quality improvements after applying the DPO to eight LLMs, demonstrating the value of implicit feedback in the wild. Our data collection website, dataset, and codes can be found at https://github.com/themehulpatwari/llm-implicit-feedback/.

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces H-RePlan, a hierarchical replanning framework that improves robustness of multi-device computer-use agents by separating local strategy recovery from global replanning, and demonstrates its effectiveness on a new fault-injected benchmark, HeraBench.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-device agent systems struggle with fine-grained failure recovery because they lack a systematic model of device-local execution strategies, leading to coarse-grained retries or replanning that are inefficient and sometimes ineffective. The authors want to enable agents to better decide when a failure can be resolved locally on the same device versus when a cross-device or global replanning is necessary, thus improving robustness and efficiency of multi-device workflows.

Method: The authors design H-RePlan, a hierarchical replanning framework that operates across an orchestrator layer and device layers. Each device is given interchangeable execution strategies spanning APIs, CLIs, and GUIs. A compact cross-layer failure abstraction is introduced so that devices can attempt local strategy recovery first, and only escalate to the orchestrator for global replanning when necessary. To assess the framework, they build HeraBench, a benchmark that creates cross-device workflows over Linux and Android and injects both strategy-level and device-level failures to test recovery capabilities.

Result: In experiments on HeraBench, H-RePlan significantly outperforms baselines that use a single execution strategy per device or only coarse-grained recovery. It achieves higher task completion rates, better adherence to instructions, and more perfect-pass outcomes, while also lowering the token cost needed for reliable end-to-end task success.

Conclusion: Scope-aware hierarchical recovery, which cleanly distinguishes between device-local strategy repair and orchestrator-level replanning, is crucial for robust and efficient execution of real-world multi-device computer-use tasks. H-RePlan, combined with the HeraBench benchmark, demonstrates a practical and effective approach to achieving this.

Abstract: Real-world computer-use tasks often span multiple applications and devices, requiring agents to coordinate heterogeneous environments under dynamic runtime failures. Existing multi-device agent systems support task decomposition and cross-device assignment, but recovery remains largely coarse-grained: when execution fails, they typically retry the same strategy, reassign the subtask, or revise the global plan, without systematically modeling the device-local strategy space. This limits their ability to distinguish failures that can be repaired within the current device from those that require cross-device replanning. We propose \textbf{H-RePlan}, a hierarchical replanning framework for multi-device agents with unified API--CLI--GUI execution. H-RePlan equips each device with interchangeable execution strategies and separates device-local strategy recovery from orchestrator-level global replanning through a compact cross-layer failure abstraction. To evaluate this capability, we introduce \textbf{HeraBench}, a fault-injected benchmark that constructs cross-device workflows over Linux and Android devices and injects strategy- and device-level failures. Experiments show that H-RePlan substantially outperforms single-strategy and coarse-grained multi-device baselines, achieving higher completion, instruction adherence, and perfect-pass rates while reducing the token cost required for reliable end-to-end success. These results demonstrate that scope-aware hierarchical recovery is essential for robust multi-device agent execution.

</details>


### [56] [StylisticBias: A Few Human Visual Cues Drive Most Social Biases in MLLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20527)
*Shaghayegh Kolli,Timo Cavelius,Nafiseh Nikeghbal,Samantha Dalal,Jana Diesner*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces StylisticBias, a controlled benchmark to measure how specific visual attributes in images influence social judgments made by multimodal large language models, isolating visual style effects from identity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of social bias in multimodal large language models usually compare different individuals or demographic groups, which confounds visual appearance with identity and makes it hard to pinpoint which visual cues actually drive biased judgments. As MLLMs are increasingly used in impactful contexts, there is a need for a controlled, fine-grained tool to study how particular visual attributes (like age, body type, fashion style) influence model outputs.

Method: The authors construct a benchmark called StylisticBias by first generating 500 photorealistic base faces. For each base face, they systematically create about 50 variants, each differing in exactly one visual attribute (e.g., age, body type, fashion style), while keeping the underlying identity constant. This yields around 25,000 images where only one attribute changes at a time. They then use these images to probe six different multimodal large language models across 25 binary social-judgment tasks, measuring how changing each attribute affects model responses.

Result: Empirically, they find that age and body type are the most influential at the identity level (i.e., they cause large consistent shifts across many identities), while fashion style and some other visual attributes produce the largest shifts at the individual attribute level. About 15 visual attributes explain nearly 80% of the total variation in model judgments, indicating that bias is highly concentrated in a relatively small set of cues. The models show the greatest sensitivity in tasks where the social judgment is semantically related to appearance, particularly in socioeconomic and style-related evaluations.

Conclusion: StylisticBias provides a fine-grained, controlled benchmark for assessing visual-attribute-level social biases in multimodal large language models. By holding identity fixed and varying one cue at a time, it enables clearer causal analysis of how particular visual attributes shape model judgments. The findings reveal that a small subset of visual cues—especially age, body type, and fashion/style—accounts for most bias-related variation, underscoring the need to address these concentrated appearance-based sensitivities when deploying MLLMs. The authors publicly release the code and dataset to support further research and mitigation efforts.

Abstract: Multimodal large language models (MLLMs) are increasingly deployed in personally and societally consequential settings, yet the visual cues that shape how these models judge people remain poorly understood. Prior work often compares different (groups of) individuals, making it difficult to separate appearance effects from identity differences. We introduce StylisticBias, a controlled benchmark for evaluating attribute-level social bias in MLLMs. We generate 500 photorealistic base faces and create about 50 single-attribute variations per face, producing about 25K images. This design keeps identity fixed and changes one visual attribute at a time. It lets us measure how specific cues shift model judgments. We evaluate six MLLMs across 25 binary social judgment scenarios. We find that age and body type dominate identity-level effects, while fashion style and other visual cues drive the largest attribute-level shifts. We further find that about 15 attributes account for nearly 80\% of the total variation, showing that bias is concentrated in a small set of visual cues. Sensitivity is strongest in judgments that are semantically aligned with appearance, especially socioeconomic and style-related judgments. We release StylisticBias as a benchmark for fine-grained bias evaluation in multimodal models. Code and dataset: https://github.com/timo-cavelius/StylisticBias and https://hf.co/datasets/shaghayegh/stylistic-bias-dataset.

</details>

### [57] [Deontic Policies for Runtime Governance of Agentic AI Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19464)
*Anupam Joshi,Tim Finin,Karuna Pande Joshi,Lalana Kagal*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces AgenticRei, a deontic policy framework to govern autonomous LLM-based agents beyond simple access control, covering obligations, dispensations, conflict resolution, and ontological reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing policy engines (XACML, Rego, Cedar) only handle permit/deny decisions and lack richer governance constructs needed for autonomous agentic AI that can call tools, handle sensitive data, and collaborate across organizations. Enterprises need to express not only what is allowed or forbidden, but also what agents must do after certain actions, how obligations can be waived in special circumstances, and how to resolve conflicts between policies, especially in complex domains like healthcare, cybersecurity, and privacy.

Method: The authors design AgenticRei, a governance framework based on a deontic policy language derived from the Rei framework and encoded in OWL. Policies are evaluated at runtime by an external high-performance logic engine, separate from the LLM. The same policy evaluation pipeline is used for both tool invocations and inter-agent messaging. AgenticRei adds constructs for obligations, dispensations, conflict resolution, and reasoning over domain ontologies on top of basic permit/prohibit rules. They illustrate the expressiveness of the system through governance examples in security and privacy scenarios.

Result: AgenticRei can represent and enforce governance constraints—particularly obligations, dispensations, and policy conflict handling—that current production policy engines cannot naturally express. The framework supports ontological reasoning over domain class hierarchies and can be integrated with existing agent orchestration standards like A2AS. Through examples, the authors demonstrate that many realistic enterprise governance requirements for agentic AI can be captured in their deontic policy language and enforced at runtime outside the LLM.

Conclusion: Deontic, ontology-based policy languages like AgenticRei are better suited than traditional permit/deny policy engines for governing autonomous LLM-based agents in enterprise settings. By externalizing and enriching policy evaluation to include obligations, dispensations, conflict resolution, and ontological reasoning, AgenticRei provides a more complete governance substrate that can sit alongside standard agent frameworks and address emerging security and privacy challenges of agentic AI.

Abstract: Autonomous agentic AI systems driven by Large Language Models (LLMs) introduce a new class of security, privacy, and compliance challenges: an agent that can invoke tools, manipulate data, install software, and coordinate with peer agents across organizational boundaries must be constrained not just by authentication and access control, but by the full structure of enterprise governance. This includes specifying what agents are permitted and prohibited from doing, what they areobliged to do after certain actions (e.g., notify the CISO), under what conditions a standing obligation may be waived, and which rules take precedence when policies conflict. This governance problem exceeds what current policy engines provide. Systems such as XACML, Rego, and Cedar address only the permit/prohibit subset of this governance structure. They do not provide obligation lifecycle management, meta-policy conflict resolution, dispensations that waive obligations in specific circumstances, and ontological reasoning over domain class hierarchies commonly found in applications such as healthcare, cybersecurity, or data privacy. We propose AgenticRei, which realizes key governance requirements such as obligations, dispensations, policy conflict resolutions, and reasoning over policies, as well as the basic permit/prohibit constraints. We use a deontic policy language built on the Rei framework, expressed as OWL (Web Ontology Language) and evaluated at runtime by a high-performance logic engine entirely outside the LLM. The same pipeline governs both tool invocations by the agent and agent-to-agent messages. We show through examples that deontic policies capture governance constraints around security and privacy that mostly cannot be expressed in current production engines. Our approach composes naturally with industry-standard frameworks like A2AS.

</details>


### [58] [Measuring Curriculum Alignment across Topical Coverage, Competency, and Cognitive Depth: A Longitudinal Framework Applied to CS2013 and CS2023](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19469)
*Sherzod Turaev,Mary John,Saja Aldabet,Mamoun Awad,Nazar Zaki,Khaled Shuaib*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents a human-in-the-loop, retrieval-based pipeline to quantitatively map how well an undergraduate CS program covers international CS curriculum guidelines (CS2013, CS2023) and how this coverage changes over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Undergraduate CS programs are guided by periodically updated international curricula (e.g., CS2013, CS2023), but departments lack a rigorous, reproducible method to measure how fully their courses cover these guidelines or how coverage shifts when the guidelines are revised. Existing approaches are often ad hoc, manual, and not easily comparable across time or institutions.

Method: The authors build a human-in-the-loop measurement pipeline. They model both the CS program (courses, syllabi) and the curriculum guidelines (knowledge units, competencies, cognitive levels) as structured text corpora. They use semantic retrieval to propose candidate mappings between courses and guideline knowledge units, benchmarking seven different retrievers and selecting a reciprocal-rank-fusion ensemble as best. Human experts then confirm or reject these candidate mappings under a clear definition of coverage. The resulting course-to-knowledge-unit maps are independently validated by a second rater, with agreement measured by Cohen’s kappa. The same retrieve-then-confirm framework is extended to map competencies and cognitive depth levels. The pipeline is applied longitudinally to a single accredited BSc in Computer Science, comparing its alignment to CS2013 and CS2023.

Result: Empirically, the best-performing retriever is an ensemble based on reciprocal rank fusion; a widely praised long-context model performs worse than a smaller sentence-level model, indicating that retriever choice must be empirically validated. Inter-rater reliability is substantial (Cohen's kappa 0.64 for CS2023, 0.69 for CS2013). The studied program covers about half of the guideline knowledge units (49.7% of CS2023 and 50.9% of CS2013), with similar overall coverage across the decade. For covered units, the program explicitly articulates relevant competencies for roughly 88% under both guidelines. However, it matches the recommended cognitive depth for 76% of covered units under CS2023 versus 95% under CS2013, indicating that CS2023 raises depth expectations more than the program has changed. Longitudinal analysis identifies persistent coverage gaps in areas such as parallel and distributed computing, programming language foundations, and systems fundamentals, which remain undercovered relative to both guideline versions and ABET criteria.

Conclusion: The paper demonstrates a reusable, validated instrument for quantitatively assessing how well a CS program covers evolving international curriculum guidelines. The human-in-the-loop, retrieve-then-confirm pipeline can track coverage over time, distinguish between structural program gaps and changes driven by new standards, and extend to competencies and cognitive depth. Findings from the case study program suggest stable breadth of coverage but reveal deeper expectations in CS2023 and persistent weaknesses in several core areas. The authors make the instrument available on request, supporting broader adoption and cross-institutional comparisons.

Abstract: Undergraduate computer science is governed by international curricular guidelines revised about once a decade, yet programs lack a reliable, reproducible way to measure how completely they cover the current guidelines and how that coverage shifts when the guidelines are restructured. We address this with a human-in-the-loop pipeline that measures a program's coverage of an external body of knowledge, applied longitudinally to one accredited BSc in Computer Science against Computer Science Curricula 2013 (CS2013) and 2023 (CS2023). The pipeline represents the program and each guideline as structured corpora, generates candidate course-to-knowledge-unit matches by semantic retrieval, and confirms them through human judgment under an explicit coverage definition. Of seven benchmarked retrievers, a reciprocal-rank-fusion ensemble was strongest, and a reputed long-context model underperformed a small sentence model, so retriever choice must be measured. Both maps were validated by an independent second rater (Cohen's kappa 0.64 for CS2023, 0.69 for CS2013). The program covers 49.7% of CS2023 and 50.9% of CS2013 knowledge units, near-constant across a decade. Extending the same retrieve-then-confirm design to competency articulation and cognitive depth shows that the program articulates the competency for ~88% of covered units under each guideline, yet delivers it at the recommended depth for 76% of present units under CS2023 against 95% under CS2013, a gap reflecting the newer guideline's raised expectations, not the program. The longitudinal comparison separates persistent structural gaps (parallel and distributed computing, foundations of programming languages, systems fundamentals), uncovered against both guidelines and ABET, from differences that reflect the standard's evolution. The instrument is reusable and available from the authors on request.

</details>


### [59] [Diffusion Language Models: An Experimental Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19475)
*Thomas Bertolani,Davide Bucciarelli,Leonardo Zini,Marcella Cornia,Lorenzo Baraldi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper systematically compares diffusion-based language models (DLMs) to understand their capabilities, trade-offs, and practical deployment characteristics across multiple tasks and inference settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although many Diffusion Language Models have been proposed as an alternative to autoregressive LLMs, inconsistent evaluation setups (datasets, metrics, inference budgets, and hyperparameters) make it hard to fairly compare them or understand when they are preferable. The community lacks a unified, empirical study that clarifies how DLM architectures and inference-time choices affect performance and efficiency.

Method: The authors conduct a broad empirical study of eight state-of-the-art DLMs over eight benchmarks covering reasoning, coding, translation, knowledge-intensive tasks, and structured problem solving. They evaluate both generation quality and computational efficiency. In addition, they perform ablations on inference-time factors—such as number of denoising steps, context length, block size, and parallel unmasking strategies—and run controlled experiments on smaller models trained under identical conditions to isolate the effects of architectural and inference choices.

Result: The experiments reveal clear strengths and weaknesses of diffusion-based language models across task types, architectures, and compute budgets. Performance is shown to be highly sensitive to generation-time design decisions like denoising schedules, context length, and masking strategies, which in turn create concrete trade-offs between output quality and computational cost. The study maps out where DLMs are competitive and where they lag, and quantifies how inference hyperparameters shift this balance.

Conclusion: Diffusion-based language models are viable but their effectiveness depends heavily on how generation is configured at inference time. There is no single best setting; instead, practitioners must navigate task-dependent trade-offs between accuracy and efficiency. The paper offers practical guidance by systematically characterizing these trade-offs and clarifying the deployment behavior of modern DLMs under varied conditions.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have revolutionized language modeling through autoregressive generation, enabling strong performance across a wide range of tasks. Recently, Diffusion Language Models (DLMs) have emerged as an alternative paradigm that generates text through iterative denoising rather than next-token prediction, allowing parallel refinement of entire sequences. While numerous diffusion-based architectures have been proposed, differences in evaluation protocols, datasets, inference budgets, and generation hyperparameters make it difficult to compare their capabilities and understand the trade-offs they offer. In this work, we present a systematic experimental analysis of modern DLMs. Specifically, we evaluate eight state-of-the-art DLMs across eight benchmarks spanning reasoning, coding, translation, knowledge, and structured problem solving, while explicitly considering both generation quality and computational efficiency. Beyond downstream evaluation, we analyze the impact of key inference-time factors, including denoising steps, context length, block size, and parallel unmasking strategies, and complement large-scale experiments with controlled comparisons of smaller models trained under identical conditions. Our analysis highlights the strengths and limitations of diffusion-based language modeling across different tasks, architectures, and inference budgets. We show that the behavior of DLMs is strongly influenced by generation-time design choices, leading to distinct trade-offs between performance and computational efficiency. Overall, our study provides practical insights into the capabilities and deployment characteristics of contemporary DLMs.

</details>


### [60] [Hidden Anchors in Multi-Agent LLM Deliberation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19494)
*Apurba Pokharel,Ram Dantu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They model multi-agent LLM deliberation with hidden internal anchors that shape agents’ opinions, showing anchors can be recovered and explain super-consensus accuracy gains beyond classical opinion-dynamics models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent LLM deliberation improves reasoning, but existing models like DeGroot and Friedkin–Johnsen don’t capture agents’ persistent internal beliefs and cannot explain how accuracy can surpass the convex hull of initial opinions. The authors aim to provide a principled dynamical model that mirrors human social decision-making and explains when and why deliberation works.

Method: They frame multi-agent deliberation as a closed-loop dynamical system where each agent has a latent, time-invariant internal belief—an anchor—that continuously pulls its expressed opinion during interactions. Using observed deliberation trajectories, they develop a procedure to infer each agent’s anchor and then test the inferred model by predicting held-out deliberation runs. They compare this anchor-based model to classical consensus rules across three open-weight LLM families, analyzing whether deliberation remains in or escapes the convex hull of initial opinions.

Result: They show that agents’ internal anchors can be uniquely and reliably recovered from deliberation transcripts alone. These anchors explain behavior that standard consensus models rule out, specifically that an agent’s confidence in the correct answer can exceed all initial confidence levels, leaving the convex hull of initial beliefs. Empirically, across three LLM families, they find a spectrum: all models appear influenced by internal anchors to a similar degree, but differ in where those anchors lie relative to initial opinions, which in turn determines whether deliberation stays within or escapes the initial belief hull.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that multi-agent LLM deliberation is better understood as a closed-loop dynamical system with latent internal anchors, rather than as simple averaging dynamics. Anchors can be inferred and used to test whether a model is genuinely anchor-driven, and the position of these anchors—especially when far from initial expressed opinions—explains when deliberation can surpass initial beliefs and achieve higher accuracy. This provides both a conceptual framework and an empirical diagnostic for understanding and designing multi-agent LLM systems.

Abstract: Multi-agent LLM deliberation, where agents exchange and revise answers over several rounds, is increasingly used to improve reasoning and accuracy, yet how and why it works is rarely modelled. Such deliberation mirrors how humans reach decisions. As social animals we are pulled both by the group, the herd effect that classical opinion-dynamics models such as DeGroot and Friedkin--Johnsen capture, and by our own internal belief, which they do not. We model multi-agent deliberation as a closed-loop dynamical system in which each agent carries a hidden internal belief, its anchor, that continually pulls its opinion regardless of its neighbours. We show this anchor can be recovered from the deliberation alone, and that it explains a behaviour classical consensus rules forbid: an agent's confidence in the correct answer can climb past where any agent started, escaping the space (convexhull) formed by the initial beliefs. Checking whether the recovered anchor also predicts held-out runs (generalizes) gives a simple test for when a model is truly driven bysuch an anchor. Across three open-weight model families this is a spectrum, not all-or-nothing. All anchors' influence are about equally strongly, but they differ in where the anchor sits, and only when it sits far from the initial opinions does deliberation escape the hull and need the full closed-loop model.

</details>


### [61] [DeXposure-Claw: An Agentic System for DeFi Risk Supervision](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19501)
*Aijie Shu,Bowei Chen,Wenbin Wu,Cathy Yi-Hsuan Chen,Fengxiang He*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents DeXposure-Claw, a specialized, forecast-grounded LLM-based supervision system for decentralized finance that reduces false alarms in regulatory monitoring by routing decisions through structured evidence, and introduces DeXposure-Bench, a regulator-aligned evaluation framework.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Regulators monitoring decentralized finance face complex, fast-changing networked credit risks. General-purpose LLM agents tend to overreact to weak evidence, recommending high-stakes interventions, and current benchmarks do not properly measure regulator-relevant false alarms. The authors are motivated to design a supervision system and evaluation methodology that aligns with regulatory needs, reduces spurious interventions, and provides auditable, structured decision rationales.

Method: They build DeXposure-Claw, an agentic supervision pipeline with three main components: (1) DeXposure-FM, a graph time-series foundation model that forecasts future credit exposure networks in DeFi; (2) deterministic monitors and stress scenarios that transform model forecasts into structured artifacts such as typed alerts, attribution signals, and scenario evidence; and (3) data-health and confidence-gating mechanisms that determine when the system can escalate to emitting supervisory tickets. These tickets are auditable and contain rationales. Additionally, they propose DeXposure-Bench, a six-axis evaluation harness whose decision axis evaluates generated tickets against a regulator-aligned absolute-loss ground truth and explicitly tracks false-intervention rates.

Result: On five years of weekly real-world DeFi data, the system performs well, with experiments fully supporting its effectiveness. It successfully generates regulator-aligned supervisory tickets with fewer false alarms, and DeXposure-Bench provides meaningful, loss-based and false-intervention–aware evaluation of its decisions. The implementation has been open-sourced on GitHub.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that forecast-grounded, structured-evidence pipelines like DeXposure-Claw can overcome key limitations of generic LLM agents in supervisory contexts such as decentralized finance. By combining graph time-series forecasting, deterministic monitoring, and rigorous evaluation via DeXposure-Bench, the system offers regulators a more reliable, auditable, and quantitatively grounded way to manage DeFi credit risk.

Abstract: Decentralized finance exposes supervisors to fast-moving, networked credit risks. General-purpose LLM agents fit this setting poorly: they over-read weak evidence and recommend high-stakes interventions, while existing evaluations offer no regulator-aligned way to measure the resulting false alarms. We introduce DeXposure-Claw, a forecast-grounded agentic supervision system that routes LLM decisions through structured evidence: (1) DeXposure-FM, a graph time-series foundation model, forecasts future exposure networks; (2) deterministic monitors and stress scenarios then turn those forecasts into typed alerts, attribution signals, and scenario evidence; and (3) data-health and confidence gates constrain escalation before DeXposure-Claw emits auditable supervisory tickets with rationales. We further develop DeXposure-Bench, a six-axis evaluation harness, whose decision axis scores tickets against a regulator-aligned absolute-loss ground truth and an explicit false-intervention rate. Experiments on five years of weekly real data fully support our system. Code is at https://github.com/EVIEHub/DeXposure-Claw.

</details>


### [62] [LLM Doesn't Know What It Doesn't Know: Detecting Epistemic Blind Spots via Cross-Model Attribution Divergence on Clinical Tabular Data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19509)
*Akshat Dasula,Prasanna Desikan,Jaideep Srivastava*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether large language models (LLMs) working on structured clinical data can recognize when they don’t know, by comparing them to an XGBoost model and analyzing how their attributions diverge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly being used on structured clinical data, but it is unclear if they can reliably indicate their own uncertainty—critical for safety in healthcare. Existing confidence scores from LLMs may not be trustworthy, and the authors want a way to detect when the LLM’s reasoning diverges from a well-calibrated model without needing access to LLM internals.

Method: They compare Qwen 2.5 7B to an XGBoost model on a structured prediction task. Using attribution divergence analysis, they quantify how much the LLM’s feature attributions disagree with XGBoost’s, introducing the Attribution Disagreement Score (ADS). They test different interventions (few-shot examples, SHAP-based feature evidence) and build a cross-model calibrator that uses attribution divergence to estimate when the LLM is reliable, instead of relying on its own verbalized confidence.

Result: 1) LLM verbalized confidence is nearly constant and unrelated to true accuracy, varying mostly with prompt format. 2) The LLM has an inverse difficulty effect: it is less accurate when XGBoost is very confident and more accurate when XGBoost is moderately uncertain. 3) Combining few-shot examples with SHAP-based feature evidence greatly reduces attribution disagreement (ADS 1.54→0.38) and boosts accuracy (49%→75.3%) without further training. 4) A cross-model calibrator driven by attribution divergence dramatically improves calibration (expected calibration error 0.254→0.080), yielding patient-specific reliability scores without extra access to model internals or repeated queries.

Conclusion: LLMs on structured clinical data cannot be trusted to self-report uncertainty via verbal confidence, showing a “cold start” problem. However, cross-model attribution divergence—especially when combined with external feature evidence and few-shot examples—can improve both their performance and our ability to estimate when they are reliable. This provides a practical route toward more epistemically self-aware LLMs in structured prediction tasks, particularly in clinical contexts.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly applied to structured clinical data, yet whether they can recognize the limits of their own knowledge on such tasks remains unexplored. We study this question through the lens of cross-model attribution divergence with the goal of reducing epistemic uncertainty for structured tasks, comparing Qwen 2.5 7B and XGBoost on a prediction task via attribution divergence analysis. We report four findings. First, LLM verbalized confidence is epistemically vacuous, it outputs a near-constant (0.856-0.937) regardless of whether accuracy is 49% or 75.3%, tracking prompt format rather than prediction quality. Second, the LLM exhibits an inverse difficulty effect: accuracy drops to 64.8% when XGBoost is 99% correct, but matches XGBoost (73.8% vs. 73.1%) when it is moderately uncertain. Third, few-shot examples and SHAP-derived feature evidence are orthogonal, super-additive interventions: they reduce the Attribution Disagreement Score (ADS) from 1.54 to 0.38 and improve accuracy from 49% to 75.3% without training. Fourth, a cross-model calibrator that determined LLM reliability using attribution divergence signals reduces expected calibration error from 0.254 to 0.080, replacing uninformative verbalized confidence with patient-specific reliability estimates, without accessing model internals or requiring repeated inference. We frame these findings as a cold start problem for LLMs on structured data and outline a path toward genuine epistemic self-awareness.

</details>


### [63] [REVEAL++: Differentiable Phenotypic Grouping for Vision-Language Retinal Modeling of Alzheimer's Disease Risk](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19522)
*Ethan Elio Meidinger,Seowung Leem,Zeyun Zhao,Ruogu Fang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new continuous, soft-weighted contrastive learning framework that aligns retinal fundus images with clinical risk narratives to better predict future Alzheimer’s disease, outperforming prior discrete group-based and standard vision‑language methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing vision-language methods for predicting Alzheimer’s disease from retinal images use hard phenotypic group assignments (discrete clusters of similar risk profiles) when forming multi-positive pairs in contrastive learning. This rigid, hand-designed grouping ignores the continuous spectrum of disease risk and decouples the process of forming phenotypic groups from representation learning, potentially limiting performance and robustness for early neurodegenerative risk prediction.

Method: The authors reformulate phenotypic similarity as a continuous, learnable construct within contrastive learning. Instead of assigning each subject to a fixed group, they compute inter-subject similarity as a differentiable weighting function based on intra-modality embedding similarities for both retinal images and structured clinical risk profiles. These weights define soft multi-positive relations via a continuous aggregation operator, so each sample contributes to others proportionally to its similarity. On top of this, they introduce a soft-target contrastive loss that simultaneously learns cross-modal alignment (retina–narrative) and refines the phenotypic similarity structure in an end-to-end fashion.

Result: On UK Biobank retinal imaging data for incident Alzheimer’s disease prediction, the continuous soft-weighted framework yields consistent improvements over (1) discrete group-based contrastive learning methods that use hard phenotypic clusters and (2) standard vision-language baselines without phenotypic structure. The gains demonstrate that modeling phenotypic similarity as a graded signal improves early risk prediction performance.

Conclusion: Treating phenotypic similarity as a continuous, learnable signal rather than as fixed, hard groups leads to more flexible and informative supervision in contrastive vision-language models. This soft-target, similarity-weighted framework offers a principled way to capture the spectrum of neurodegenerative risk from multi-modal retinal and clinical data, providing a stronger foundation for population-scale modeling and potentially more accurate early prediction of Alzheimer’s disease.

Abstract: The retina offers a noninvasive window into neurodegenerative disease, capturing subtle structural patterns associated with a risk of future cognitive decline. Vision-language alignment frameworks such as REVEAL have shown that pairing retinal fundus images with structured clinical risk narratives improves early prediction of Alzheimer's disease (AD). A key design choice in these approaches is the use of phenotypic grouping, where individuals with similar risk profiles are treated as multi-positive pairs during contrastive learning. However, existing methods operationalize phenotypic similarity as a discrete construct, relying on hard group assignments that impose rigid supervision and decouple group formation from representation learning. We propose a continuous formulation of phenotypic structure within contrastive learning. Rather than assigning samples to fixed clusters, we model inter-subject similarity as a differentiable weighting function derived from intra-modality embedding similarities in both retinal images and risk profiles. These weights define soft multi-positive relationships through a continuous aggregation operator, enabling graded supervision that reflects the spectrum nature of disease risk. We further introduce a soft-target contrastive objective that jointly learns cross-modal alignment and phenotypic structure in an end-to-end manner. Evaluated on UK Biobank retinal imaging data for incident AD prediction, the proposed framework consistently outperforms discrete group-based contrastive learning and standard vision-language baselines. By treating phenotypic similarity as a learnable, continuous signal rather than a fixed grouping rule, our approach provides a principled and robust foundation for population-scale neurodegenerative risk modeling from multi-modal retinal and clinical data.
