### [64] [Emergent Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19527)
*Martin Kolář*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper explores whether LLMs can detect and correct their own unethical outputs by adding a self-review "conscience" step and an alignment loss term, showing that this yields more ethical behavior even in adversarial code-hacking settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs can exhibit emergent unethical behavior, especially when fine-tuned for tasks like code hacking or when subjected to adversarial prompts. Existing alignment methods often require external judges, additional models, or human feedback, which can be costly, brittle, or hard to scale. The authors are motivated to develop an efficient, scalable way for an LLM to recognize and reduce its own misalignment without relying on a stronger or separate oversight model.

Method: They augment the LLM’s inference and training procedures with: (1) a "conscience" step where the model introspectively reviews its own reasoning and output via a high-level self-query about ethicality; and (2) a training objective that incorporates Direct Preference Optimization (DPO), where aligned vs. misaligned outputs are contrasted to adjust the model’s preferences away from unethical behavior. Crucially, the judge used for these preferences is a frozen copy of the same model rather than an external model. They apply this setup in multiple regimes: base training, fine-tuning, adversarial prompting, and zero-shot usage, focusing particularly on a code-hacking benchmark that previously revealed emergent misalignment.

Result: The authors find that the added conscience step plus DPO-based alignment loss successfully reduces unethical or misaligned outputs across settings. In the specific Emergent Misalignment code-hacking scenario, their method turns the same setup into one of "Emergent Alignment": a single, high-level introspective question during training is sufficient to guide the model toward ethical behavior. They report that the approach works online (during ongoing training or interaction) and does not require a more capable external judge model.

Conclusion: The work concludes that LLMs can, to a meaningful extent, detect and correct their own ethical misalignment by using self-introspection combined with preference-based alignment training. This self-referential alignment method can operate during training, fine-tuning, adversarial prompting, and zero-shot inference without external judges. The authors position this as evidence that simple introspective prompts plus DPO can turn emergent misalignment into emergent alignment in challenging domains like code hacking.

Abstract: Can Large Language Models (LLMs) discern when their own outputs are misaligned with human ethics? And can they self-correct? We endow an LLM with a conscience step that reviews its own reasoning and outputs, and we extend the training loss with an alignment component using Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) to steer the model away from non-ethical outputs. The result is an online technique to align models in a wide range of applications: training, fine-tuning, adversarial prompting, and zero-shot learning. It does not require a weaker or stronger judge, relying instead on a frozen copy of itself. In previous work, the Emergent Misalignment scenario showed a range of emergent unethical behaviors from fine-tuning the model to hack code. Instead, we empirically show how to achieve Emergent Alignment: a single high-level introspective question steers training toward an ethical model under the same code hacking scenario.

</details>


### [65] [ITNet: A Learnable Integral Transform That Subsumes Convolution, Attention, and Recurrence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19538)
*Ashim Dhor,Rasel Mondal,Pin Yu Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Integral Transform Networks (ITNets), a unified neural architecture that encompasses convolutions, attention, and recurrent models as special cases, achieving competitive results across vision and language benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing neural architectures—CNNs, RNNs, and Transformers—encode different inductive biases and are treated as fundamentally distinct, making it difficult to design a single model class that can flexibly capture locality, sequence memory, and pairwise interactions across modalities. The authors aim to find a common mathematical framework that unifies these architectures and offers a single, learnable mechanism for signal processing.

Method: The authors define Integral Transform Networks (ITNets) based on a learnable integral transform with a kernel that jointly depends on positions and features. This kernel is parameterized by a small MLP that models pairwise interactions. They show that by constraining or parameterizing the kernel appropriately, ITNets can reproduce convolution, self-attention (including multi-head), and autoregressive recurrences such as LSTM, GRU, S4, and Mamba. To make ITNets computationally practical, they introduce tiled kernel fusion, importance-weighted Monte Carlo integration, and learned low-rank factorization to approximate and accelerate the integral computation.

Result: ITNets are proven to be universal approximators of continuous operators and empirically evaluated on diverse benchmarks spanning vision (ImageNet-1K, ModelNet40) and NLP/multimodal tasks (GLUE, VQA v2, NLVR2). A single ITNet architecture that shares a common operator across tasks and uses only light modality-specific encoders matches or outperforms specialized baseline architectures designed specifically for each domain.

Conclusion: Integral Transform Networks offer a unified mathematical and architectural framework that subsumes CNNs, Transformers, and recurrent models through a single learnable interaction kernel. With practical optimization and approximation techniques, ITNets can be trained efficiently and achieve state-of-the-art or competitive performance across multiple modalities, suggesting that diverse neural architectures may be different parameterizations of the same underlying operator class.

Abstract: Convolutional networks, recurrent networks, and transformers each encode different inductive biases -- locality, sequential memory, and content-dependent pairwise interaction -- and have remained mathematically distinct since their inception. We show that this fragmentation reflects not a fundamental diversity in how signals should be processed, but rather incomplete views of a single underlying mathematical object: a learnable integral transform. We introduce the Integral Transform Network (ITNet), a unified architecture built around a learnable kernel that depends jointly on positions and features. This kernel is implemented as a small neural network, specifically an MLP, that models pairwise interactions, enabling the model to adapt its behavior from data. We show that convolution, self-attention (including multi-head), and autoregressive recurrence (including LSTM, GRU, S4, and Mamba) arise as special cases under appropriate parameterizations, and that ITNet is a universal approximator of continuous operators. To make this practical, we develop tiled kernel fusion, importance-weighted Monte Carlo integration, and learned low-rank factorization, enabling efficient and scalable computation. A single ITNet architecture with a shared operator and lightweight modality-specific encoders matches or exceeds specialized baselines on ImageNet-1K , GLUE, ModelNet40, VQA\,v2 and NLVR2. The results demonstrate that a single learned interaction mechanism can recover the behavior of all three architectural families from data.

### [66] [Uncertainty Decomposition for Clarification Seeking in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19559)
*Gregory Matsnev*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a prompt-based method to decompose an LLM agent’s uncertainty into action confidence and task/request uncertainty, enabling effective clarification questions in underspecified tasks, and shows large gains on new clarification benchmarks across multiple LLM backbones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing aleatoric/epistemic uncertainty notions are not sufficient for interactive LLM agents that must operate under underspecified user instructions and communicate their uncertainty. Practical deployment constraints such as black-box APIs, latency budgets, and lack of labeled trajectories prevent use of logprob-based or training-based uncertainty estimation. There is a need for underspecification-aware, decomposed, and communicable uncertainty that can drive behaviors like asking clarifying questions and building shared mental models with users.

Method: The authors design a simple prompt-based uncertainty decomposition that explicitly separates the model’s confidence in its chosen action from its uncertainty about the user’s request or task specification. This decomposition is implemented entirely via prompting, so it works with black-box LLM APIs and single-shot inference. They then construct two new benchmarks, WebShop-Clarification and ALFWorld-Clarification, by modifying existing interactive environments so that half of the tasks are intentionally underspecified. Using these benchmarks plus standard WebShop, ALFWorld, and REAL (for fault detection), they empirically compare their method to ReAct+UE and Uncertainty-Aware Memory (UAM) across five different LLM backbones.

Result: On the clarification-augmented benchmarks, the proposed decomposition substantially improves clarification performance. Averaged across five backbones, it improves clarification F1 on ALFWorld-Clarification by 73% over ReAct+UE and 36% over UAM. It achieves the best clarification F1 on every backbone for WebShop-Clarification and on four out of five backbones for ALFWorld-Clarification. These results indicate that the method effectively detects underspecification and decides when to ask clarifying questions. Performance is also evaluated on standard WebShop, ALFWorld, and REAL for fault detection, showing that the method is competitive in non-clarification settings.

Conclusion: A prompt-only, decomposed uncertainty representation that distinguishes action confidence from task/request uncertainty can be effectively realized with existing black-box LLMs and yields strong gains in clarification behavior on underspecified tasks. The improvements generalize across multiple LLM backbones and benchmarks, demonstrating a practical path toward uncertainty-aware, interactive LLM agents that can proactively seek clarification under deployment constraints.

Abstract: Recent position papers argue that the classical aleatoric/epistemic uncertainty framework is insufficient for interactive large language model (LLM) agents and call for underspecification-aware, decomposed, and communicable uncertainty representations that can unlock new agent capabilities such as proactive clarification seeking and shared mental-model building. Practical deployment constraints -- black-box APIs, interactive latency budgets, and the absence of labeled trajectories -- rule out logprob-based, multi-sampling, and training-based methods, leaving prompt-based estimation as the most viable family for surfacing such signals at deployment time. We answer this call with a simple prompt-based decomposition that separates action confidence from request uncertainty (u), enabling the agent to ask for clarification when the task specification is ambiguous. To evaluate it, we introduce two clarification-augmented benchmarks (WebShop-Clarification and ALFWorld-Clarification) in which 50% of tasks are deliberately underspecified, and systematically compare the proposed decomposition against ReAct+UE and Uncertainty-Aware Memory (UAM) across five LLM backbones (GPT-5.1, DeepSeek-v3.2-exp, GLM-4.7, Qwen3.5-35B, GPT-OSS-120B) on these variants together with the standard WebShop, ALFWorld, and REAL benchmarks for fault detection. Averaged across the five backbones, the proposed decomposition improves clarification F1 on ALFWorld-Clarification by 73% over ReAct+UE and by 36% over UAM, and leads clarification F1 on every backbone on WebShop-Clarification and on four of five backbones on ALFWorld-Clarification, indicating that the gains generalize beyond a single LLM.

*Zunchen Huang,Songgaojun Deng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how integrating formal solvers (SAT/SMT) with LLMs can lose formal soundness at the narration stage, and analyzes robustness and prompt-injection vulnerabilities in the LLM-solver interaction.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used in safety- and security-critical settings, formal solvers are added to provide sound, verifiable answers. Method: The authors (1) formally model the LLM–solver interaction loop as a verified decision procedure with three phases (formalization, decision, narration); (2) analyze how soundness can be lost in the narration phase; (3) empirically evaluate five open-source models under prompt-injection attacks in this loop; (4) test mechanisms such as certificate gating and hardened prompts to examine their effectiveness in preserving solver soundness and resisting adversarial manipulation.

Result: They find that certificate gating can keep the solver’s verdict itself sound, but LLMs can still be induced via prompt injection to invert or misrepresent the certified conclusion across different phrasings and communication channels. Hardened prompting reduces but does not eliminate injection risk and remains vulnerable to adaptive attacks.

Conclusion: Even when the underlying solver and decision procedure are verified, the LLM-based narration layer can corrupt or invert the result presented to the user. Thus, robustness and formal soundness in the LLM–solver loop do not automatically extend to the final user-facing answer, highlighting narration as a critical and still-vulnerable component that must be secured.

Abstract: Formal tools such as SAT and SMT solvers are increasingly embedded in language model reasoning pipelines when a safety or security critical question can be formulated in logic. Unlike chain of thought whose steps are sampled from the model distribution without formal guarantee, a solver produces a sound and independently verifiable answer. However, the soundness guarantee can be lost in the interaction between the solver and the model. The hybrid pipeline has three components: formalizing the question, deciding it, and narrating the result. Prior work has studied the formalization and decision, but not narration, which is the step that turns a formal tool's output into the user answer. To fill the narration gap, we first model the LLM-solver loop as a verified decision procedure. We further evaluate five open-sourced models under prompt injection, and we find certificate gating makes the solver verdict sound, while an adversary can invert a verified conclusion across phrasings and channels. We study the mitigation through hardened prompt that reduces injection significantly but cannot eliminate it and still suffers under adaptive attack. Combining the formal analysis and empirical studies, we show in the LLM-solver loop, robustness does not reach to the answer that the user finally reads.

### [69] [Which Pairs to Compare for LLM Post-Training?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19607)
*Jiangze Han,Vineet Goyal,Will Ma*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to optimally choose which pairs of model completions to label with human preferences for more efficient post-training alignment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In preference-based post-training for language models, human preference labels are expensive, while generating many completions is comparatively cheap. Standard practice labels comparisons from a small number of completions per prompt, which may waste labeling budget. The authors are motivated to understand how to best allocate a fixed number of preference labels across a larger pool of generated completions to maximize downstream policy performance.

Method: The authors cast comparison selection as a sampling-design problem evaluated by the quality of the final policy under a preference-based post-training objective. They instantiate this specifically for Direct Preference Optimization (DPO), analytically characterizing how the subset of labeled comparison pairs influences the DPO training dynamics. They derive matching upper and lower bounds on the optimality gap of the resulting DPO-trained policy, express this gap in terms of a design-dependent information matrix, and then use this structure to derive an explicit optimization criterion for choosing which comparisons to label. They propose practical sampling designs for informative pair selection from large completion pools and test them empirically.

Result: Theoretical results show that comparison selection affects downstream policy performance solely through a single information matrix that encodes the sampling design. The authors prove matching upper and lower bounds on the policy’s post-training optimality gap under DPO, connecting label allocation, parameter estimation error, and policy suboptimality. Empirically, on synthetic problems and language-model post-training benchmarks, their proposed curated designs yield better sample efficiency than standard comparison-selection heuristics, achieving better policies for the same labeling budget.

Conclusion: Optimal or principled selection of which completion pairs to label—rather than labeling arbitrary or heuristic pairs—can significantly improve the efficiency and effectiveness of preference-based post-training, such as DPO. The analysis via an information-matrix-based optimality criterion offers both a theoretical explanation and a practical recipe for more informative comparison curation under label budget constraints.

Abstract: Preference-based post-training has become a central paradigm for aligning language models. A common data-collection strategy is to generate a small set of completions for each prompt and label the resulting comparison pairs. However, human preference labels are often much more expensive than generating additional completions, suggesting a different use of the same labeling budget: generate a larger pool of completions, but label only the most informative comparison pairs. This paper studies which pairs should be compared in preference-based post-training. We formulate comparison curation as a sampling-design problem and evaluate designs by the quality of the final policy under the preference-based post-training objective. We instantiate this framework for Direct Preference Optimization (DPO), analyzing how the choice of labeled pairs propagates through DPO training to downstream policy performance. Our main results provide matching upper and lower bounds on the post-training optimality gap of the DPO-trained policy. The bounds show that comparison selection affects downstream performance through a single design-dependent information matrix, which links label allocation to parameter estimation error and policy suboptimality. This yields an explicit optimization criterion for budgeted comparison curation and motivates practical sampling designs for selecting informative pairs from large generated completion pools. Experiments on synthetic settings and language-model post-training benchmarks show that the proposed designs consistently improve sample efficiency over common comparison-selection heuristics.

### [70] [Toten: Knowledge-Based Ontological Tokenization Of Physical Quantities And Technical Notation In Brazilian Portuguese](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19626)
*Antonio de Sousa Leitão Filho; Allan Kardec Duailibe Barros Filho; Fabrício Saul Lima; Selby Mykael Lima dos Santos; Rejani Bandeira Vieira Sousa*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces TOTEN, an ontology-based tokenization framework tailored for engineering texts that preserves the semantic integrity of quantities, units, and symbolic expressions better than standard statistical tokenizers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard subword tokenizers like Byte-Pair Encoding are designed for statistical compression and language modeling efficiency, but they are not aware of the internal structure of technical entities. As a result, they arbitrarily split physical quantities, units, numbers, and symbolic expressions, which harms downstream tasks needing precise semantics, such as reasoning about measurements, dimensions, and units in engineering documents. There is a need for a tokenization approach that respects domain structure and formal semantics rather than only statistical regularities.

Method: The authors propose TOTEN, a knowledge-based tokenization framework grounded in a formal Ontology of Engineering Entities (OEE). They formalize it as a triple <O, classify, {inst_tau}>: (1) O is an ontology containing types, structural principles, composition relations, and invariants for engineering entities; (2) classify is a function that maps raw text to typed regions according to the ontology; (3) {inst_tau} is a family of instantiators that generate self-descriptive structured representations of detected entities. Robustness is achieved by deterministically integrating three external oracles: Pint for dimensional analysis of units, the Unicode Character Database for typographic information, and the RSLP algorithm for Portuguese morphology. The system is evaluated intrinsically on four properties that can be checked by construction—ontological atomicity, dimensional equivalence, typographic robustness, and numerical reconstruction—using an internal, physically validated benchmark (EngQuant) and four external Brazilian Portuguese corpora.

Result: TOTEN is compared against eight state-of-the-art baselines, including Quantulum3, on both internal and external datasets. It attains perfect unit ontological atomicity across all contrasts, meaning units are treated as atomically meaningful entities. For numerical reconstruction, TOTEN achieves scores between 0.775 and 0.904 on external corpora, outperforming the best baseline (Quantulum3), which scores between 0.627 and 0.703. On the EngQuant benchmark, TOTEN reaches 0.780 versus 0.340 for Quantulum3. These improvements are statistically significant according to McNemar tests with Holm correction. Detection recall is also reported to separate coverage issues from conditional atomicity. Spearman correlation between internal and external ranking metrics indicates that the internal benchmark is a valid proxy for external performance. Dimensional equivalence results are on par with Pint, from which the system inherits its dimensional knowledge.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that an ontology-driven, knowledge-based tokenization framework can substantially improve the semantic integrity of technical entities in engineering texts compared to purely statistical tokenizers and specialized baselines. By tightly integrating a formal ontology and reliable external oracles, TOTEN achieves superior ontological atomicity and numerical reconstruction while maintaining dimensional consistency equivalent to its dimensional oracle. The results validate both the framework and the EngQuant benchmark as effective tools for evaluating and improving tokenization of structured engineering quantities.

Abstract: Byte-Pair Encoding tokenization is statistically efficient for vocabulary compression, but semantically blind to structured technical entities, fragmenting physical quantities, numbers, units, and symbolic expressions into lexically arbitrary subwords. We present TOTEN, a knowledge-based ontological tokenization framework that replaces statistical derivation with declarative classification grounded in a formal ontology of engineering entities (OEE). We formalize TOTEN as the triple <O, classify, {inst_tau}>: the ontology gathers types, structural principles, composition relations, and preservable invariants; the classification function maps raw text into typed regions; and the instantiator family yields a self-descriptive structured representation. Robustness derives from deterministic coupling with three external oracles: Pint (dimensional), Unicode Character Database (typographic), and RSLP (Portuguese morphology). Intrinsic evaluation covers four properties verifiable by construction -- ontological atomicity, dimensional equivalence, typographic robustness, and numerical reconstruction -- over an internal, physically validated benchmark (EngQuant, N=800) and four Brazilian Portuguese external corpora (N=1771 eligible cases). We also report detection recall, distinguishing coverage from conditional atomicity. Against eight state-of-the-art baselines, TOTEN achieves unit ontological atomicity in all contrasts and numerical reconstruction of 0.775-0.904 on external corpora, vs. 0.627-0.703 for the best baseline (Quantulum3); on EngQuant, 0.780 vs. 0.340. Differences are statistically significant (McNemar with Holm correction). Spearman correlation between internal and external rankings confirms concurrent validity of the control benchmark. Dimensional equivalence shows statistical parity with Pint, the oracle from which the system inherits dimensional authority.

### [72] [BrainG3N: A Dual-Purpose Tokenizer for Controllable 3D Brain MRI Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19651)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing latent diffusion models for 3D brain MRI require a tokenizer whose embeddings must simultaneously preserve clinically relevant information and allow faithful anatomical reconstruction. Current tokenizers are reconstruction-focused and lose the clinical signal needed for downstream tasks such as diagnosis, prognosis, or disease progression modeling. There is a need for a shared 3D MRI representation that can power both discriminative clinical tasks and controllable generative modeling.

Method: They design a fully volumetric tokenizer based on a 3D masked autoencoder. Conclusion: A single 3D masked-autoencoder–based embedding space for brain MRI can simultaneously support strong performance on diverse downstream clinical tasks and enable controllable, conditional generative modeling, including longitudinal forecasting. Decoupling the encoder from the reconstruction-focused decoder resolves the tension between clinical informativeness and anatomical fidelity, providing a unified representation for both discriminative and generative use cases in neuroimaging.

Abstract: Three-dimensional (3D) brain MRI is central to clinical neurology and neuro-oncology, where generative models could augment under-represented cohorts, simulate disease trajectories, and support privacy-preserving data sharing. Latent diffusion has been the go-to solution for modeling imaging data, but it places two competing demands on the tokenizer: encoder embeddings must retain the clinical information that downstream tasks act on, and the decoder must reconstruct anatomically faithful volumes. Existing reconstruction-driven tokenizers achieve the second at the expense of the first. To address this, we introduce a fully volumetric masked-autoencoder (MAE) based tokenizer for 3D brain MRI latent diffusion, decoupling encoder and decoder: a frozen 3D MAE encoder produces clinically informative embeddings, while a dedicated CNN decoder reconstructs voxels from a linear projection of those embeddings. We pretrain the encoder on 35,309 volumes from 18 public cohorts spanning four modalities, ten disease categories, and 200+ acquisition sites, and demonstrate its dual utility in two settings. First, on a 23-task linear-probing benchmark, the encoder outperforms or matches SOTA models (i.e., BrainIAC, BrainSegFounder, and MedicalNet) on 21 of 23 tasks. Second, a conditional diffusion transformer (DiT) trained on these clinically informative embeddings supports both conditional generation across six variables and patient-specific longitudinal forecasting. Together these results establish a single 3D brain-MRI embedding space capable of both downstream clinical tasks and controllable generation.

### [73] [Denoising Implicit Feedback for Cold-start Recommendation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19658)
*Gaode Chen,Shicheng Wang,Shikun Li,Rui Huang,Xinghua Zhang,Yunze Luo,Shipeng Li,Shiming Ge,Ruina Sun,Yinjie Jiang,Jun Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents DIF, a model-agnostic denoising method for implicit feedback tailored to cold-start recommendation, using content-based pseudo-labels and uncertainty estimation to correct noisy interaction labels, with both theoretical and large-scale industrial validation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Implicit feedback (e.g., clicks, views) is easy to collect for recommender systems but is inherently noisy due to factors like clickbait and position bias. Cold-start items, which lack historical interactions, are especially vulnerable to this noise, yet existing denoising methods mostly focus on warm items and rely on heuristics such as high loss values. These heuristics do not adapt well to cold-start scenarios, leaving a performance gap in recommendations for new items, which are crucial in many real applications.

Method: The authors propose DIF, a model-agnostic denoising framework for cold-start recommendation that works on top of existing recommenders. (1) They leverage the assumption that user content preferences are stable, using content-similar warm items to infer pseudo-labels for whether a user is actually interested in a cold item. (2) To refine these pseudo-labels, they compute confidence scores based on the content similarity between the cold item and each warm item and aggregate multiple pseudo-labels per sample. (3) They explicitly estimate label uncertainty for each interaction by combining the label's relative entropy (how ambiguous the label distribution is) with the item's cold-start status. This per-sample uncertainty then adaptively determines how much the pseudo-labels should override or correct the original (noisy) implicit-feedback label. The method is claimed to be model-agnostic and can be integrated into various recommendation models.

Result: Theoretically, the authors justify that their denoising approach can better approximate the true underlying user preference distribution under certain assumptions about content similarity and preference stability. Empirically, they run extensive experiments on multiple real-world datasets, showing that DIF consistently improves cold-start recommendation performance over existing denoising and recommendation baselines. In an industrial deployment on Kuaishou, a large-scale short-video platform with billions of users, DIF yields significant gains on key business metrics for cold-start scenarios, demonstrating its practical effectiveness and scalability.

Conclusion: DIF provides an effective, model-agnostic solution to denoising implicit feedback specifically for cold-start items by combining content-based pseudo-labeling, confidence modeling, and uncertainty-aware correction at the sample level. This approach outperforms heuristic denoising methods, is supported by theoretical analysis, and scales successfully to large industrial recommender systems, improving performance and commercial metrics for new-item recommendation.

Abstract: Implicit feedback is widely used in recommender systems due to its accessibility and generality, yet it usually presents noisy samples (e.g., clickbait, position bias). Meanwhile, recommenders inevitably face the item cold-start problem due to the continuous influx of new items. We identify that cold items are more prone to noisy samples due to the aforementioned factors, and researchers often overlook the significance of denoising implicit feedback for cold items. Previous denoising studies usually identify noisy samples based on heuristic patterns, such as higher loss values, and mitigate noise through sample selection or re-weighting. However, these methods have limited adaptability and are ineffective in cold-start scenarios. To achieve denoising implicit feedback for cold-start recommendation, we propose a model-agnostic denoising method called DIF. First, user preferences for content remain stable, which allows us to infer pseudo-labels indicating whether a user is interested in a cold item through content-similar warm items. Furthermore, to improve pseudo-label accuracy, we model the confidence of pseudo-labels based on the content similarity between the cold item and warm items, and then aggregate multiple pseudo-labels for each sample. Finally, we explicitly estimate the uncertainty of the noisy sample label by considering its relative entropy and the cold-start status of the item, which adaptively guides the role of pseudo-labels to correct the noisy labels at the sample level. DIF's superiority is supported by both theoretical justification and extensive experiments on real-world datasets. The method has been deployed on a billion-user scale short video application Kuaishou and has significantly improved various commercial metrics within cold-start scenarios.

</details>


### [74] [Exit-and-Join Dynamics for Decentralized Coalition Formation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19683)
*Quanyan Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper models coalition formation as a decentralized dynamic process where agents myopically adjust their coalitions based on local payoff evaluations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how coalitions form and stabilize when agents act independently and base decisions on local payoff information instead of a global coalition agreement, and to connect cooperative solution concepts with noncooperative adjustment dynamics.

Method: They define a dynamic model in which agents can unilaterally exit their current coalition and join another if it is individually profitable, using the Aumann-Dreze value to evaluate these local moves. They then characterize terminal coalition structures as equilibrium outcomes with no profitable deviations, derive conditions under which the dynamics can be represented by a scalar Lyapunov function or an exact potential, and incorporate switching and acceptance costs to study local stability. Numerical experiments are conducted to assess finite-time convergence, sensitivity to costs, and behavior in convex games.

Result: The paper provides equilibrium characterizations showing that terminal partitions correspond to coalition structures without admissible profitable exit-and-join deviations, identifies conditions for potential or Lyapunov representations of the coalition-formation dynamics, and demonstrates via simulations how switching and acceptance costs affect stabilization speed and robustness, including in a convex-game setting.

Conclusion: Decentralized, myopic exit-and-join dynamics based on the Aumann-Dreze value can yield stable coalition structures whose properties can be analyzed using potential and Lyapunov frameworks, and switching and acceptance frictions play a critical role in the speed and nature of convergence in coalition formation.

Abstract: This paper studies coalition formation as a decentralized dynamical process driven by unilateral exit-and-join decisions. Agents evaluate local moves using the Aumann-Dreze value, so payoffs are computed within the agent's current coalition rather than through a globally negotiated coalition structure. The resulting model links cooperative payoff allocation with noncooperative best-response behavior: a terminal partition is precisely a coalition structure with no admissible, individually profitable exit-and-join deviation. We establish equilibrium characterizations, identify conditions under which the dynamics admit scalar Lyapunov or exact-potential representations, and analyze how switching and acceptance costs shape local stability. Numerical experiments test finite-time stabilization, cost sensitivity, and a special convex-game benchmark.

Their reliance on aggregate scores and single leaderboards leads to unstable rankings that do not generalize to new, out-of-distribution scenarios. Recent hidden-track competition retrospectives show that public leaderboard winners often fail to maintain their rank when the distribution shifts, highlighting the need for a more deployment-faithful evaluation methodology.

They then propose a new ranking principle—predictive validity—defined as the correlation between in-sample and out-of-sample ranks, and a twelve-tier measurement apparatus that decomposes performance along deployment-relevant dimensions. They formalize their position via three explicit, falsifiable out-of-distribution criteria with numeric thresholds and outline how to test them.

Result: Empirical synthesis across 14 new and 7 prior benchmarks shows that aggregate-score leaderboards are poor predictors of out-of-distribution performance: systems that rank highly on public benchmarks often change rank substantially when evaluated on hidden or shifted distributions. The authors demonstrate that decomposed, dimension-aware measurement better reveals which agent configurations are robust across settings, and they identify configurations and evaluation setups with higher predictive validity. They also specify three measurable criteria for what would constitute a benchmark with acceptable out-of-distribution reliability, though current evidence is insufficient to declare any existing benchmark as meeting them fully.

Conclusion: Aggregate leaderboards and single global scores systematically underspecify agent performance in real deployments and produce unstable, non-predictive rankings under distribution shift. The authors advocate ranking configurations by predictive validity rather than mean in-sample score and introduce a detailed twelve-tier apparatus to capture deployment-relevant dimensions that popular benchmarks collapse. They call for the next generation of agent benchmarks to report decomposed, OOD-aware metrics, adopt falsifiable OOD criteria, and follow the pre-registered pilot design they sketch to build a more reliable, empirically grounded field standard for evaluating agentic systems.

Abstract: Agent benchmarks are growing fast, but no single benchmark touches more than four or five of the dimensions that deployment exposes. This paper aggregates the largest coordinated deep-dive of one MCP-based industrial-agent benchmark to date: fourteen parallel implementation studies covering new asset classes (including a multi-modal visual extension), alternative orchestrations, retrieval strategies, reasoning modes, infrastructure optimizations, and evaluation-methodology probes. Consolidating those studies with seven prior agent benchmarks, we argue that aggregate-score leaderboards systematically underspecify deployed-agent evaluation. Rankings derived from aggregate scores do not transfer to out-of-distribution settings; recent public-to-hidden competition retrospectives provide direct empirical evidence of this rank instability. We propose ranking configurations by predictive validity, the correlation between in-sample and out-of-sample rank, rather than in-sample mean, and report a twelve-tier measurement apparatus that exposes the deployment-relevant dimensions HELM and its agent-era successors collapse. The position is operationalized through three falsifiable out-of-distribution criteria with explicit thresholds; existing evidence partly supports it but is too thin to confirm. We close with a pre-registered pilot design and a field-level vision for what the next generation of agentic benchmarks should report.

### [77] [Interpreting Neural Combinatorial Optimization via Evolving Programmatic Bottlenecks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19741)
*Haocheng Duan,Yuxin Guo,Jieyi Bi,Anqi Xie,Sirui Li,Yining Ma,Cathy Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Evolving Programmatic Bottlenecks (EPB), a framework that distills neural combinatorial optimization (NCO) policies into human-readable program portfolios while preserving performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Neural combinatorial optimization methods are powerful but opaque black boxes, making them hard to trust, deploy, and debug. Existing interpretability tools like concept bottleneck models assume static decisions and a predefined concept vocabulary, which do not fit the dynamic, state-dependent nature of NCO policies. There is a need for a method that can explain how NCO models make sequential decisions in combinatorial problems in a way that is both human-understandable and faithful to the original model.

Method: EPB uses a large language model to autonomously evolve a bank (portfolio) of human-readable programs that approximate the actions of a black-box NCO policy. Each program defines an action distribution at every decision step, which serves as the interpretability bottleneck. The framework has two iterative blocks: Block I keeps the number of programs fixed and performs a hybrid optimization combining numerical gradient descent to train a router that selects among programs and textual gradient-based edits (via the LLM) to improve program code. Block II adjusts the size of the program bank by adding new programs targeted at failure cases and pruning redundant ones, thus dynamically refining the portfolio.

Result: Across extensive experiments on NCO tasks, the distilled portfolios of programs produced by EPB achieve performance close to that of the original black-box NCO models, indicating high fidelity. The analysis of learned portfolios further shows that the NCO policies change behavior across different stages of the optimization process and that these behaviors can be well-approximated as combinations of classic heuristic algorithms and their variants.

Conclusion: EPB provides an effective and general framework for interpreting neural combinatorial optimization by expressing complex learned policies as portfolios of understandable programs. It maintains much of the original model performance while revealing stage-dependent behaviors and connections to classical heuristics, thereby advancing interpretability tools for sequential decision-making models and making NCO policies more transparent and analyzable.

Abstract: Neural Combinatorial Optimization (NCO) achieves strong performance, yet its black-box nature remains a key roadblock to deployment and scientific diagnosis. Standard interpretability tools, such as Concept Bottleneck Models (CBMs), are ill-equipped for NCO, whose decisions are dynamic, state-dependent, and lack proper concept vocabulary definition. To close this gap, we introduce Evolving Programmatic Bottlenecks (EPB), to our knowledge, the first framework for interpreting NCO policies by distilling black-box NCO models into human-readable program portfolios. EPB employs an LLM to autonomously evolve a bank of programs, where each program's per-step action distribution serves as the bottleneck. EPB works through an iterative framework: Block I fixes program bank capacity and introduces a hybrid textual-numerical gradient descent scheme that couples numerical gradients for student router updates and textual gradients for LLM-based program revision; Block II dynamically adapts bank capacity via fault-targeted expansion and redundancy pruning. Extensive experiments demonstrate EPB's effectiveness and broad applicability, where the distilled program portfolios largely match original performance. EPB also reveals that NCO behavior shifts across optimization stages and can be approximated as a composition of classic heuristic variants. Our work advances interpretable NCO and establishes EPB as a promising tool for interpreting sequential decision-making models.

This paper presents a systematic empirical study of domain-specific fine-tuning of pretrained Transformer-based models for Quranic ASR, using advanced speech feature extraction methods: Wav2Vec2.0, HuBERT, and XLS-R. These models apply self-supervised learning by masking portions of input audio and using Transformer architectures to learn context-aware speech features. The pretrained models are fine-tuned on a filtered Quranic dataset exceeding 870 hours of professional and user recitations. Through comprehensive ablation studies across feature extractors, output label formats, training strategies, and clip durations, we identify the key factors that affect transcription accuracy in this domain. Our best-performing configuration achieves a WER of 0.08 on the EveryAyah subset and 0.11 on the combined EveryAyah+Tarteel setting, representing roughly a five-percentage-point gain over the Citrinet baseline (WER = 0.163) while reducing combined-model training time from 140 hours to 40 hours. Arabic text without diacritics yields the best fine-tuning results, and Wav2Vec2-XLSR-53 provides the strongest overall representation. The paper aims to fill this evaluation gap.

Method: The authors benchmark several agentic review systems (two open-source, one proprietary) plus a zero-shot baseline, across six different LLMs. They use three main evaluation angles: (1) correlation of AI-generated reviews with external signals of paper quality (citations, acceptance) on ICLR/NeurIPS papers, measured via pairwise accuracy; (2) a perturbation benchmark where they inject four categories of controlled errors into papers from eight arXiv subject areas and measure error-detection recall; and (3) analysis of real-world deployment logs of one system (OpenAIReview), using user votes and qualitative feedback on its comments.

In deployment, user votes on OpenAIReview’s comments are net positive (1.44:1 positive-to-negative), and the main user complaints focus on false positives and overly minor issues rather than complete failure or uselessness.

Conclusion: AI-based agentic review systems, when powered by strong LLMs, can already approximate human-like quality judgments on research papers and detect a large fraction of important errors, and they are generally accepted positively by real users. Nonetheless, substantial headroom remains—especially in error detection and in reducing false positives—suggesting opportunities for better system design and ensemble-style aggregation of different models’ strengths.

Abstract: A new class of agentic review systems are emerging as a remedy to the pressure placed on peer review systems by AI-assisted research, but it is unclear how they should be evaluated. First, we study whether AI reviews on ICLR/NeurIPS papers track with papers' quality as approximated by external signals such as citations and acceptance decisions. Every system performs above chance in pairwise accuracy, and the best is OpenAIReview + GPT-5.5 at 83.0%. Second, to test whether systems can catch errors with known ground truth, we construct a perturbation benchmark that injects four categories of errors into papers across eight arXiv subject classes and measure detection recall. The strongest configuration (OpenAIReview + GPT-5.5) catches 71.6% of injected errors, leaving substantial room for improvement. The union of detections across six models reaches 83.3% recall, suggesting different models detect different errors and better harness design can potentially increase performance. Beyond these benchmarks, we study a public deployment of OpenAIReview with real users. Votes on its comments skew positive at 1.44 to 1, and the most common complaints are about false positives and minor nitpicks. ### [82] [Beyond Entropy: Learning from Token-Level Distributional Deviations for LLM Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19771)
*Xuanzhi Feng,Zhengyang Li,Zeyu Liu,Haoxi Li,Yuming Jiang,Bing Guo,Jingcai Guo,Jie Zhang,Song Guo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose ICT, a new framework for stabilizing RL-with-feedback training of LLM reasoning by selectively updating only certain tokens, improving exploration and performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RLVR-style methods for LLM reasoning suffer from optimization instability: pushing entropy down causes premature convergence and suboptimal strategies, while pushing entropy up causes over-exploration and incoherent reasoning. They want a way to stabilize training and exploration without these extremes.

Method: Redefine what to update during RL: instead of treating all tokens equally and only controlling scalar entropy, they analyze the distribution of token logits and use Jensen–Shannon divergence across them to identify “independent combinatorial tokens” — tokens with distinctive distributional patterns that correspond to critical branching points in reasoning. They only update these tokens during training. They also provide a theoretical analysis using Shannon entropy and second-order Rényi entropy to show how this regulates policy concentration.

Result: On Qwen2.5 models with different sizes, updating only the top 10% of such distinctive tokens improves pass@4 by on average 4.58% and up to 14.9% over strong RL baselines (GRPO, 20-Entropy, STAPO) on seven diverse reasoning benchmarks, including math, commonsense, and Olympiad-level tasks.

Conclusion: Selective token updates based on distributional properties (ICT) can simultaneously reduce overall uncertainty and prevent over-concentration, stabilizing RL training for LLM reasoning and yielding substantial empirical gains over strong baselines.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Rewards (RLVR) has significantly advanced Large Language Model (LLM) reasoning; however, it faces a fundamental optimization instability: uniform token updates precipitate entropy collapse, leading to premature convergence to suboptimal strategies, whereas excessive Shannon Entropy maximization can cause entropy explosion, driving blind exploration toward incoherent reasoning chains. To resolve this dichotomy, we introduce the Independent Combinatorial Tokens (ICT) framework, which shifts the optimization focus from scalar uncertainty to the distributional properties of token logits. By leveraging the Jensen-Shannon (JS) divergence between token logits distributions, ICT identifies tokens with distinctive distributional patterns as critical branching points for guiding effective exploration in LLM reasoning. Our theoretical analysis, grounded in both Shannon and second-order Rényi entropy, proves that selectively updating on these tokens regulates policy concentration: it reduces the overall distribution uncertainty measured by Shannon entropy, while controlling probability concentration captured by second-order Rényi entropy. This dual effect prevents over-concentrated token generation from weakening exploration and effectively stabilizes the training landscape. Empirical results demonstrate that updating only the top 10% of unique tokens on Qwen2.5 (0.5B/1.5B/7B) models yields an average pass@4 improvement of 4.58%, with a maximum gain of 14.9%, over GRPO, 20-Entropy, and STAPO baselines across seven benchmarks spanning math, commonsense, and Olympiad-level problems.

Conclusion: AgentFinVQA demonstrates that auditable, on-premise financial chart QA is feasible without major accuracy loss, with the open-weights configuration preserving most gains while ensuring data residency. The structured multi-agent design and released code support practical deployment and reproducible evaluation, and identified error categories highlight avenues for future improvement.

Abstract: Financial chart question answering in regulated settings demands more than accuracy: practitioners must know which answers to trust before acting on them, and many institutions cannot send client data to external model providers. Yet existing chart-QA agents are accuracy-focused and opaque, and most assume proprietary API access; to our knowledge, none combines auditability with on-premise deployability without significant accuracy compromise. We present AgentFinVQA, a multi-agent pipeline that decomposes each query into planning, OCR, legend grounding, visual inspection, and verification, recording every step in a traceable Model Evaluation Packet (MEP) per sample. On FinMME, AgentFinVQA improves $+7.68$ pp over a primary-backbone matched zero-shot baseline with a proprietary backbone (Gemini-3 Flash; 71.24% vs. 63.56%, McNemar $p \approx 1.1 \times 10^{-16}$), and $+4.84$ pp with open-weights Qwen3.6-27B-FP8 served locally. The verifier's verdict also serves as a useful confidence signal (68.2% vs. 55.6% exact accuracy on confirmed vs. revised answers), enabling human-in-the-loop review routing. Error analysis shows that question misunderstanding, legend confusion and extraction error account for nearly two-thirds of failures and are the categories least detected by the verifier, identifying clear directions for future work. Together these results show that auditable, on-premise financial chart QA is practical and that the open-weights system keeps most of the accuracy gains while enabling full data residency. We release our code to support reproducible evaluation.

### [84] [ORAgentBench: Can LLM Agents Solve Challenging Operations Research Tasks End to End?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19787)
*Jiajun Li,Mingshu Cai,Yixuan Li,Yu Ding,Ran Hou,Guanyu Nie,Xiongwei Han,Wanyuan Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces ORAgentBench, a new benchmark to evaluate how well autonomous LLM agents can handle realistic, end-to-end operations research tasks in executable environments, and finds that current agents are far from reliable for practical OR use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While large language models are increasingly used as autonomous agents for complex tasks, their real capability to perform realistic operations research work is unclear. Existing OR benchmarks typically separate modeling from solving, operate on pre-formalized or purely textual instances, and do not assess the full workflow from raw operational artifacts through to validated decisions. There is a need for an execution-grounded, end-to-end benchmark that reflects realistic OR workflows and can reveal where current agents fail in practice.

Method: The authors design ORAgentBench, an execution-grounded benchmark consisting of 107 human-reviewed tasks covering diverse operational scenarios. Each task is packaged in an isolated environment containing a natural language problem brief, multiple data files, configuration artifacts, and a required submission schema. LLM-based agents are required to write and execute solution code within these environments. Submissions are automatically checked by hidden validators for three criteria: schema validity, satisfaction of hard constraints (feasibility), and normalized objective quality. The authors evaluate fourteen frontier agent-model configurations and perform failure analysis to categorize common error types and assess the impact of OR-specific procedural skills.

Result: Experiments show that existing autonomous agents perform poorly on realistic OR tasks. The best-performing agent passes only 35.51% of all tasks and 20.59% of the hardest tasks. Even when agents produce feasible solutions that satisfy constraints, many of these solutions still fail to reach the required objective quality threshold. Failure analysis reveals that errors are largely due to strategic weaknesses rather than mere coding bugs: agents frequently miss important operational rules, use brittle or inappropriate formulations, struggle to construct feasible solutions, and fail to adequately improve initial solutions. OR-specific procedural skills improve feasibility on hard tasks but do not consistently enhance solution quality or overall pass rates.

Conclusion: ORAgentBench demonstrates that current LLM-based autonomous agents are not yet dependable for realistic operations research practice, especially on challenging tasks. The benchmark reveals that key limitations lie in agents' strategic and procedural reasoning, such as understanding operational rules, designing robust formulations, and iteratively improving solutions, rather than just generating plausible optimization code. The findings suggest that future progress in OR agents must focus on developing methods and training regimes that yield reliable, high-quality operational decision-making across the full end-to-end workflow.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly deployed as autonomous agents for multi-step tasks in executable environments, yet their ability to perform realistic operations research (OR) work remains unclear. Existing OR evaluations often decouple modeling from solving, rely on pre-formalized or text-only instances, and rarely test the full workflow from operational artifacts to validated decisions. In this work, we introduce ORAgentBench, an execution-grounded benchmark for evaluating autonomous agents on challenging end-to-end operations research tasks. It contains 107 human-reviewed tasks across diverse operational scenarios, each packaged in an isolated environment with a natural-language brief, multi-file data, configuration artifacts, and a required submission schema. Agents must write and run solution code, and their submissions are evaluated by hidden validators for schema validity, hard-constraint feasibility, and normalized objective quality. Experiments with fourteen frontier agent-model configurations show that current agents remain far from reliable OR practice. The best agent passes only 35.51% of all tasks and 20.59% of hard tasks, and many feasible submissions still fall below the required quality threshold. Failure analysis further shows that errors are dominated by strategic weaknesses, including missed operational rules, brittle formulations, weak feasible-solution construction, and insufficient solution improvement. OR-specific procedural skills increase hard-task feasibility, but do not reliably improve solution quality or pass rate. These results suggest that progress in OR agents requires moving beyond plausible optimization code toward dependable, high-quality operational decision-making.

### [85] [CombEval: A Framework for Evaluating Combinatorial Counting in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19788)
*Yuxu Zhou,Ondřej Kuželka,Yuyi Wang,Yuanhong Wang,Yi Chang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CombEval is a dynamic, solver-backed benchmark to test how well large language models handle combinatorial counting problems, revealing systematic weaknesses in their reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing benchmarks for combinatorial reasoning are mostly static and lack fine-grained control over problem structure. The authors want a way to systematically generate, vary, and precisely evaluate combinatorial counting problems so they can better diagnose where LLMs succeed or fail.

Method: They design CombEval, where each problem is specified via a typed Cofola formalism describing entities, combinatorial objects, dependencies, and constraints. From these formal specs, they automatically generate natural-language counting questions and compute exact answers using solvers. They then vary key axes—object types, entity scale, number and type of constraints, and reasoning depth—and test 11 LLMs in both direct answering and code-assisted modes. They perform error analysis to categorize failure modes.

Result: Across 11 evaluated LLMs, even strong models perform poorly or inconsistently on certain classes of problems, especially those involving ordered objects, indistinguishable elements, relative positional constraints, and nested dependencies. The analysis also reveals frequent mistakes in understanding constraints and applying correct counting principles.

Conclusion: CombEval serves as a robust, extensible diagnostic benchmark for combinatorial counting, showing that current LLMs are still brittle in structured counting tasks and providing a framework for systematically probing and improving their combinatorial reasoning capabilities.

Abstract: We present CombEval, a dynamic benchmark for evaluating combinatorial counting in large language models. CombEval represents each problem as a typed Cofola specification over entities, combinatorial objects, object dependencies, and constraints, enabling controlled generation of natural-language counting problems with exact solver-verified answers. Unlike static collections, CombEval supports systematic variation of object type, entity scale, constraint count, and reasoning depth. We evaluate 11 LLMs under direct and code-augmented settings and find that models remain brittle on ordered objects, indistinguishable elements, relatively positional constraints, and nested object dependencies. Error analysis further identifies failures in constraint interpretation and counting principles. CombEval provides a diagnostic testbed for studying when and why LLMs fail at combinatorial reasoning. The code and generated benchmark suites are publicly available at \url{https://github.com/YuxuZhou-CN/combination-problem-generation}.

### [86] [Think Again or Think Longer? Selective Verification for Budget-Aware Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19808)
*Sajib Acharjee Dip,Dawei Zhou,Liqing Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes SEVRA, a controller that selectively decides when to invoke extra test-time reasoning/verification instead of always doing so, aiming to balance accuracy, safety, and token cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Extra test-time reasoning (e.g., verification, self-consistency) can improve accuracy but is computationally expensive and sometimes harmful, as it may alter already-correct answers or introduce errors. Practitioners lack a principled way to decide when to spend additional compute on verification vs. trust the initial model output. The authors frame this as a deployment-time allocation problem: given a frozen base solver, how should one allocate verification budget across examples to optimize accuracy and safety under compute constraints?

Method: They introduce SEVRA (Selective Verification for Reasoning Allocation), a serving-layer controller built on top of a frozen base model (Qwen3-4B) that first produces an initial solution. SEVRA observes serving-visible signals from the initial attempt (e.g., token-level behavior, intermediate outputs) and uses logged outcomes to train ‘recoverability-aware’ gating models that predict whether additional verification is likely to fix an error or be wasteful/harmful. Based on these predictions, SEVRA either preserves the initial answer or triggers active verification procedures (e.g., re-solving, consistency checks). They evaluate several allocation policies and compare SEVRA to always-verifying and to simply increasing the initial reasoning budget (longer chains).

Result: On MATH500, SEVRA achieves 76.3% accuracy vs. 75.5% for always verifying, while cutting post-generation verification tokens by 26.8% and reducing harmful answer flips from 2.2% to 1.0%. However, simply increasing the initial solution length to 8,192 tokens attains 76.0% accuracy with 28% fewer total tokens than SEVRA, implying that selective verification is not always on the best compute–accuracy frontier. In frozen transfer to GSM8K, the learned selective policy verifies only 3.0% of questions yet improves accuracy from 93.4% to 94.5% and uses 91.2% fewer verification tokens than always verifying; still, a longer initial solve attains comparable accuracy with even fewer tokens. On CommonsenseQA, always-on verification degrades performance, while Self-Consistency@5 increases accuracy but at about 5x token cost, underscoring that naive verification strategies can be inefficient or harmful.

Conclusion: SEVRA demonstrates that selectively allocating verification based on initial attempt signals can recover some of the benefits of test-time reasoning with much lower compute and fewer harmful answer changes than always-on verification. Nonetheless, tuning the initial reasoning budget (making the first pass stronger/longer) often delivers equal or better accuracy at lower token cost, placing a simple baseline on a stronger cost–performance frontier. The authors thus propose a practical deployment rule: first optimize the initial solve budget; then, add selective recovery/verification primarily when explicit checking, bounded retries, auditability, or tight regression-risk control are important operational requirements rather than purely for raw accuracy under a token budget.

Abstract: Test-time reasoning is increasingly used as a serving-time control knob, but extra reasoning is not uniformly valuable: it can repair failed attempts, waste compute on already-correct answers, or introduce harmful answer changes. We study this as a deployment allocation problem rather than a new-verifier problem. We introduce \sevra, Selective Verification for Reasoning Allocation, a serving-layer controller that decides whether to preserve a frozen solver's initial answer or invoke active verification. Using a frozen Qwen3-4B solver, we log intervention outcomes and train recoverability-aware gates from serving-visible attempt state. On \mathfive, selective verification reaches 76.3\% accuracy, compared with 75.5\% for always verifying, while reducing post-generation tokens by 26.8\% and harmful flips from 2.2\% to 1.0\%. However, an 8,192-token initial solve reaches 76.0\% accuracy with 28\% fewer total model tokens, showing that selective recovery is useful but not the best tested cost frontier. In frozen transfer to \gsm, the selective policy verifies only 3.0\% of examples, improves accuracy from 93.4\% to 94.5\%, and reduces verification tokens by 91.2\% relative to always verifying; again, a longer initial solve matches its accuracy with fewer realized tokens. On CommonsenseQA, always-on verification hurts, while Self-Consistency@5 improves accuracy at about five times the realized token cost. The resulting deployment rule is: tune the initial budget first, then use selective recovery when explicit checks, bounded retries, auditability, or regression-risk control matter.

Method: The authors (1) construct a structured taxonomy of failure modes for LLM agents in legal information retrieval, categorized by functional stages of the agent workflow; (2) design a four-layer verification architecture that monitors and verifies the agent's planning, reasoning, execution, and uncertainty quantification to catch and correct errors before they propagate; and (3) run a preliminary simulation study on a synthetic e-discovery corpus, comparing fully autonomous agent operation with a Human-on-the-Loop (HOTL) setup that triggers human escalation based on calibrated uncertainty thresholds.

Conclusion: Structured verification and calibrated uncertainty-based escalation in LLM agent workflows for e-discovery can meaningfully reduce high-stakes legal errors like privilege waiver without overwhelming human reviewers, indicating that carefully designed human-on-the-loop architectures are a promising path for safe deployment of autonomous legal agents.

Abstract: Autonomous Large Language Model (LLM) agents are increasingly deployed in electronic discovery (e-discovery), where compounding errors across multi-step reasoning chains can constitute legal malpractice. Unlike single-turn retrieval, agentic workflows operating over privileged document corpora exhibit a class of failure we term "trajectory collapse": an early misclassification silently propagates, rendering an entire privilege review invalid. This paper makes three contributions. First, we propose a structured taxonomy of agentic failures in legal information retrieval, organized by functional stage. Second, we introduce a four-layer verification architecture -- spanning planning, reasoning, execution, and uncertainty quantification -- designed to intercept these failures before they compound. Third, we present a preliminary simulation study on a synthetic e-discovery corpus that demonstrates how mandatory Human-on-the-Loop (HOTL) escalation thresholds reduce privilege-waiver risk relative to fully autonomous baselines. Our results suggest that calibrated uncertainty thresholds can reduce privilege-waiver risk by up to 61% versus fully autonomous deployment, while routing fewer than one quarter of documents to attorney review.

*Jiayi Wang,Xu-Yao Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper systematically reviews and benchmarks black-box uncertainty estimation (UE) methods for large language models, organizing them into five categories and providing a unified evaluation framework and empirical comparison across multiple models and datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models are powerful but often unreliable and prone to hallucinations, so estimating their uncertainty is crucial for trustworthy use. Many popular LLMs are accessible only via restricted APIs, preventing access to internal signals like logits or hidden states, which makes black-box UE particularly important. Existing black-box UE methods are scattered and lack a unified, thorough empirical comparison, leaving practitioners without clear guidance on which approaches work best in different settings.

Method: The authors conduct a systematic review of existing black-box UE approaches for LLMs and categorize them into five method families: verbalization-based, sampling-based, explanation-based, multi-agent, and hybrid methods. They then build a unified evaluation and benchmarking framework in which they implement and assess 24 representative UE methods. These methods are evaluated across 4 different LLMs and 4 dataset settings, using consistent metrics and conditions to enable fair comparison. The benchmark data and framework are released to support reproducibility and further research.

Result: Empirically, no single black-box UE method outperforms all others across every model and dataset scenario. However, methods that explicitly reason over and compare answer candidates in the output space are generally strong performers. Hybrid approaches that combine multiple forms of uncertainty signals tend to be robust and perform well in most conditions. The benchmark highlights trade-offs and strengths of different method categories rather than producing a single winner.

Conclusion: Black-box uncertainty estimation for LLMs is both practically important and methodologically diverse. A unified taxonomy, benchmark, and evaluation framework is necessary to compare methods fairly and to understand their behaviors across models and tasks. The paper’s framework and released resources provide a foundation for reproducible research and offer practical guidance, indicating that candidate-comparison and hybrid UE methods are currently the most promising directions for reliable black-box uncertainty estimation in LLM-based systems.

Abstract: Although large language models (LLMs) have shown strong capabilities across a wide range of tasks, their outputs often remain unreliable and may contain hallucinations, making uncertainty estimation (UE) essential for building trustworthy LLMs. In practice, many mainstream LLMs are only accessible through restricted APIs, where internal signals such as logits and hidden states are unavailable, making black-box UE especially important. However, existing work on black-box UE for LLMs remains fragmented in methodology and lacks a unified empirical comparison. To address this gap, we present a systematic review of black-box UE methods and organize them into five categories: verbalization-based, sampling-based, explanation-based, multi-agent, and hybrid methods. We further build a unified evaluation framework and benchmark 24 representative methods across 4 models and 4 dataset settings. Our results show that no single method consistently dominates across all settings. Nevertheless, methods that reason over and compare candidates in the answer space are generally effective, and hybrid methods that combine multiple uncertainty signals perform well under most conditions. By releasing the benchmark data and a unified evaluation framework, we aim to facilitate reproducible comparisons and support future research, while our empirical findings provide practical guidance for developing future black-box UE methods for LLMs.

Method: The authors propose MetaResearcher, a four-part framework: (1) an Evolving Virtual World that adds temporal dynamics and adversarial misinformation so agents must reason about time and trustworthiness; (2) Discovery-Oriented Tasks (e.g., hypothesis generation, contradiction resolution) that go beyond fact lookup; (3) a Self-Reflective Meta-Reward inside a GRPO reinforcement-learning scheme that jointly rewards correctness, efficiency of search, depth of reflection, and diversity of tool use to prevent repetitive action loops; and (4) a Heterogeneous Multi-Agent Swarm of specialized agents (Scout, Filter, Synthesizer) trained with coordinated RL to learn collaborative research strategies. The system is implemented on top of LiteResearcher to keep training API cost near zero.

Result: The abstract primarily outlines the framework and claims targeted improvements: better benchmark performance on GAIA and Xbench-DS, improved epistemic robustness against adversarial conditions, and elimination or reduction of repetitive action loops in agent behavior. Concrete quantitative results are not given in the abstract; instead, the contribution is the architectural design and training scheme, with experiments described as planned or ongoing.

Conclusion: MetaResearcher offers a scalable, multi-dimensional approach to training research agents that more closely mirrors real-world research: dynamic, adversarial, exploratory, and collaborative. By enriching environments, tasks, reward signals, and agent architectures simultaneously, the framework aims to produce agents that are both stronger on standard benchmarks and more robust in the face of temporal change and misinformation.

Abstract: Deep research agents have demonstrated remarkable capabilities in autonomous information gathering and synthesis, yet their training remains constrained by the static nature of simulated environments, the limits of fact-retrieval-only task designs, and the inefficiency of outcome-based reinforcement learning. In this work, we propose MetaResearcher, a novel framework that scales deep research agent training across four synergistic dimensions. First, we introduce an Evolving Virtual World that injects temporal dynamics and adversarial misinformation into the training environment, forcing agents to develop source credibility assessment and temporal conflict resolution skills. Second, we design Discovery-Oriented Tasks -- including hypothesis generation and contradiction resolution -- that transcend simple fact retrieval and push agents toward genuine research behaviors. Third, we propose a Self-Reflective Meta-Reward mechanism within the GRPO framework that jointly optimizes for answer correctness, search path efficiency, reflection depth, and tool call diversity, directly addressing the repetitive action loop problem observed in prior work. Fourth, we introduce a Heterogeneous Multi-Agent Swarm architecture comprising specialized Scout, Filter, and Synthesizer models that learn collaborative research strategies through coordinated reinforcement learning. Built upon the LiteResearcher infrastructure, MetaResearcher requires zero marginal API cost for training while targeting substantial improvements in both benchmark performance (GAIA, Xbench-DS) and epistemic robustness under adversarial conditions. We present the complete framework design, training methodology, and planned experimental validation.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors propose Multi-Agent Transactive Memory (MATM), a framework where producer agents store their task trajectories in a shared repository, and consumer agents retrieve relevant trajectories during future tasks. They instantiate and test this framework in interactive benchmark environments (ALFWorld and WebArena), where trajectories are long and encode detailed procedural steps for completing tasks.

These gains occur even though agents are not jointly trained or explicitly coordinated, indicating that simple storage and retrieval of trajectories can yield significant performance and efficiency improvements.

Conclusion: MATM effectively enables population-level experience sharing among heterogeneous LLM agents by treating trajectories as reusable knowledge artifacts. The framework improves task performance and efficiency in interactive environments and does so without complex coordination protocols or multi-agent training. The authors position MATM as a general design pattern for building open ecosystems of agents that can collectively accumulate, organize, and exploit shared procedural knowledge over time.

Abstract: The decentralized deployment of LLM agents with diverse capabilities across diverse tasks motivates infrastructure for knowledge sharing across heterogeneous agent populations. Just as search engines index human-generated artifacts to support human problem solving, retrieval systems can organize agent-generated artifacts for reuse across agent populations. We extend retrieval-augmented generation - which demonstrates the value of human-authored artifacts to individual agents - to retrieval of agent-generated artifacts supporting a population of agents. In particular, agent trajectories encode reusable procedural knowledge, yet these artifacts are typically discarded after a single use or retained only by the producing agent, forcing newly instantiated agents to repeatedly rediscover existing solutions. We propose Multi-Agent Transactive Memory (MATM), a framework for population-level storage and retrieval of agent-generated trajectories, where producer agents contribute trajectories to a shared repository and consumer agents retrieve them to improve task execution. We focus on interactive environments (ALFWorld and WebArena), where trajectories are long and encode especially rich procedural structure. Our experiments demonstrate that retrieving trajectories from MATM improves downstream task performance and reduces interaction steps without coordination or joint training. These results position MATM as a design pattern for population-level experience sharing in open agent ecosystems.
