### [92] [eCNNTO: A Highly Generalizable ConvNet for Accelerating Topology Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19921)
*Shengbiao Lu,Xiaodong Wei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces eCNNTO, an element-based convolutional neural network framework to accelerate density-based topology optimization by predicting near-optimal element densities and skipping most finite element iterations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Density-based topology optimization is computationally expensive because it requires many iterations of finite element analysis, especially for high-resolution meshes. Prior work using per-element deep belief networks sped up optimization but ignored spatial correlations, sometimes producing disconnected or poor-quality structures. There is a need for a faster, data-efficient, and more generalizable surrogate that respects spatial relationships between elements.

Method: The authors design an element-based CNN with residual connections (eCNNTO) that takes as input the density history of elements in space and predicts their near-optimal densities, enabling early termination of the standard TO loop. Unlike per-element DBNs, the CNN processes spatial neighborhoods, capturing correlations and improving structural connectivity. A novel training strategy is proposed: instead of using early-stage density histories as training data, the model is trained on final-stage histories, which better represent converged patterns and allow reduction of required training samples. The trained eCNNTO model is then used within TO to forecast densities and skip most intermediate finite element iterations across various boundary conditions, loads, geometries, meshes, and with non-design regions.

Result: In numerical experiments on 2D and 3D topology optimization problems, eCNNTO shows strong generalization across different problem setups and mesh resolutions. The method achieves substantial speedups by reducing the number of required optimization iterations: up to about 90% reduction in iterations for 2D problems and about 97% for 3D problems, while maintaining acceptable structural quality and avoiding the disconnected features seen in earlier DBN-based approaches.

Conclusion: eCNNTO effectively accelerates density-based topology optimization by combining element-level modeling with CNN-based spatial learning and a carefully chosen training regime. It overcomes limitations of earlier DBN-based surrogates, requires only modest training data, generalizes well to a wide range of TO settings, and drastically reduces the number of optimization iterations in both 2D and 3D with preserved design quality.

Abstract: This work proposes an element-based Convolutional Neural Network (CNN) to accelerate density-based Topology Optimization (TO), termed eCNNTO. TO generally undergoes a large number of iterations, where finite element analysis is performed in every iteration, leading to the efficiency bottleneck especially when dense meshes are used to achieve high-resolution designs. To address this limitation, eCNNTO is proposed to build upon Kallioras et al. (2020), where a Deep Belief Network (DBN) was trained for every element to predict its near-optimal density from its early history, thereby skipping the great majority of iterations and significantly accelerating the TO procedure. However, the method lacks spatial correlations among neighboring elements and may lead to disconnected features in the final structure. The proposed method employs CNN with residual connections to address this issue. On top of it, a novel training strategy is introduced to further enhance the optimization efficiency, where the training dataset consists of the final stage density histories rather than early ones. This change can also help reduce the required training data size. eCNNTO requires only a small dataset to train and yet it can be generalized to problems with largely different boundary conditions, loading cases, design domain geometries, mesh resolutions, as well as non-design domains. In the end, the generalization capabilities and efficiency of eCNNTO are demonstrated through a variety of examples in two and three dimensions, achieving up to 90% and 97% reduction of iterations, respectively.

</details>


### [93] [The Tao of Agency: Autotelic AI, Embedded Agency and Dissolution of the Self](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19924)
*Aritra Sarkar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper explores autotelic AI, where agents generate their own goals, and argues that the core challenge is defining and relativizing the agent’s own self-boundary, integrating insights from intrinsic motivation, embeddedness, and a proposed quantum and LLM-based framework.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most AI assumes goals are externally provided. The authors want to investigate what happens when agents autonomously generate their own goals (autotelic AI). This requires rethinking how agents define objectives, how they are individuated from their environment, and how they understand themselves as entities to which goals can be assigned.

Method: The paper conceptually unifies several strands—intrinsic motivation, resource-based priors, causal-interventional learning, homeostasis, and embeddedness—into a single framework for autotelic agency. It analyzes the notion of embeddedness to show how it individuates agents but also leads to non-unique partitions of self. The authors then extend this framework in three directions: (1) a quantum formulation making the agent–environment cut a physical phenomenon, (2) a philosophical interpretation using non-dual contemplative traditions, and (3) an illustrative implementation using an LLM-based agent architecture.

Result: The analysis shows that embeddedness is necessary but not sufficient for autotelic agency, because the same dynamics can be partitioned into multiple valid agent–environment decompositions, each implying a different candidate self. Consequently, the fundamental issue becomes how an autotelic agent generates and relativizes its own boundary, rather than just how it generates goals. The proposed framework accommodates this insight and illustrates how it might be treated in quantum, philosophical, and LLM-based settings.

Conclusion: The central challenge of autotelic AI is constructing a self-model with a boundary that the agent both believes in (for action) and can see through (for understanding). Embeddedness provides a basis for individuation but introduces non-uniqueness of the agent’s boundary, revealing that multiple candidate selves can coexist within the same dynamics. By integrating diverse perspectives and suggesting quantum and LLM-based extensions, the paper proposes a unified conceptual framework for studying and eventually implementing truly self-motivated, self-defining AI agents.

Abstract: Most artificial intelligence systems are built on the assumption that goals are exogenous and specified by the designer. Exploring what happens when an agent begins generating its own goals opens the field of autotelic AI. Agents are expected not merely to pursue objectives but to discover them. In this article, we trace its consequences through intrinsic motivation, resource-driven priors, causal-interventional learning, homeostasis, and embeddedness; the last of which is found to be a necessary but not sufficient condition for autotelic agency. Embeddedness individuates the agent at the cost of revealing that the individuation is non-unique, such that the same dynamics admit many valid partitions, each defining a different candidate self.
  The deepest problem with autotelic AI is therefore not how the agent generates goals, but how it generates and relativizes the self to which the goals are assigned. The agent must believe in its own boundary in order to act, and see through that boundary in order to understand. We consolidate these developments into a single framework and extend it along three directions: a quantum formulation in which the agent-environment cut becomes physical, a philosophical reading against non-dual contemplative traditions, and a concrete LLM-based agentic instantiation.

### [94] [PhysDrift: Bridging the Embodiment Gap in Humanoid Co-Speech Motion Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.19935)
*Zhangzhao Liang,Xiaofen Xing,Mingyue Yang,Wenlve Zhou,Xiangmin Xu*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most co-speech gesture generation systems are built on human motion representations (e.g., SMPL-X) and only later retarget motions to robots. This human-centric approach causes an embodiment gap: motions that look good in human space become less expressive, less diverse, and less synchronized with speech once mapped to a specific humanoid robot with different kinematic and physical constraints. The authors aim to achieve expressive, speech-aligned robot gestures that are truly executable and dynamically stable on physical humanoids.

Method: 1) Analyze the embodiment gap by studying how human-to-robot retargeting affects motion diversity and speech-motion synchronization. 2) Propose IK-EER, a retargeting and curation framework that optimizes inverse kinematics for the humanoid while explicitly preserving prosody-aligned timing between speech and motion. This yields a curated dataset of robot-native gestures. 3) Propose PhysDrift, a co-speech motion generation framework that directly predicts humanoid joint trajectories from speech, trained on the curated robot-native data and regularized with physical constraints to improve dynamic stability and smoothness. The system avoids intermediate human-body representations at both training and inference.

Result: Empirical studies show that standard retargeting preserves only coarse semantics but compresses gesture diversity and harms prosody synchronization. With IK-EER and PhysDrift, the authors achieve better speech-motion alignment, higher motion diversity, greater physical plausibility, smoother trajectories, more efficient inference, and improved real-time performance on humanoid robots, validated by quantitative metrics and real-world robot deployment.

Conclusion: Human-centric co-speech pipelines are fundamentally limited by an embodiment gap between human motion manifolds and robot constraints. By curating robot-native motion data with prosody-aware IK optimization and training an embodiment-aware generator that outputs executable joint trajectories, the authors demonstrate that maintaining embodiment consistency throughout the pipeline leads to more expressive, synchronized, and physically robust robot co-speech behaviors.

Abstract: Humanoid robots require co-speech motions that are not only expressive and speech-aligned, but also physically executable under embodiment constraints. Existing co-speech generation pipelines are predominantly human-centric: motions are first generated in human-body representations such as SMPL-X and subsequently retargeted to humanoid robots. In this work, we identify a fundamental embodiment gap in this paradigm, where the mismatch between human motion manifolds and humanoid embodiment constraints disrupts embodiment consistency during motion transfer and physical execution. Through extensive analysis, we show that although retargeting can preserve coarse motion semantics, it significantly compresses motion diversity and weakens prosody-motion synchronization, limiting expressive humanoid behaviors. To address this problem, we first propose IK-EER, a prosody-preserving humanoid motion curation framework that jointly optimizes kinematic feasibility and speech-motion temporal alignment during retargeting. Building upon the curated robot-native motion dataset, we further introduce PhysDrift, an embodiment-aware co-speech motion generation framework that directly predicts executable humanoid joint trajectories from speech without relying on intermediate human-body representations. Unlike conventional human-centric pipelines, PhysDrift maintains embodiment consistency throughout both training and inference while incorporating physical regularization to stabilize robot motion dynamics. Extensive experiments and real-world humanoid deployment demonstrate that embodiment-aware robot-native generation substantially improves speech-motion alignment, physical plausibility, motion smoothness, inference efficiency, and real-time interaction capability.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current dexterous robotic manipulation in the real world needs heavy human supervision and manual algorithm engineering, which limits scalability and general physical intelligence. The authors aim to create a repeatable, automated feedback loop that moves coding agents from simulation-only success to effective real-world robotics research with minimal human effort.

Method: The authors design ENPIRE, a harness framework comprising four core modules that together realize an automated physical feedback routine. The Environment (EN) module handles automatic scene reset and task outcome verification. The Policy Improvement (PI) module launches policy refinement based on performance signals. The Rollout (R) module executes candidate policies on one or multiple robots, potentially in parallel, to collect data. The Evolution (E) module lets coding agents analyze logs, consult the literature, and modify training infrastructure and algorithm code to address failure modes. This architecture creates a closed-loop optimization process where coding agents iteratively test, diagnose, and improve manipulation policies on real hardware with minimal human intervention.

Result: Using ENPIRE with frontier coding agents, the system autonomously trains dexterous manipulation policies that reach a 99% success rate on several challenging real-world tasks, including organizing a pin box, fastening a zip tie, and performing tool use. Performance further improves and training accelerates when multiple agents are deployed on a fleet of robots to parallelize rollouts and experimentation. The framework also supports fair ablations across different training recipes and coding-agent variants because the optimization loop is standardized and automated.

Conclusion: The study concludes that ENPIRE provides a practical and scalable abstraction for bringing coding agents into real-world robotics, turning manipulation learning into a controllable, automated optimization process that minimizes human involvement. This suggests a viable path toward autonomously advancing physical-world robotics research by tightly integrating coding agents with structured environment control, evaluation, and iterative improvement on robot hardware.

Abstract: Achieving dexterous robotic manipulation in the real world heavily relies on human supervision and algorithm engineering, which becomes a central bottleneck in the pursuit of general physical intelligence. Although emerging coding agents can generate code to automate algorithm search, their successes remain largely confined in digital environments. We conjecture that the missing abstraction to automate robotics research is a repeatable feedback loop for real-world policy improvement: reset the scene, execute a policy, verify the outcome, and refine the next iteration. To bridge this gap, we introduce ENPIRE, a harness framework for coding agents that instantiates this physical feedback routine with four core modules: an Environment module (EN) for automatic reset and verification, a Policy Improvement module (PI) that launches policy refinement, a Rollout module (R) to evaluate policies with one or multiple physical robots operating in parallel, and an Evolution module (E) in which coding agents analyze logs, consult literature, improve training infrastructure and algorithm code to address failure modes. This closed-loop system transforms real-world manipulation learning into a controllable optimization procedure, minimizing human effort while allowing fair ablations across training recipe and agent variants. Powered by ENPIRE, frontier coding agents can autonomously train a policy to achieve a 99% success rate on challenging, dexterous manipulation tasks, such as organizing a pin box, fastening a zip tie, and tool use, a process that further accelerates when we dispatch an agent team on a robot fleet. Our results suggest a practical and scalable path toward deploying coding agents to autonomously advancing robotics in the physical world.

Main category: cs.AI

  <summary>Details</summary>

Attempts to explore more broadly often run into reward hacking because reward models are unreliable under distribution shift. The authors aim to enable broader, scalable exploration in complex embodied environments without suffering from reward hacking.

Method: 1) Use embodied world models as the testbed, where physical plausibility and task completion serve as stringent constraints. 2) Introduce “Reward as an Agent,” an agentic reward framework that actively evaluates generated behaviors to provide more robust, adaptive reward signals under distribution shift, reducing reward hacking. 3) Propose Dynamic-Aware Rollout Diversification via DynDiff-GRPO to explicitly expand exploration in the action space, diversifying trajectories, broadening state-action coverage, and encouraging richer embodied behaviors beyond conservative rollouts. 4) Combine the agentic reward verifier with the diversified exploration scheme to run RL on top of world models.

Result: The combined approach substantially mitigates reward hacking while significantly improving accuracy and performance across multiple open-source embodied world models. It demonstrates that broader exploration can be made effective and stable when supported by robust, agentic reward verification.

Conclusion: Broader, dynamics-aware exploration in world-model-based RL is not fundamentally limited by exploration itself but by the reliability of verification and reward under distribution shift. By treating reward as an agent and coupling it with explicit rollout diversification, one can safely scale exploration, reduce reward hacking, and achieve better performance in complex embodied environments.

Abstract: While RL has become a promising tool for refining world models, existing methods largely rely on conservative rollouts near the training distribution, limiting exploration, behavioral diversity, and richer dynamic discovery. In this work, we challenge this conservative paradigm. We argue that the core limitation is not exploration itself, but the lack of reliable verification strategies to support broader exploration. Without reliable verification, expanded exploration becomes highly susceptible to reward hacking, where policies exploit imperfect rewards without achieving genuine improvement. To evaluate this motivation, we instantiate our method in embodied world models, where physical plausibility, and task completion provide a rigorous testbed for scalable RL under complex dynamics. On the verification side, we introduce Reward as an Agent, an agentic reward framework that actively evaluates generated behaviors to provide robust reward signals and mitigate reward hacking under distribution shifts. On the exploration side, we introduce Dynamic-Aware Rollout Diversification through DynDiff-GRPO, which explicitly expands action-space exploration to diversify trajectories, broaden state-action coverage, and encourage richer embodied behaviors beyond conservative rollout regimes. By unifying Reward as an Agent with DynDiff-GRPO, we enable RL on a more reliable reward foundation with substantially diversified sampling, effectively mitigating reward hacking while yielding significant accuracy gains across multiple open-source world models, thereby demonstrating that broader exploration can scale successfully when grounded in robust verification.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors empirically evaluate two orchestration architectures—DAG Plan and Execute, and ReAct—on 208 realistic, production-inspired enterprise scenarios. They test them at three scales: Persona (<10 agents), Department (20–80 agents), and Enterprise (~200 agents). They measure precision, robustness, latency, and correctness, particularly under scaling stress.

Result: They find that orchestration performance is dominated by scale rather than intrinsic task complexity. Both DAG Plan and Execute and ReAct perform well at small scales, but performance degrades at enterprise scale due to increasing noise and inefficiency in agent discovery, which especially harms simple tasks. DAG Plan and Execute yields better precision and structured parallelism at smaller scales but incurs higher coordination overhead that becomes problematic at large scale. ReAct shows greater robustness in the face of failures by handling them incrementally. The introduced Task Manager reduces latency for high-priority queues by 14–75% and improves the correctness of handling related events by more than 20 percentage points at enterprise scale.

Conclusion: The main limitation in scaling enterprise multi-agent AI is not task difficulty but orchestration under large numbers of agents and events, particularly noisy agent discovery. DAG-style planning is advantageous at small scales, but its overhead hurts at enterprise levels, where more incremental approaches like ReAct fare better. A dedicated Task Manager that handles continuous operation—through priority inference, event merging, and preemption—can substantially improve responsiveness and correctness, especially for high-priority work in large-scale enterprise settings.

Abstract: Enterprise AI aims to move toward continuous event monitoring, detection, and action across specialist agents, yet existing multi-agent systems largely assume discrete request-response workflows and remain underexplored at enterprise scale. We evaluate DAG Plan and Execute and ReAct across 208 production-derived enterprise scenarios spanning Persona (<10 agents), Department (20-80), and Enterprise (200) scales, and introduce a Task Manager for continuous operation via priority inference, related-event merging, and preemption. Results show that scale, not task complexity, dominates orchestration performance: both architectures perform well at small scale but degrade at enterprise scale as agent discovery noise becomes the primary bottleneck, with simple tasks degrading more sharply than complex ones. DAG Plan and Execute offers higher precision and structured parallelization at smaller scales, but its higher overhead worsens at enterprise scale; ReAct is more robust by handling failures incrementally. The Task Manager reduces high-priority queue latency by 14-75% and improves related-event correctness by over 20 percentage points at enterprise scale.

*Minsu Kim,Se-Young Yun*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reinforcement learning for formal theorem proving usually relies on sparse, binary pass/fail rewards, despite proof assistants internally having rich structured information. This mismatch wastes potentially useful feedback and limits learning efficiency and reliability. The authors aim to exploit the full structure provided by proof assistants to get dense, sound rewards that better guide model training for formal reasoning.

Method: They treat the Lean proof assistant as a symbolic process oracle. Model-generated proof attempts are parsed into sequences of tactics. Lean’s elaboration engine is used to (1) verify which individual tactic steps are locally sound and (2) identify the first failing step. From this they derive dense, type-theoretically grounded credit signals at the tactic and token level. These signals are integrated into a GRPO-style reinforcement learning objective with new schemes such as first-error propagation and first-token credit assignment, which combine outcome-level and process-level advantages. They test this framework using models like STP-Lean and DeepSeek-Prover-V1.5.

Result: Across multiple experimental settings, tactic-level supervision using Lean-derived structured rewards outperforms outcome-only RL baselines. The models trained with this dense feedback achieve higher performance on formal reasoning benchmarks including MiniF2F and ProofNet, demonstrating better theorem-proving capability and training efficiency.

Conclusion: Symbolic proof assistants like Lean can function not just as final verifiers but as process-level reward oracles during training. By exposing and leveraging their internal, fine-grained verification signals, one can build RL frameworks that combine the scalability of language models with the rigor and reliability of symbolic verification, leading to more effective and trustworthy formal reasoning systems.

Abstract: While reinforcement learning from verifiable rewards (RLVR) typically has relied on a single binary verification signal, symbolic proof assistants in formal reasoning offer rich, fine-grained structured feedback. This gap between structured processes and unstructured rewards highlights the importance of feedback that is both dense and sound. In this work, we demonstrate that the Lean proof assistant itself can serve as a symbolic process oracle, supplying both outcome-level and fine-grained tactic-level verified feedback during training. Proof attempts are parsed into tactic sequences, and Lean's elaboration marks both locally sound steps and the earliest failing step, yielding dense, verifier-grounded credit signals rooted in type theory. We incorporate these structured rewards into a GRPO-style reinforcement learning objective with first-error propagation and first-token credit methods that balances outcome- and process-level advantages. Experiments with STP-Lean and DeepSeek-Prover-V1.5 show that tactic-level supervision outperforms outcome-only baselines in most settings, delivering improvements on benchmarks such as MiniF2F and ProofNet. Beyond empirical gains, our study highlights a broader perspective: symbolic proof assistants are not only verifiers at evaluation time, but can also act as process-level reward oracles during training. This opens a path toward reinforcement learning frameworks that combine the scalability of language models with the reliability of symbolic verification for formal reasoning.

### [100] [Residual-Space Evolutionary Optimization via Flow-based Generative Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20084)
*Zhuo Cao,Lena Krieger,Fernanda Nader,Xuan Zhao,Hanno Scharr,Ira Assent*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a model-agnostic framework that combines flow-based generative editing with evolutionary algorithms by optimizing in a residual space learned via conditional flow matching, enabling effective exploration–exploitation trade-offs for non-differentiable or black-box objectives in data editing tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing data editing methods with generative models usually rely on differentiable objectives and gradient-based optimization. In flow-based generative models, edits are applied via forward/backward integration and many practical objectives are non-differentiable or black-box, so gradient-based methods are not applicable or perform poorly. There is a need for a principled way to perform optimization-based editing in such settings that can handle non-differentiable objectives, preserve instance-specific features, and still explore diverse edits.

Method: The authors propose residual-space evolutionary optimization, a framework that leverages conditional flow matching (CFM) to decompose data representations into condition-controlled factors and instance-specific residuals. They conduct optimization directly in this residual space using evolutionary algorithms. The framework separates the search into two regimes: (1) self-pollination, which performs local exploitation by refining residuals while preserving key features of individual instances; and (2) cross-pollination, which enables broader exploration by recombining residuals across different, heterogeneous samples to generate diverse candidates. This approach is model-agnostic and suited to non-differentiable or black-box objectives.

Result: On the MorphoMNIST benchmark for counterfactual generation, the method is shown to balance target alignment (meeting desired edits), instance preservation (maintaining identity/structure of the original sample), and diversity of generated counterfactuals better than competing approaches or baselines. The authors also demonstrate the approach on crystal structure data, showing that the same exploration–exploitation decomposition works beyond images and can handle real-world scientific data with complex, potentially black-box objectives.

Conclusion: Residual-space evolutionary optimization provides a general, model-agnostic way to perform data editing with flow-based generative models in the presence of non-differentiable or black-box objectives. By operating in a disentangled residual space given by conditional flow matching and combining local refinement (self-pollination) with global recombination (cross-pollination), the framework achieves a useful balance between target alignment, instance preservation, and diversity. The successful applications to both image-based (MorphoMNIST) and scientific (crystal) data suggest that the method is broadly applicable to counterfactual generation and generative editing tasks across domains.

Abstract: Data editing with generative methods typically requires differentiable objectives and gradient-based search. However, these assumptions break down in flow-based settings, where edits are performed through forward and backward integration and often involve non-differentiable or black-box objectives. We introduce residual-space evolutionary optimization, a model-agnostic framework that addresses this gap by combining flow-based generative editing with evolutionary algorithms. Building on the observation that conditional flow matching (CFM) can disentangle condition-controlled factors from instance-specific residuals, our framework directly operates in residual space and separates two complementary search regimes: self-pollination performs local exploitation through feature-preserving residual refinement, and cross-pollination promotes broader exploration by recombining residuals across heterogeneous samples. As a proof of concept, we validate on MorphoMNIST, a benchmark dataset for counterfactual generation, and on crystal data, demonstrating that this exploration--exploitation decomposition provides a useful mechanism for balancing target alignment, instance preservation, and diversity, and extends beyond images to real-world scientific domains.

### [102] [ScaffoldAgent: Utility-Guided Dynamic Outline Optimization for Open-Ended Deep Research](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20122)
*Zhibang Yang,Xinke Jiang,Yuzhen Xiao,Ruizhe Zhang,Yue Fang,XinFei Wan,Zhengxing Song,Yuxuan Liu,Yuheng Huang,Xu Chu,Junfeng Zhao,Yasha Wang*

TL;DR: ScaffoldAgent is a dynamic, utility-guided outline optimization framework for open-ended deep research that improves long-form report quality and factual grounding.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Open-ended deep research needs systems that can iteratively retrieve information and write long-form reports, using outlines as structural scaffolds. Existing approaches either fix the outline early or adjust it with simple local heuristics, causing scaffold drift as new information is added and providing delayed or weak feedback about whether outline changes improve the final report. A better method is needed to continually and systematically optimize the outline as research progresses.

Method: The paper formulates outline evolution as a structured decision process with three operations on the outline: Expansion (adding detail), Contraction (merging or pruning content), and Revision (restructuring). It introduces ScaffoldAgent, which uses these operations to dynamically adjust the outline during research. A utility-guided feedback mechanism evaluates each operation based on estimated downstream benefits measured through retrieval gain, structural coherence, and the quality of trial generations. This utility signal is then used to decide which outline node to modify, which operation to apply, and when to stop modifying the outline during inference.

Result: On DeepResearch Bench and DeepResearch Gym benchmarks, ScaffoldAgent outperforms existing deep research agents, yielding better long-form report generation and stronger factual grounding. The experiments demonstrate consistent gains across tasks, indicating that dynamic, utility-guided outline optimization is more effective than static or heuristic-based approaches.

Conclusion: Dynamically optimizing the outline as a decision process with explicit operations and utility-guided feedback mitigates scaffold drift in open-ended deep research. ScaffoldAgent shows that treating outline management as a central, learnable component leads to more coherent and factual long-form reports. This framework provides a principled way to coordinate retrieval, evidence organization, and generation, and it can be extended to other complex information-seeking and writing tasks.

Abstract: Open-ended deep research (OEDR) requires systems to acquire knowledge through multi-round retrieval and generate coherent long-form reports. The outline plays a central role as a structural scaffold that coordinates retrieval, evidence organization, and generation. However, existing methods either fix the outline before writing or refine it with local heuristics, leading to scaffold drift under continuous information accumulation and delayed feedback for evaluating outline modifications. We propose ScaffoldAgent, a utility-guided dynamic outline optimization framework for OEDR. ScaffoldAgent models outline evolution as a structured decision process with three operations: Expansion, Contraction, and Revision, enabling controlled updates to the report scaffold. It further introduces a utility-guided feedback mechanism that estimates the downstream value of each outline operation from retrieval gain, structural coherence, and trial-generation quality. The resulting utility signal guides node selection, operation scheduling, and termination during inference. Experiments on DeepResearch Bench and DeepResearch Gym show that ScaffoldAgent consistently improves long-form report generation and factual grounding over existing deep research agents.

</details>

### [104] [RACL: Reasoning-Agent Control Layers for Continuous Metaheuristic Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20142)
*Antón Asla Manzárraga*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes RACL, a reasoning-agent control layer that sits on top of existing metaheuristic optimizers, using an LLM-style agent to monitor and adjust the search process without changing constraints or replacing the base solver.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Metaheuristics are powerful but often rely on hand-crafted, opaque control rules and parameter settings that are hard to tune, adapt or explain for different instances and operational conditions. There is a need for a systematic way to learn, validate and explain algorithmic control policies for these optimizers while preserving existing business constraints and solver implementations.

Method: RACL introduces a reasoning agent that observes the optimizer’s operational memory and run-time behavior, formulates bounded hypotheses about useful interventions, applies controlled changes to the search process (e.g., intensification/diversification, memory management), evaluates their impact, enforces guardrails to maintain feasibility and safety, and consolidates successful interventions into reusable control policies. The agent uses execution logs and live interactions (in the proof-of-concept, via Codex) to iteratively refine and ultimately distill stable algorithmic control rules, demonstrated here on a vehicle routing metaheuristic (e.g., ALNS) without changing the core optimizer or business constraints.

Result: In vehicle routing experiments, RACL consistently improves or matches baseline control policies: it improves or ties the default Operational Memory Policy in 21/21 feasible instances and improves or ties a non-reasoning Stagnation-Triggered Policy (STP) in 18/21 feasible cases. Average cost improvement of RACL vs STP is -0.641%. In a focused Sevilla-9/10 runtime sample, RACL reduces average cost by -8.337% compared to a Fixed policy and -1.605% compared to STP, with no material additional computational overhead. A policy proxy was used post hoc to make evaluation reproducible, though the proof-of-concept used Codex as the live reasoning agent.

Conclusion: RACL shows that a reasoning agent can effectively sit on top of an existing metaheuristic to learn and manage algorithmic control rules, improving solution quality without changing the solver’s core logic or constraints and without incurring tangible runtime penalties. The approach demonstrates a practical path to integrating LLM-style reasoning into traditional optimization pipelines for better, explainable and automatically discovered search control policies.

Abstract: This paper introduces RACL, a Reasoning-Agent Control Layer for metaheuristics. RACL places a reasoning agent above an existing optimizer. The agent does not replace the optimizer and does not modify business constraints. Instead, it controls the optimizer's internal search behavior by observing operational memory, reasoning over past behavior, formulating bounded hypotheses, testing interventions, evaluating outcomes, applying guardrails, consolidating useful policies and explaining its decisions. The experiment uses vehicle routing as a testbed, but the contribution is not a new routing solver, a particular ALNS configuration or a specific set of routing rules. The contribution is the RACL method: a way for a reasoning agent to discover, validate, consolidate and explain algorithmic control rules for a metaheuristic. In the current experimental setting, RACL improves or ties the Operational Memory Policy in 21 of 21 feasible cases and improves or ties a non-reasoning Stagnation-Triggered Policy in 18 of 21 feasible cases, with an average RACL vs STP cost delta of -0.641%. In the Sevilla-9/10 runtime sample, RACL improves average cost by -8.337% versus Fixed and -1.605% versus STP without showing material computational overhead. During the proof-of-concept, Codex was used as an in-the-loop reasoning agent observing executions, interpreting logs and proposing live bounded interventions. The policy proxy was later used only to make quantitative evaluation reproducible.

### [106] [Modularity-Free Conflict-Averse Training for Generalized PINNs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20156)
*Heejo Kong,Beomchul Park,Sung-Jin Kim,Seong-Whan Lee*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why high-capacity PINNs fail under conflict-averse optimization and introduces ModSync, a structural regularization method to maintain beneficial interaction between loss components, achieving better PDE-solving performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Training PINNs is unstable because different loss terms (PDE residual, boundary conditions) interfere. New conflict-averse optimizers help but break down as models get larger. The authors want to understand this capacity-related failure and design a method that works reliably for overparameterized networks.

Method: They first empirically and conceptually study a failure mode in large PINNs where the network self-organizes into separate modules that handle different loss terms with minimal interaction (functional modularity). This blocks convergence to good multi-objective (Pareto-stationary) solutions. To fix this, they propose Modular-Sparsity Synchronization (ModSync), which augments conflict-averse training with a structural penalty that discourages task-exclusive connections while preserving and promoting connections that contribute to multiple tasks. ModSync adaptively shapes network sparsity patterns to sustain cross-objective coupling.

Result: Across multiple PDE benchmark problems, applying ModSync to conflict-averse PINN training avoids the capacity-induced modularity failure. Models maintain stronger interaction between residual and boundary objectives, converge more reliably, and attain lower PDE error than prior PINN training methods, achieving new state-of-the-art accuracy on the tested tasks.

Conclusion: Overparameterization can cause PINNs trained with conflict-averse optimization to split into isolated task-specific modules that hurt multi-objective convergence. By explicitly regularizing the network structure to suppress task-exclusive pathways and synchronize sparsity across objectives, ModSync stabilizes training, preserves beneficial coupling between loss terms, and significantly improves PDE-solving accuracy, offering a more reliable route to scaling up PINNs.

Abstract: Physics-informed neural networks (PINNs) have become a powerful framework for solving PDEs by embedding physical laws into differentiable objectives. Despite their advances, training PINNs remains fragile: recent conflict-averse optimization schemes alleviate gradient interference between residual and boundary losses, but we show that their effectiveness deteriorates as model capacity increases. In this paper, we identify a capacity-induced failure mode, where overparameterized networks undergo functional modularity, self-partitioning into task-exclusive modules that suppress cross-objective interaction and hinder convergence toward Pareto-stationary points. To address this issue, we propose a novel framework, Modular-Sparsity Synchronization (ModSync), which integrates structural optimization into conflict-averse training by penalizing task-exclusive connections while preserving interaction-promoting pathways. Extensive experiments across diverse PDE benchmarks demonstrate that ModSync consistently prevents capacity-driven failures, sustains robust cross-objective coupling, and achieves state-of-the-art accuracy. Codes are available at \url{https://github.com/heejokong/ModSync}.

Method: They introduce HISR, a hypergraph-based implicit semantic reasoning framework. This disentangled embedding mitigates over-smoothing typical of standard graph embeddings and supports robust inference when parts of the transmitted information are missing or corrupted.

Conclusion: Modeling semantic information with hypergraphs and context-specific subspaces yields richer and more resilient semantic representations for communication systems. HISR can better capture complex multi-entity relations and maintain high semantic inference accuracy even with partial information loss, making it a promising direction for next-generation semantic-aware communication architectures.

Abstract: Semantic-aware communication has emerged as a transformative paradigm for next-generation communication systems, shifting the fundamental goal from transmitting bit-level symbols to reliably recovering and understanding the semantic meaning of information. Previous studies have demonstrated that representing the semantic content of source messages as graph-based structures can significantly improve communication efficiency and the accuracy of semantic inference at the receiver. However, existing solutions typically employ graphs that capture only pairwise relationships, thereby neglecting higher-order implicit correlations commonly observed in real-world scenarios, such as group interactions, multi-entity associations, and complex relational contexts. This limitation reduces semantic expressiveness and makes semantic inference susceptible to ambiguity and performance degradation, particularly under noisy or corrupted channel conditions. To address these issues, this paper proposes a novel hypergraph-based implicit semantic reasoning framework, HISR, which leverages hypergraphs to represent complex multi-entity relationships among semantic knowledge entities. In HISR, entities and their associated higher-order relations are mapped into dedicated semantic subspaces tailored to distinct relational contexts. This design not only disentangles diverse semantic interactions to mitigate the over-smoothing effects commonly found in traditional graph embedding methods but also enables robust semantic inference even when partial information loss occurs during transmission. Numerical results show that the proposed HISR achieves up to a 36.6% improvement in implicit semantic interpretation accuracy over the state-of-the-art benchmarks.

### [108] [Apparent Psychological Profiles of Large Language Models are Largely a Measurement Artifact](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20205)
*Jelena Meyer,David Garcia,Dirk U. Wulff*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LLMs’ apparent stable psychological traits measured by human-designed instruments are mostly artifacts caused by systematic response biases, not true model properties.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Human psychological instruments are being repurposed to profile LLMs for usability, safety, and as stand-ins for human subjects, but it is unclear whether such profiles are valid for models instead of being measurement artifacts.

Method: The authors administered multiple personality and risk-preference measures, including self-report style scales and behavioral tasks, to 56 instruction-tuned LLMs and to large human comparison samples, then used a formal psychometric framework and variance decomposition to analyze how much between-entity variation reflects real traits versus systematic response bias, and how instrument characteristics like response orthogonality affect reliability.

Result: They found: (1) Between-model differences are dominated (81–90%) by a simple directional response bias toward particular scale ends or labeled options, unlike humans (9–16%). (2) This bias decreases but persists as model capability increases. (3) Apparent instrument reliability for LLMs is largely explained by “response orthogonality,” the share of items where trait direction and bias direction oppose each other. (4) Apparent model profiles shift with item content and can be engineered via item selection.

Conclusion: Psychological profiles inferred for LLMs using human-focused instruments mainly reflect instrument-induced response bias rather than genuine model traits. Therefore such instruments may lack validity for LLMs, and new evaluation tools explicitly designed around response orthogonality are needed to meaningfully assess model psychology-like properties.

Abstract: Psychological instruments designed for humans are increasingly used to assign large language models (LLMs) stable psychological profiles that affect their usability, safety assessment, and use as proxies for human participants in research. Using a formal psychometric framework, we show that these profiles are largely a measurement artifact. Administering a battery of personality and risk-preference instruments spanning self-reports and behavioral tasks to 56 instruction-tuned LLMs alongside large human reference samples, we report four findings. First, differences between models are driven not by the traits an instrument targets but by a directional response bias, a tendency to respond toward one end of the scale, or one labeled option, regardless of item content; a variance decomposition attributes 81-90% of between-model variation to this bias, against 9-16% in humans. Second, the bias declines with model capability but is not eliminated by it. Third, because bias rather than trait drives responding, an instrument's apparent reliability is almost entirely predicted by its response orthogonality, a term we coin for the proportion of items for which trait and bias point in opposite directions. Fourth, the profile a model appears to have shifts with the items used and can be manufactured through item selection. These results demonstrate that the apparent psychological profiles of LLMs are artifacts of the instrument used to measure them, not properties of the models themselves. As instruments borrowed from human psychology are rarely fully orthogonal and may inherently lack validity for LLMs, we call for dedicated assessments centered on response orthogonality.

</details>


### [109] [Beyond Accuracy: Measuring Logical Compliance of Predictive Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20208)
*Guillaume Olivier Delplanque,Pierre Genevès,Nabil Layaïda,Zephirin Faure*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose Rule Violation Score (RVS), a metric to measure how well machine learning models obey logical rules, independently of predictive accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Common ML metrics (accuracy, error, ranking) only evaluate how close predictions are to ground truth labels and ignore whether predictions respect logical or domain constraints. In safety-critical domains like healthcare or finance, violating known rules can be as serious as being inaccurate, but there is no standard way to measure such logical consistency. The authors want a general, model-agnostic metric to quantify rule compliance.

Method: They define Rule Violation Score (RVS), which measures how much a model’s predictions violate a given set of logical rules. RVS distinguishes between hard rules (must hold) and soft rules (statistical tendencies), is applicable to any predictive model over a relational vocabulary, and can be computed through SQL queries automatically generated from Horn clauses. RVS is used both to evaluate trained models and to assess the logical consistency of datasets and the quality of the rules themselves.

Result: They apply RVS to three benchmarks in knowledge graph link prediction and relational regression, covering rule-based, embedding-based, and neuro-symbolic models. Experiments show that models with similar predictive accuracy can differ greatly in RVS, i.e., in how well they obey the rules. This exposes behavioral differences not visible with standard predictive metrics.

Conclusion: Predictive accuracy alone is insufficient to characterize model quality in settings where logical or domain constraints matter. RVS provides a complementary, model-agnostic metric for logical consistency, usable via automatically generated SQL over Horn rules, and is useful both for evaluating models and diagnosing datasets and rule sets. It reveals important differences between models that conventional metrics miss.

Abstract: Machine learning models are predominantly evaluated through predictive performance metrics such as ranking quality, prediction error, or classification accuracy. While these metrics effectively quantify how closely predictions match the ground truth, they do not assess whether model outputs respect predefined logical or domain-specific constraints. In high-stakes applications, including healthcare, finance, and autonomous systems, logical consistency can be as critical as predictive accuracy, yet no standard metric captures this dimension. We introduce the Rule Violation Score (RVS), a complementary evaluation metric that quantifies the extent to which a predictive model respects a given set of logical rules, independently of predictive accuracy. RVS treats hard rules (strict constraints) and soft rules (statistical regularities) differently, can be evaluated on any dataset and on any predictive model expressed over a relational vocabulary, and can be computed using SQL queries that are automatically generated for Horn rules. Beyond evaluating models, RVS can also evaluate the logical consistency of training datasets and help identify poorly defined rules. We evaluate RVS on three benchmarks covering knowledge graph link prediction and relational regression, including rule-based, embedding-based, and neuro-symbolic predictive models. Our results demonstrate that two models achieving comparable predictive accuracy can exhibit substantially different levels of logical compliance, revealing differences in model behavior that standard metrics fail to capture.

### [111] [QMFOL: Benchmarking Large Language Model Reasoning via Quantifiable Monadic First-Order Logic Test Case Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20227)
*Xinyi Zheng,Ling Shi,Tianlong Yu,Yongxin Zhao,Lorenz Goette,Kailong Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces QMFOL, a framework for automatically generating first-order logic reasoning benchmarks with controllable logical complexity, and uses it to build QMFOLBench to systematically evaluate LLM reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing reasoning benchmarks for large language models lack fine-grained, quantifiable control over logical complexity and often trade off semantic diversity against logical consistency, making it hard to diagnose and compare models’ deductive reasoning capabilities as they improve.

Method: They design QMFOL, which programmatically builds monadic first-order logic structures using controlled conjunction and disjunction patterns. The framework parameterizes depth, width, label type (True/False/Unknown), and distractors to control complexity. These formal structures are then converted into natural-language reasoning tasks by LLMs. An external logical prover performs round-trip verification to ensure the natural-language instances preserve the intended logical structure and labels. Using these tools, they generate QMFOLBench, a dataset of 2880 instances spanning 960 logically and semantically diverse configurations.

Result: On QMFOLBench, six large reasoning models and two LLMs show systematically decreasing accuracy and increasing computation cost as logical complexity (e.g., depth, width, distractors) grows. Models are consistently better on True-labeled problems than on False or Unknown ones, and their performance is sensitive to superficial semantic changes even when the underlying logical form is fixed.

Conclusion: QMFOL provides a scalable, automated, and logically verified way to generate deductive reasoning benchmarks with tunable complexity. QMFOLBench demonstrates that current models remain brittle under controlled increases in logical difficulty and semantic variation. The framework enables more precise, diagnostic evaluation of LLM reasoning and can support future development of more robust reasoning systems.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have made significant progress in reasoning, particularly in deductive reasoning, which is crucial for high-stakes decision-making. As models improve, evaluation benchmarks should evolve to keep pace. However, existing benchmarks lack fine-grained control over logical complexity and struggle to balance semantic diversity with logical consistency.
  To address these issues, we propose QMFOL, an automated framework for generating monadic first-order logic reasoning tasks with quantifiable and controllable complexity. It constructs formal logical structures using conjunction and disjunction patterns, enabling precise control over reasoning depth, width, label types, and distractors. These structures are then translated into natural language via LLMs, with logical consistency ensured through round-trip verification using an external prover. Based on our framework, we build QMFOLBench, a benchmark comprising 2880 instances with 960 configurations across diverse logical and semantic dimensions. Evaluations on six large reasoning models (LRMs) and two LLMs show that performance degrades and computational overhead increases with rising logical complexity. Models perform better on True-labeled tasks than on False or Unknown ones, and exhibit sensitivity to semantic variation. Overall, QMFOL offers a scalable and reliable approach for constructing deductive reasoning benchmarks with controllable complexity, enabling more precise evaluation of reasoning capabilities in modern language models.

### [112] [Thermodynamic Measure of Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20231)
*Ishanu Chattopadhyay*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Defines and thermodynamically grounds a quantitative measure of intelligence as the lawful amplification of rare but valid futures, showing recursive self-simulation is (almost) necessary and sufficient to achieve it.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clarify whether and how intelligence can be objectively measured across very different systems (from matter to machines to humans), and identify the architectural features that make high intelligence physically possible. The authors want a universal, thermodynamically grounded definition instead of ad-hoc or anthropocentric ones.

Method: They formalize a domain where some futures are rare but valid under physical constraints. They define a thermodynamic measure, "rare-valid lift", quantifying how much a system raises the probability of those rare-valid futures compared to passive dynamics. They then model intelligent systems as agents that build internal models of the world including themselves, leading to recursive self-simulation in which the agent simulates futures that include its own actions. Using this formalism, they derive: (1) a necessity theorem that high rare-valid lift is impossible without high-fidelity internal simulation of rare-valid futures, and (2) a conditional near-sufficiency theorem stating that if the internal simulation faithfully tracks rare-valid futures and contains an effective policy, then the system can achieve near actuation-limited optimal lift.

Result: They obtain a precise link between internal architecture and thermodynamic performance: high thermodynamic intelligence (high rare-valid lift) requires accurate internal representation of rare-valid futures, and with such accurate self-simulation plus a good policy, the system nearly maximizes the lift allowed by its actuators. This produces a scale on which any system—passive physical processes, engineered controllers, AI models, humans, or information engines—can be assigned a measurable level of intelligence based on its effect on the distribution of physically admissible futures.

Conclusion: Intelligence can be meaningfully measured as the lawful amplification of rare but valid futures, captured by a thermodynamic quantity called rare-valid lift. Recursive self-simulation is not just a plausible feature but, under the paper’s assumptions, is necessary and nearly sufficient for high thermodynamic intelligence. This yields a unified, physically grounded framework for quantifying and comparing intelligence across a wide variety of systems.

Abstract: Can intelligence be measured? We propose that intelligence can be defined as the lawful amplification of rare but valid futures: a system increases the probability of outcomes that would be unlikely under passive dynamics but remain admissible under the constraints of the domain. We start with the premise that an intelligent system must model the world and its own place within it. Because the system is part of the world it models, this leads naturally to recursive self-simulation: the system represents futures in which its own actions are part of the trajectory. Our central results give a necessity statement and a conditional near-sufficiency statement connecting this architecture to a precise thermodynamic measure of lawful amplification of rare-valid futures: high rare-valid lift is impossible unless the internal simulation identifies rare-valid futures with high fidelity; conversely, when rare-valid fidelity is high and the simulation contains an effective policy, the achievable lift approaches the actuation-limited optimum. Thus recursive self-simulation is not merely a plausible feature of intelligence but, under the stated assumptions, is necessary and nearly sufficient for high thermodynamic intelligence. The resulting framework makes intelligence measurable on a universal scale, from passive matter and feedback controllers, large language models, and humans as text generators to Maxwell-demon-like information engines.

</details>


### [113] [A Multi-Agent system for Multi-Objective constrained optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20236)
*Federica Filippini*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MAMO, a multi-agent reinforcement learning framework that automatically learns the weights for combining cost and constraint violations, improving constrained optimization in dynamic environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Policy performance and safety heavily depend on these weights, and in non-stationary environments the right trade-off between primary objectives and constraints can change over time. There is a need for an approach that can adaptively and autonomously balance multiple objectives without manual tuning.

Method: The authors propose MAMO (Multi-Agent system for Multi-Objective constrained optimization), which treats the selection of reward weights as its own learning problem. They separate (decouple) task execution from objective design: one part of the system focuses on learning a good policy for the task given a reward structure, while another agent (or agents) learns how to set or adapt the weights that balance cost minimization and constraint violations, using multi-agent reinforcement learning techniques.

Result: Within the abstract, they mainly claim a conceptual result: MAMO can autonomously discover appropriate reward weightings, allowing better trade-offs between optimizing the main cost and respecting constraints, especially in dynamic or non-stationary environments. Specific empirical performance gains are not detailed in the abstract but are implied.

Conclusion: MAMO provides a first step toward more autonomous, adaptive, and robust RL-based solutions for constrained optimization in dynamic settings by learning how to balance multiple objectives through a multi-agent formulation, instead of relying on fixed, manually tuned penalty weights.

Abstract: Many decision-making problems in computing and networking systems can be naturally formulated as cost-minimization problems under performance constraints. In dynamic environments, reinforcement learning (RL) is often used to solve such problems at runtime by embedding both costs and constraint violations into a single scalar reward through weighted penalty terms, following a Lagrangian-inspired formulation. However, in this context the behavior of the learned policy critically depends on the choice of these weights, which are typically selected manually. This makes it difficult to identify an appropriate trade-off between optimizing the primary objective and effectively avoiding constraint violations, particularly in non-stationary environments where their relative importance may change. This paper presents MAMO (Multi-Agent system for Multi-Objective constrained optimization), an approach to tackle this balancing problem through multi-agent RL. MAMO decouples task execution from objective design by formulating the selection of reward weights as a learning problem, providing a !rst step towards more autonomous and robust RL-based solutions for constrained optimization problems in dynamic environments.

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MACR, a multi-agent framework to resolve knowledge conflicts for LLMs by assessing model confidence, selectively using internal and external knowledge, and coordinating specialized agents to detect and resolve inconsistencies, yielding better and more interpretable results than prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs rely on both internal parametric knowledge and external context from prompts, but these two sources—and even different external sources—can conflict. Existing methods usually assume one source (model or context) is correct and simply prioritize it, ignoring that both can be partially wrong and that explicit conflict resolution is needed. This gap motivates a framework that can assess reliability and actively reconcile inconsistent knowledge.

Method: The authors propose MACR, which has two main components. First, an adaptive knowledge assessment and retrieval module estimates the LLM’s confidence in its own answer using a modified semantic entropy measure. If confidence is high, the model’s internal knowledge is externalized as text; if low, relevant external knowledge is retrieved, producing base contexts. Second, an inductive multi-agent reasoning framework with three specialized agents operates over these contexts: one induces explicit rules from them, another analyzes and surfaces conflicts among all contexts, and a third resolves the inconsistencies to produce a final, coherent answer along with an explanation of the resolution.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks, MACR outperforms state-of-the-art baselines on tasks involving conflicting or unreliable knowledge sources. It not only improves answer accuracy but also generates interpretable conflict analyses that show how contradictions were detected and resolved, demonstrating the benefits of explicit multi-agent conflict resolution over simple source prioritization.

Conclusion: MACR provides an effective and interpretable way to handle knowledge conflicts in LLM-based systems by dynamically balancing internal and external information and using a multi-agent reasoning process to detect and reconcile inconsistencies. This moves beyond binary trust in either the model or the context and suggests that structured conflict resolution can substantially improve robustness in knowledge-intensive LLM applications.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have achieved strong performance across a wide range of language-based tasks by leveraging both extensive parametric knowledge and in-context learning ability, enabling them to incorporate external information provided in the input prompt. However, the integration of external knowledge can introduce conflicts, not only between the model's internal parametric knowledge and the external information, but also among multiple pieces of external contexts. Existing approaches typically assume that either the model or the provided context is reliable, overlooking the possibility that both sources may contain errors, and avoid conflicts by privileging one source over the other, rather than actively resolving inconsistencies. To address these limitations, we propose a novel framework MACR for LLM knowledge conflict resolution that moves beyond the conventional binary choice paradigm and incorporates an explicit conflict-resolution mechanism based on a multi-agent reasoning approach. Specifically, we first propose an adaptive knowledge assessment and retrieval approach that employs a modified semantic entropy measure to quantify an LLM's confidence in its answer to a given query. Based on this confidence estimation, MACR either externalizes the model's internal knowledge as textual representations or retrieves relevant external knowledge when internal knowledge is insufficient, generating basic contexts for subsequent reasoning. Then we introduce an inductive multi-agent reasoning framework with three specialized agents that, respectively, induce explicit rules, analyze potential conflicts, and resolve inconsistencies across all available contexts. Empirical results demonstrate that MACR significantly outperforms state-of-the-art baselines across benchmarks, while also providing interpretable resolutions of explicit conflicts.

### [116] [Lagrange: An Open-Vocabulary, Energy-Based Sparse Framework for Generalized End-to-End Driving](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20274)
*Shihao Ji,HongXi Li,Zihui Song,Mingyu Li*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing end-to-end autonomous driving methods either use dense geometric models that are computationally heavy and weak in high-level semantics, or sparse query-based planners that are efficient but restricted to closed-set objects and thus fragile to out-of-distribution events. Vision-Language-Action models provide open-vocabulary reasoning but their discrete, autoregressive token generation is incompatible with the continuous, high-frequency control required for driving. There is therefore a need for a framework that combines open-world semantic understanding with efficient, continuous, kinematically valid planning.

Method: The authors propose Lagrange, a driving framework based on Masked Latent Fields (MLF). Vision-Language Models are used to generate class-agnostic object proposals and encode them into continuous semantic visual tokens. An intent-driven masked cross-attention module selects and temporally filters relevant entities, then decodes the attended tokens into an implicit continuous energy field over spatial coordinates. Decision-making is formulated as a Lagrangian action minimization problem on this energy field, which yields trajectories that respect vehicle kinematics and enable collision avoidance.

Result: Through extensive offline evaluation on the nuScenes benchmark (standard) and CODA benchmark (long-tail, with rare and anomalous events), Lagrange demonstrates robust performance, showing improved capacity to handle open-world and out-of-distribution scenarios while remaining computationally efficient and producing kinematically feasible trajectories. The framework also provides interpretable intermediate representations through its energy fields and semantic tokens.

Conclusion: Lagrange offers a promising approach for open-world autonomous driving by unifying open-vocabulary semantic reasoning with continuous, kinematically constrained trajectory optimization. By leveraging masked latent fields and VLM-based semantic tokens instead of dense volumetric maps or closed-set queries, it achieves a better trade-off between computational efficiency, robustness to anomalous events, and interpretability, pointing toward more scalable and reliable autonomy in complex real-world environments.

Abstract: Scaling end-to-end autonomous driving to complex, open-world environments requires perceptual models that generalize to anomalous scenarios and planners that produce kinematically valid trajectories. Existing paradigms face a distinct dichotomy between representational efficiency and generalization capacity. Dense models (e.g., occupancy networks), while geometrically robust, incur critical computational bottlenecks and struggle with high-level semantic reasoning. Conversely, sparse, query-based planners are efficient but reliant on closed-set definitions, rendering them vulnerable to out-of-distribution (OOD) events. Although recent Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models offer open-vocabulary reasoning, their autoregressive, discrete token generation fundamentally conflicts with the continuous, high-frequency control requirements of vehicle dynamics. To address this, we propose Lagrange, an open-vocabulary, computationally sparse driving framework based on Masked Latent Fields (MLF). Rather than relying on dense volumetric reconstructions or closed-set query mechanisms, Lagrange exploits Vision-Language Models (VLMs) to encode class-agnostic object proposals into continuous semantic visual tokens. We introduce an intent-driven masked cross-attention module that temporally filters irrelevant entities, decoding the attended tokens into an implicit continuous energy field defined over spatial coordinates. By framing decision-making as a Lagrangian action minimization problem spanning this energy field, we enforce strict compliance with vehicle kinematics while executing collision avoidance. Extensive offline evaluations on both standard (nuScenes) and long-tail (CODA) benchmarks demonstrate that Lagrange establishes a promising framework for robust, interpretable, and kinematically feasible open-world autonomy.

### [118] [SoftSkill: Behavioral Compression for Contextual Adaptation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20333)
*Xijia Tao,Yihua Teng,Xinyu Fu,Ziru Liu,Kecheng Chen,Yuzhi Zhao,Suiyun Zhang,Rui Liu,Lingpeng Kong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SoftSkill, a method that converts natural-language agent skills into compact continuous “soft skill” prefixes for frozen language models, improving task performance while replacing long Markdown prompts with a few virtual tokens.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current agent skills are usually encoded as long, natural-language Markdown instructions that must be reinterpreted by a frozen language model at inference time for every task instance. This is inefficient and indirect: the model repeatedly re-reads and translates the same textual policy and procedure into behavior. The authors want to know whether such skills can instead be represented as compact continuous objects that directly condition the model’s behavior, thereby reducing prompt length, improving efficiency, and possibly yielding better task performance without finetuning the base model.

Method: The authors propose SoftSkill, a frozen-backbone approach where a natural-language skill description initializes a continuous context object (a short prefix of virtual tokens). This prefix is then refined via training a soft delta using next-token prediction, while keeping the base language model frozen. At inference time, the trained SoftSkill prefix is prepended as a latent behavioral prior instead of a long Markdown skill prompt. They evaluate this by attaching a length-32 SoftSkill prefix to Qwen3.5-4B across several tasks, and compare against both no-skill prompting and SkillOpt, a previous method that relies on long Markdown skills.

Result: Using a length-32 SoftSkill prefix with Qwen3.5-4B yields substantial performance gains: +8.3 points over no-skill prompting on SearchQA, +42.1 points on LiveMath, and +1.3 points on DocVQA. Compared to SkillOpt, SoftSkill further improves accuracy by 5.2 points on SearchQA and 12.5 points on LiveMath, while replacing hundreds to thousands of textual skill tokens with only a few virtual tokens. In more complex agentic, multi-step execution settings, SoftSkill benefits from sparse trajectory imitation but does not yet reliably compress long-horizon procedural behavior.

Conclusion: SoftSkill demonstrates that natural-language agent skills can be effectively transformed into compact continuous prefixes that act as latent behavioral controls for frozen language models. This reduces prompt length dramatically and can outperform both no-skill prompting and existing skill-optimization methods on several tasks. However, compressing complex, long-horizon agentic behaviors remains challenging. Overall, the work argues that some task skills are better encoded as learned latent controls over model initialization rather than as additional Markdown instructions that must be reinterpreted at inference time.

Abstract: Agent skills are commonly deployed as natural-language Markdown files that encode answer policies, evidence-use habits, and task procedures. These files are readable and portable, but they are consumed indirectly: for each task instance, a frozen language model must translate a long textual artifact into generation-time behavior. This paper asks whether a natural-language skill can instead initialize a compact continuous context object, refined by a trainable soft delta while the base model remains frozen. We propose SoftSkill, a frozen-backbone method that tunes such soft skills with next-token prediction and deploys them as latent behavioral priors at inference time. In our main single-round setting, a length-32 SoftSkill prefix on Qwen3.5-4B improves over no-skill prompting by 8.3 points on SearchQA, 42.1 points on LiveMath, and 1.3 points on DocVQA. Relative to SkillOpt, SoftSkill improves accuracy by 5.2 points on SearchQA and 12.5 points on LiveMath, while replacing hundreds to thousands of Markdown skill tokens with a few virtual tokens. We further study agentic execution as a harder boundary case, where sparse trajectory imitation provides useful signal but does not yet robustly compress long-horizon procedural behavior. More broadly, the results suggest that some task skills are better treated not as additional Markdown to be reinterpreted at inference time, but as compact latent controls over how a frozen model enters the task.
