### [120] [Rethinking Shrinkage Bias in LLM FP4 Pretraining: Geometric Origin, Systemic Impact, and UFP4 Recipe](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20381)
*Qian Zhao,Kunlong Chen,Changxin Tian,Zhonghui Jiang,Haitao Zhang,Chaofan Yu,Peijie Jiang,Mingliang Gong,Jia Liu,Ziqi Liu,Zhiqiang Zhang,Jun Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies a fundamental bias problem in current non-uniform FP4 (E2M1) formats used for LLM pretraining, shows how it leads to training instability especially with Random Hadamard Transform, and proposes a uniform FP4 (UFP4) recipe based on E1M2/INT4 grids that yields more stable and accurate 4‑bit training and should be supported in future hardware.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: FP4 training can greatly reduce memory and computation for large language model pretraining, but existing practical implementations and hardware paths rely on the non-uniform E2M1 format, which empirically show instability and accuracy loss. The paper is motivated by the lack of a clear theoretical understanding of why E2M1-based FP4 training is unstable and how to design better 4-bit formats and recipes that both exploit tools like the Random Hadamard Transform (RHT) and are compatible with current and future accelerators.

Method: The authors analytically study the representational geometry of non-uniform E2M1 FP4 versus uniform E1M2/INT4 grids and formally characterize a phenomenon they call Shrinkage Bias, a systematic negative rounding error arising from asymmetric quantization bins. They analyze how this bias accumulates multiplicatively across network layers and how RHT amplifies it, thus connecting grid geometry with training dynamics. Building on this, they design UFP4, a uniform 4-bit training recipe that (1) uses E1M2/INT4-style uniform grids, (2) applies RHT to all three training GEMMs, and (3) limits stochastic rounding to backward activation gradients (dY). They empirically evaluate on Dense 1.5B and MoE 7.9B/124B pretraining runs, with scaling-law analysis and ablations to isolate contributions of each component.

Result: They demonstrate that E2M1-based FP4 suffers from Shrinkage Bias that causes systematic underestimation and instability, particularly when combined with RHT, explaining prior difficulties with FP4 pretraining on that format. In contrast, their UFP4 recipe with uniform grids avoids this bias and better leverages RHT, achieving consistently lower loss degradation relative to BF16 baselines across Dense 1.5B, MoE 7.9B, and MoE 124B long-run pretraining. Scaling-law fits and ablations confirm that the gains are due to the uniform grid choice and the specific rounding and RHT strategy, rather than incidental factors.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the non-uniform E2M1 FP4 format is fundamentally disadvantaged for stable and accurate 4-bit LLM pretraining because of inherent Shrinkage Bias that compounds across layers and is worsened by RHT. Uniform 4-bit grids such as E1M2 or INT4, when combined into their UFP4 recipe, avoid this issue and yield better BF16-relative performance on large-scale pretraining. Therefore, future accelerator architectures and software stacks should treat uniform 4-bit grids as first-class training primitives, rather than centering solely on E2M1-style formats.

Abstract: FP4 training promises substantial reductions in memory and computation cost for LLM pretraining, yet current FP4 hardware paths and recipes, including NVIDIA Blackwell/Rubin-class systems and AMD MI350-series GPUs, remain centered on E2M1 data elements. In this study, we identify a fundamental limitation of that choice: non-uniform formats such as E2M1 inherently suffer from Shrinkage Bias, a systematic negative rounding error caused by the geometric asymmetry of their representable bins. We show that this bias accumulates multiplicatively across layers and is amplified by the Random Hadamard Transform (RHT), providing a unified explanation for the training instability observed in existing E2M1-based FP4 recipes. In contrast, uniform grids (E1M2/INT4) bypass this grid-geometry error and better convert the improved bucket utilization from RHT into higher quantization quality. Based on this finding, we propose UFP4, a uniform 4-bit training recipe that applies RHT to all three training GEMMs while restricting stochastic rounding to dY alone. On Dense 1.5B, MoE 7.9B, and MoE 124B long-run pretraining, UFP4 consistently achieves lower BF16-relative loss degradation than strong E2M1-based baselines, supported by scaling-law analysis and ablation studies. Our results suggest that future accelerators should support E1M2/INT4-style uniform 4-bit grids as first-class training primitives alongside E2M1.

### [121] [LedgerAgent: Structured State for Policy-Adherent Tool-Calling Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20529)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Introduce LedgerAgent, which maintains a separate explicit task-state ledger containing facts, identifiers, constraints, and conditions, renders this ledger into the prompt for reasoning, and checks policy constraints before executing environment-changing tool calls, blocking violations.

Result: Across four customer-service domains and with both open- and closed-weight models, LedgerAgent improves average pass^k compared to standard prompt-based tool-calling, with especially large gains under stricter multi-trial consistency metrics.

Conclusion: Explicit state maintenance and policy checking via a separate ledger at inference time yields more reliable, policy-adherent tool use by customer-service agents than implicit prompt-based state management.

Abstract: Policy-adherent tool-calling agents in customer-service domains must maintain task states across turns while calling tools and obeying domain policies. Task states consist of relevant facts, identifiers, constraints, and conditions observed through user interaction and tool calls. In standard agents, task states are not represented separately. Observations, tool returns, and policy instructions are placed in the prompt, leaving agents to reconstruct the relevant states from the prompt each time they decide what to do next. This design makes state management implicit, creating two common failure modes. An agent may retrieve the right facts but later ground its decision in stale, missing, or incorrect information; and a syntactically valid tool call may still violate a domain policy that depends on the current task state. We introduce \textsc{LedgerAgent}, an inference-time method for tool-calling agents that maintains observed task states in a separate ledger and renders the states into the prompt. The ledger is also used to check state-dependent policy constraints before environment-changing tool calls are executed, blocking policy violations. Across four customer-service domains and a mixed panel of open- and closed-weight models, \textsc{LedgerAgent} improves average pass\textasciicircum{}k over a standard prompt-based tool-calling approach, with the largest gains under stricter multi-trial consistency metrics.

*Zahra Asghari Varzaneh,Reza Khoshkangini,Pia Saldeen,Lars Johansson,Thomas Ebner*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that engineered temporal features from IVF incubator environmental data greatly improve prediction of pregnancy rates and transfer well across clinics using a hierarchical Bayesian model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current IVF pregnancy prediction models mainly use patient-level variables and underuse detailed lab environmental data, missing potential predictive signal from incubator microenvironments.

Method: The authors engineer 55 context-aware temporal features from high-resolution incubator sensor data (e.g., thermal stability, stress duration, recovery speed), compare them to raw sensor averages using cross-validated prediction error, and then fit a hierarchical Bayesian Beta regression with partial pooling to share environmental effects across two IVF clinics.

Result: On 61 weeks of Asian clinic data, temporal features cut cross-validated prediction error to 1.27% versus 3–5% with raw averages. The hierarchical model, evaluated on held-out Northern European clinic data, reaches R² = 0.86 and yields a 64% error reduction for patients aged 35–39 compared to a naive baseline.

Conclusion: Structured, temporally-aware incubator environmental monitoring provides clinically meaningful, transferable signal for predicting IVF pregnancy outcomes, and hierarchical Bayesian modeling can effectively share this environmental information across geographically distinct clinics while preserving local baselines.

Abstract: IVF pregnancy rates are routinely modeled using patient-level variables, while high-resolution laboratory environmental data remain underutilized. We show that this is a missed opportunity. Rather than relying on raw sensor averages, we engineer 55 context-aware temporal features, including rolling thermal stability, simultaneous temperature-humidity adherence, peak stress duration, and post-stress recovery speed, that capture the dynamics of incubator microenvironments. On 61 weeks of data from an Asian IVF clinic, these features reduce cross-validated prediction error to 1.27%, compared to 3-5% for raw averages. We then train a hierarchical Bayesian Beta regression model that shares environmental effects across an Asian and a Northern European clinic via partial pooling, while preserving site-specific baselines. On held-out data from the Northern European clinic, the model achieves R2 = 0.86 and a 64% error reduction for the 35-39 age group over a naive baseline, demonstrating that structured environmental monitoring contains clinically meaningful, transferable signal.

</details>


### [124] [What Do Safety-Aligned LLMs Learn From Mixed Compliance Demonstrations?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20508)
*Sihui Dai,Mann Patel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how language models interpret and respond to mixed in-context demonstrations that include both benign and harmful compliance examples, revealing that the effects of such demonstrations depend strongly on model training, ordering of examples, and model-specific behavior around refusal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While prior work shows that in-context demonstrations can jailbreak language models, it is not well understood what, exactly, models are learning from different kinds of demonstrations (benign vs harmful, compliant vs refusing) and why certain jailbreak setups succeed or fail. Understanding these mechanisms is important for designing more robust safety training and prompt-based defenses.

Method: The authors construct prompts that mix benign compliance demonstrations (safe request with helpful answer) and harmful compliance demonstrations (unsafe request with an answer that helps) in various compositions and orders. They then test multiple language models under these different demonstration setups and evaluate how often the models comply with subsequent harmful requests. They also compare models trained with and without preference optimization to see how this training stage changes the models’ reactions to mixed demonstrations, and they inspect how refusal behavior interacts with learned in-context formatting patterns.

Result: They find that benign and harmful demonstrations affect models differently and are not simple substitutes: in some models, adding benign demonstrations can actually increase harmful compliance, whereas in others it decreases it. They show that preference optimization is the key training stage that prevents benign demonstrations from increasing harmful compliance. They also observe a strong recency bias in how models use demonstrations (later examples weigh more), and identify model-specific patterns in how refusal interacts with in-context learning—some models preserve the in-context formatting even when refusing, while others ignore all in-context signals once they decide to refuse.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that demonstration-based jailbreaking is governed by nuanced interactions between example content, ordering, and training procedures. Harmful and benign examples are not interchangeable, and preference optimization plus careful consideration of demonstration ordering are crucial for mitigating jailbreak risks. The findings provide a more detailed mechanistic picture of how models extract behavioral guidance from in-context compliance demonstrations, informing both attack design and defense strategies.

Abstract: Prior work has shown that in-context demonstrations can jailbreak language models, but it remains unclear how models interpret different types of compliance demonstrations. We study this by mixing benign compliance demonstrations (non-harmful request, helpful response) with harmful compliance demonstrations (harmful request, helpful response) and testing three hypotheses about how demonstration composition drives harmful compliance. Across four models, we find that benign and harmful demonstrations are not interchangeable: benign demonstrations can either reduce or increase harmful compliance depending on the model. We further show that preference optimization is the critical training stage that prevents benign demonstrations from increasing harmful compliance, that demonstration ordering exhibits strong recency bias, and that models differ in how refusal interacts with in-context learning: some adopt demonstrated formatting even when refusing, while others override all in-context signals upon refusal. Taken together, this work moves beyond showing that demonstration-based jailbreaking works to characterizing how it works: what models extract from compliance demonstrations depends on demonstration content, ordering, and training methodology.

</details>


### [125] [Multi-LCB: Extending LiveCodeBench to Multiple Programming Languages](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20517)
*Maria Ivanova,Pavel Zadorozhny,Rodion Levichev,Ivan Petrov,Adamenko Pavel,Ivan Lopatin,Alexey Kutalev,Dmitrii Babaev*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Multi-LCB is a new benchmark extending LiveCodeBench to 12 programming languages for contamination-aware evaluation of LLM code generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LiveCodeBench (LCB) evaluates LLMs on competitive programming tasks but only in Python, which does not reflect the multi-language requirements of real-world software engineering or test cross-language generalization of LLMs.

Method: The authors construct Multi-LCB by transforming Python problems from LCB into equivalent tasks in eleven additional programming languages while preserving LCB’s contamination controls and evaluation protocol, keeping full compatibility with the original LCB format so that future LCB updates are automatically inherited. They then evaluate 24 LLMs on instruction-following and reasoning abilities across all supported languages.

Result: Experiments on 24 LLMs show evidence of Python overfitting, language-specific data contamination, and large performance gaps between Python and other languages, revealing limited multilingual code-generation robustness.

Conclusion: Multi-LCB serves as a rigorous benchmark for evaluating LLMs across multiple programming languages, directly overcoming LCB’s Python-only limitation and revealing important weaknesses in current LLMs’ cross-language code-generation capabilities.

Abstract: LiveCodeBench (LCB) has recently become a widely adopted benchmark for evaluating large language models (LLMs) on code-generation tasks. By curating competitive programming problems, constantly adding fresh problems to the set, and filtering them by release dates, LCB provides contamination-aware evaluation and offers a holistic view of coding capability. However, LCB remains restricted to Python, leaving open the question of whether LLMs can generalize across the diverse programming languages required in real-world software engineering.
  We introduce Multi-LCB, a benchmark for evaluating LLMs across twelve programming languages, including Python. Multi-LCB transforms Python tasks from the LCB dataset into equivalent tasks in other languages while preserving LCB's contamination controls and evaluation protocol. Because it is fully compatible with the original LCB format, Multi-LCB will automatically track future LCB updates, enabling systematic assessment of cross-language code generation competence and requiring models to sustain performance well beyond Python.
  We evaluated 24 LLMs for instruction and reasoning on Multi-LCB, uncovering evidence of Python overfitting, language-specific contamination, and substantial disparities in multilingual performance. Our results establish Multi-LCB as a rigorous new benchmark for multi-programming-language code evaluation, directly addressing LCB's primary limitation and exposing critical gaps in current LLM capabilities.

</details>


### [126] [FlowEdit: Associative Memory for Lifelong Pronunciation Adaptation in Flow-Matching TTS](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20518)
*Harshit Singh,Ayush Pratap Singh,Nityanand Mathur*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: FlowEdit is a framework that lets a frozen flow-matching TTS system learn and remember pronunciation fixes over time without retraining model weights.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Flow-matching TTS models have excellent zero-shot quality but are static after deployment: they mispronounce new or rare proper nouns and cannot be easily corrected without heavy retraining. There is a need for a lightweight, continual adaptation mechanism that can incorporate user feedback on pronunciations while preserving general speech quality.

Method: FlowEdit treats pronunciation corrections as edits in the latent text-embedding space rather than as updates to model parameters. Given corrective feedback, it optimizes a small token-level perturbation to the text embeddings so that the TTS output matches the desired pronunciation. These perturbations, together with their textual keys, are stored in a Modern Hopfield Network acting as a content-addressable episodic memory. At inference, for each input token, the system retrieves relevant corrections via soft attention with a similarity gate, allowing fuzzy morphological matching (e.g., variants of a name) and then applies the corresponding embedding edits to guide the frozen TTS model.

Result: On a benchmark of 312 multilingual proper nouns spanning 18 language families, FlowEdit substantially improves pronunciation accuracy: it reduces the Phoneme Error Rate on target words by 92.7% relative to the zero-shot flow-matching TTS baseline, while leaving general speech quality unchanged. The correction procedure is computationally efficient, completing in about 15 seconds per correction on a single GPU.

Conclusion: Latent conditioning edits combined with a Hopfield-network episodic memory enable effective, life-long adaptation of frozen flow-matching TTS models. FlowEdit can rapidly learn, store, and generalize pronunciation fixes for proper nouns without retraining, sustaining high-quality speech while overcoming the static nature of conventional TTS deployments.

Abstract: Flow-matching text-to-speech systems achieve remarkable zero-shot quality but remain static after deployment: pronunciation errors on out-of-vocabulary proper nouns persist unless the model is retrained. We introduce FlowEdit, a life-long adaptation framework for frozen flow-matching TTS that learns pronunciation corrections as latent conditioning edits rather than weight updates. When corrective feedback is provided, FlowEdit optimizes a token-level perturbation in the text embedding space, then stores the correction in a Modern Hopfield Network serving as content-addressable episodic memory. At inference, corrections are retrieved via soft attention with a similarity gate, enabling fuzzy morphological matching. On our curated benchmark of 312 multilingual proper nouns across 18 language families, FlowEdit reduces target-word Phoneme Error Rate by 92.7% relative to the zero-shot baseline while maintaining identical general-speech quality. Corrections complete in approximately 15 seconds on a single GPU.

</details>


### [127] [DeepSWIP: Quotient-WMC Counterfactuals for Neural Probabilistic Logic Programs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20526)
*Saimun Habib,Vaishak Belle,Fengxiang He*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces DeepSWIP, a counterfactual reasoning framework for neurosymbolic systems like DeepProbLog, enabling single-world counterfactual inference via a program transformation and weighted model counting, with theoretical guarantees and empirical validation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing neurosymbolic systems such as DeepProbLog integrate neural perception with probabilistic logic but only support associational reasoning. Counterfactual reasoning, which is crucial for causal analysis and decision making, requires a suitable causal semantics for interventions and evidence that these systems lack. The authors aim to provide a principled, computationally efficient way to perform counterfactual inference within DeepProbLog-like frameworks.

Method: The authors propose DeepSWIP, which gives DeepProbLog programs a single-world counterfactual semantics based on Single World Intervention Programs (SWIPs). They use neural materialization to convert fixed-context neural predicates into standard ProbLog probabilistic choices, then apply SWIP transformations and compute counterfactuals via weighted model counting over a single transformed program. They analyze the semantics under assumptions of finite grounding and unique-supported models, and use the quotient-WMC view of ProbLog conditionals to characterize how neural probabilities behave under interventions and conditioning. They empirically test the approach with MPI3D and a SUMO HOV simulation, comparing against a DeepTwin construction and evaluating statistical estimators.

Result: Theoretically, DeepSWIP is shown to be exact with respect to the learned, materialized functional causal model under finite grounding and unique-supported-model assumptions. The quotient-WMC formulation clarifies which neural probabilities are active and explains phenomena such as intervention cleaning, calibration sensitivity, and instability with rare evidence. Empirically, DeepSWIP matches predictions of a DeepTwin-based implementation across 12,000 counterfactual queries on the MPI3D dataset and achieves a 2.14× speedup by avoiding duplication of endogenous variables. In the SUMO HOV study, naive plug-in estimators suffer bias due to neural calibration degradation, while a properly designed randomized-policy AIPW estimator substantially reduces first-order bias for estimating population means and average treatment effects.

Conclusion: DeepSWIP extends DeepProbLog with a sound and efficient single-world semantics for counterfactual reasoning, grounded in causal intervention theory and implemented through a neural-materialization and SWIP-based transformation amenable to weighted model counting. This allows neurosymbolic systems to answer counterfactual queries in a principled way, clarifies the behavior of neural probabilities under interventions and evidence, and yields practical gains in both accuracy and efficiency, as demonstrated on synthetic and simulated tasks. The work highlights the importance of calibration and appropriate estimators when using neural components for causal effect estimation.

Abstract: Neurosymbolic systems such as DeepProbLog combine neural perception with probabilistic logic, but standard inference is associational. Counterfactual reasoning additionally requires a causal semantics for interventions and evidence. We introduce DeepSWIP, a single-world counterfactual semantics for DeepProbLog programs. Using neural materialization, we reduce fixed-context neural predicates to ordinary ProbLog choices, apply Single World Intervention Programs (SWIPs), and compute counterfactuals by weighted model counting (WMC) over a single transformed program. Under finite grounding and unique-supported-model assumptions, DeepSWIP is exact relative to the learned materialized FCM. The standard quotient-WMC form of ProbLog conditionals identifies active neural probabilities and explains intervention cleaning, calibration sensitivity, and rare-evidence instability. Experiments on MPI3D confirm the transformation against a DeepTwin construction against 12,000 queries, as predicted and a 2.14$\times$ inference speedup from avoiding the Twin's endogenous duplication. A SUMO HOV experiment shows that neural calibration degradation biases plug-in estimates, while a correctly scoped randomized-policy AIPW estimator removes most first-order bias for population mean and ATE estimands. Code is at https://github.com/saibib/deep_SWIP.

</details>


### [128] [How Do Instructions Shape Speech? Cross-Attention Attribution for Style-Captioned Text-to-Speech](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20532)
*Nityanand Mathur,Hamees Sayed,Wasim Madha,Apoorv Singh,Sameer Khurana,Akshat Mandloi,Sudarshan Kamath*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a cross-attention attribution method for speech diffusion TTS to show how individual style-caption words affect generated speech, offering the first systematic analysis of natural-language control in such models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although style-captioned TTS systems use natural-language prompts to control speaking style, it is unclear how each word in the caption actually affects acoustic properties like prosody and energy. This limits our ability to diagnose failures and improve fine-grained controllability and interpretability in expressive TTS systems. The authors are motivated to open this black box by quantifying how every caption token shapes the generation process over time and across layers in a modern diffusion-based speech model.

Method: They adapt the DAAM (Diffusion Attentive Attribution Maps) framework from images to speech diffusion models, specifically CapSpeech-TTS. Using cross-attention attribution, they compute per-token attention heatmaps over 25 transformer layers and 24 ODE steps. They construct a dataset of 3,600 samples from 120 style captions, each paired with 30 text transcripts, and analyze how caption tokens’ attention patterns relate to waveform characteristics such as F0 and energy and how these patterns evolve through the diffusion steps and network depth.

Result: Their analysis of the attention maps reveals: (1) style-related tokens have lower temporal variance in their attention, indicating they act as global conditioning signals, unlike content/function tokens; (2) the attention given to style tokens correlates with fundamental frequency (F0) and energy, tying linguistic style descriptors to prosodic realizations; (3) style conditioning is strongest in early diffusion steps and in deeper network layers; and (4) attention entropy is minimized around layer 17, coinciding with the peak importance of style tokens, suggesting this layer is where the network is most selective and style-critical processing is concentrated.

Conclusion: The study provides the first systematic attribution-based view of how natural-language style captions influence cross-attention—and thus acoustic realization—in speech diffusion TTS models. The findings show that style tokens function as global prosodic controllers whose influence is temporally and structurally localized within the model, offering insights that can guide better diagnostics, architectures, and control strategies for expressive TTS.

Abstract: Style-captioned text-to-speech systems use natural language to control voice characteristics, but how individual words influence acoustic output remains unclear. Understanding this is critical for diagnosing failure modes and improving controllability in expressive TTS. We propose cross-attention attribution for speech diffusion models, adapting the DAAM framework to the speech domain for the first time, and apply it to CapSpeech-TTS. Our method extracts per-token heatmaps across 25 layers and 24 ODE steps. We analyze 3,600 (style caption, text transcript) combinations comprising 120 style captions conditioning the generation of 30 text transcripts each, revealing how caption tokens shape waveforms. Results show: (1) style tokens have lower temporal variance than content/function tokens, confirming global conditioning; (2) style attention correlates with F0 and energy; (3) style conditioning peaks in early steps and deep layers; (4) attention entropy reaches its minimum at layer 17, co-occurring with the style importance peak, indicating maximal network selectivity at the most style-critical stage. This is the first study of how natural language influences cross-attention in speech diffusion models

</details>


### [129] [Toward Calibrated Mixture-of-Experts Under Distribution Shift](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.20544)
*Gina Wong,Drew Prinster,Suchi Saria,Rama Chellappa,Anqi Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes calibration in mixture-of-experts (MoE) models under distribution shift and proposes an adversarial reweighting scheme to improve the accuracy–calibration tradeoff, especially in challenging shifted settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While calibration of individual predictors is known to improve ensemble models, the precise conditions under which expert-level calibration benefits mixture-of-experts (MoE) models—especially under distribution shift—are poorly understood. In particular, it is unclear how routing mechanisms in MoE interact with calibrated experts to determine the overall model’s calibration, which is crucial for trustworthy probabilistic predictions.

Method: The authors theoretically and empirically study MoE models with different routing mechanisms (hard vs. soft routing) under a broad class of distribution shifts. They analyze when expert-level calibration implies calibration of the aggregate MoE. They then introduce an adversarial reweighting procedure that penalizes calibration errors of the routed aggregate under possible shifts, effectively reweighting data or predictions to stress-test worst-case calibration and adjust training accordingly.

Result: They show that for hard-routed MoE models, if each expert is calibrated, then the overall MoE remains calibrated under a wide range of distribution shifts. In contrast, for soft-routed MoEs, expert calibration alone does not guarantee calibrated aggregate predictions. Their adversarial reweighting method improves the tradeoff between accuracy and calibration—both on average and on particularly difficult data subsets—across various model classes, prediction tasks, and forms of distribution shift.

Conclusion: Expert-level calibration has different implications for MoE calibration depending on the routing scheme: it is sufficient under many shifts for hard routing but not for soft routing. By explicitly penalizing worst-case routed calibration errors via adversarial reweighting, one can achieve better-calibrated and accurate MoE models, especially in the presence of distribution shift and on challenging subsets of the data.

Abstract: Calibration aligns a model's predictive uncertainty with the frequencies of its empirical outcomes and is important for understanding and trusting reported probabilities. Recent work shows that enforcing calibration at the level of individual predictors can improve ensemble accuracy and calibration, with mixture-of-experts (MoE) models showing strong empirical improvements in particular; however, the conditions under which calibration helps MoE are not well understood. In this work, we study how MoE models behave under distribution shift, focusing on how routing mechanisms interact with expert-level calibration. We show that expert calibration is sufficient to ensure calibration of the overall model under a broad class of distribution shifts in hard-routed models, but is insufficient for calibrating soft-routed models. To address this, we propose an adversarial reweighting that penalizes calibration errors of the routed aggregate under distribution shift, and we demonstrate that it improves the accuracy-calibration tradeoff both on average and on difficult subsets of the data, across model classes, prediction tasks, and distribution shifts.

</details>
